I composed this book for you, my sons, after completing it for you as a portion of the science of medicine in its entirety and perfection. Having reached the goal of clarity and explanation therein, I deemed it fitting to complete it for you with this section on manual work.
Indeed, the practice of manual work is esteemed in our land and time, yet it is entirely absent such that its science has nearly been forgotten and its traces have disappeared. Only a few sketches of it remain in the early books, having been obscured by careless hands and confused lines until their meanings have become closed off and their usefulness has diminished.
Thus, I saw fit to revive it and compose this section on it in a manner that is clear, detailed, and concise, and to include illustrations of various tools used therein, as this would serve to further elucidate the text and provide what is necessary.
The reason why there are no skilled craftsmen in our time is that the field of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be content with the science of anatomy described by Galen. Such that he may understand the utilities of the members, their forms and natures, their connections and disconnections, and be knowledgeable about bones, nerves, muscles, their numbers, outlets, arteries, veins, and the locations of their openings.
Therefore, as Al-Qurait said, 'Many are physicians in name but few in deed,' especially those skilled in manual work. We have mentioned this briefly at the beginning of this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have mentioned regarding anatomy will surely fall into errors that may kill people, as I have often witnessed many who claim to practice this art without proper knowledge or skill.
I once saw an ignorant physician who incised a swelling on the neck of a woman, thinking it was a goitre. He severed some of the vessels in her neck, causing her to bleed profusely until she died in his hands.
I saw another physician who attempted to remove a large stone from the bladder of a man who had been suffering from hematuria. He panicked and removed it along with a piece of the bladder. The man died about three days later, and when I was called to remove the stone, I saw how large it was and the state of his health, which I had not previously appreciated.
I saw another doctor who made a living with some leaders of our region in medicine.
The doctor, hastily and without knowledge, bound the fracture near the heel of a black servant with dressings and splints, binding them tightly together without allowing the wound to breathe.
He then abandoned him to his desires for several days and ordered that the bandage not be removed until his leg and foot swelled up and nearly killed him.
So I was called to him, and I quickly removed the bandage. He felt relief from his pain, but the damage had already spread throughout the limb and could not be reversed.
And the corruption continued in the limb until he died.
I saw another doctor who applied hot fomentations to a patient with cancer. He excised it after several days until the patient's condition worsened, for if a cancer is purely from black bile, it should never be cauterized with iron.
Unless it is in an organ that can tolerate its total removal.
So know this, my son: that manual work is divided into two kinds - work that is accompanied by safety, and work that brings damage in most cases.
And I have warned throughout this book about the work involving danger and fear; you should avoid it and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find an opportunity for argumentation and criticism. Therefore, be cautious and careful with your patients, treating them gently and confirming their ailments.
And follow the best path leading to safety and favorable outcome. Avoid difficult and dangerous diseases as much as possible.
And keep yourselves away from what you fear may bring doubt to your religion and worldly affairs. It is better for your honor and will raise you in this world and the Hereafter.
Galienus said in some of his advice, 'Do not treat bad diseases so that you do not become known as bad doctors.'
This section is divided into three chapters.
Chapter One.
Chapter One: Cauterization with Fire and Strong Drugs - Arranged from Head to Foot, with Illustrations of Instruments, Methods of Cauterization, and Everything Necessary for Manual Work.
The Second Gate
In the chapter on incisions, cupping, venesection, bloodletting, surgeries, removing arrows and other such topics, all arranged in an orderly manner with illustrations of the instruments.
The Third Gate
In the chapter on dislocations and fractures, treatment of wounds and similar topics, all arranged from head to foot with illustrations of the instruments.
The First Gate
On Phthisis (Tuberculosis)
Before mentioning its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms and in what temperament it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and harms of cupping is a lengthy topic, a subtle science, and a secret art about which many wise men have spoken.
And they differed on it, but I have briefly summarized some of their words to avoid lengthiness.
I say that cupping is generally beneficial for all humors, whether with matter or without it, except for two temperaments: the hot humor without matter and the dry humor without matter.
As for the dry humor with matter, there is disagreement about it. Some said that cupping is beneficial in it, while others said the opposite, claiming that cupping does not benefit a disease that results from heat and dryness because fire has the qualities of heat and dryness.
It is impossible for a hot, dry disease to be treated with a hot, dry remedy. But the one who disagrees says that cupping with fire can benefit a hot, dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. For whenever you add the body and its moisture to the nature of fire, you make the body cold.
And I say with him because experience has shown me that, except for those who have perfected and practiced cupping extensively. And stood on the varieties of temperaments of people, the conditions of diseases in themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for other temperaments, there is no fear of them, especially cold, moist diseases, as all physicians agree on the benefit of cupping in them.
Know, O sons, that the secret of treating with cupping by fire and its superiority over cupping with a burned drug is that fire is a simple substance whose effect does not exceed the member it burns, nor does it harm another connected member except for slight harm.
The application of cauterization with burning drugs may extend its effect beyond the intended area, and perhaps cause an incurable disease in the member, or even kill. Fire, due to its nobleness and precious nature, does not do this except when it is excessive.
And we have come to understand this through experience, from the length of our service, care in our practice, and discerning the truth of the matter.
Therefore, I have avoided lengthy speech; were it not for the inappropriateness of such a secret and mysterious recipe for cauterization and its effects on bodies, along with philosophical proofs that refute doubts, to this book.
Know, O my sons, that they have differed in the appropriate time for cauterization, and most favored it at the time of spring.
I say that cauterization is suitable in all times because the harm caused by time is outweighed by the benefit gained from cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for severe, urgent pain that cannot be delayed, as there may follow something worse than slight harm caused by time.
O my sons, do not think like the common people and the foolish physicians that cauterization which cures a disease will never return.
This is not because you may think that cauterization is merely like a medicine that alters the temperament and dries up the humidity causing pain.
Rather, cauterization surpasses medication in speed of action, strength of effect, and power of control.
Disease may sometimes return, according to the temperament of the patient, their ability to recover, the strength of the disease, and other factors such as diet and habits.
God forbid that cauterization be used for a light disease in an organ with little moisture or humor. Such as tooth extraction for pain, etc., in such cases the pain may not return, and this is the least likely outcome.
As for the common people's claim that cauterization is the last resort of medicine, this is correct only to the extent that they believe nothing can be done after cauterization.
Indeed, the meaning of 'al-Kiyâ is the last resort in treatment' is that whenever we employ various remedies in a disease without success, then we apply al-Kiyâ and it works. Henceforth, al-Kiyâ is considered the last resort in treatment. However, this is not the meaning that has been generally understood or grasped by many ignorant physicians.
It was mentioned earlier that gold al-Kiyâ is better than iron al-Kiyâ. They said this because of the mildness of gold and its noble substance, claiming that it does not cause inflammation at the site of application, which is not true.
For I have tested this and found that it only works in some bodies, not others. Gold al-Kiyâ is indeed better than iron al-Kiyâ as they said, except that when you heat the iron with gold in fire, you cannot tell when it reaches the desired temperature for the redness of gold.
Because cold approaches it quickly, and even if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and causes confusion for the smith. Therefore, iron al-Kiyâ is quicker and more accurate for us to use, Allah willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cautery into sections, from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what they need, Allah willing.
Chapter One.
There is one type of cautery for the head.
This cautery is beneficial for excessive dampness and coldness in the brain, which are causes of headache, frequent colds around the eyes and ears, heavy sleep, toothache, and sore throat. In short, this cautery is useful for all diseases caused by coldness, such as epilepsy, insanity, palsy, and similar illnesses.
The image of this cautery is that you should first command the patient to evacuate with a laxative purgative for three or four nights, depending on their strength, age, and habit.
Then, you should command him to shave his head with a razor and make him sit before you, kneeling, with his hands placed on his chest.
Then, place the base of your little finger on the base of his nose between his eyes. Where your finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the brass cautery until it becomes red hot.
Reveal as much bone as a grain of barley or a lentil, then raise your hand and cover it with the same cautery or another if it has cooled. Until you see that much bone which I mentioned to you, then take some salt, dissolve it in water and dip a wick into it. Place this on the wound and leave it for three days.
Then place a wick dipped in oil on the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, if God wills.
It is said that the more a wound remains open and secretes pus, the better it is.
Some people mentioned that you should cauterize the skin to the bone and keep the cautery in place until some of the thickness of the bone is burnt, then remove it afterwards and treat it.
Others said that one should exaggerate cauterization until it strongly affects the bone, like a large ring or seal, and they claimed that this draws out the head's vapor from that spot and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals. I do not see these two types of cauterization except in some people, and I consider leaving it as better. Peace be with you, if there is healing, the head will harden when its natural connection is severed, as we have seen in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is inherently weak, the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion. Therefore, act accordingly, if God wills.
Chapter Two.
On Cauterizing the Head.
If the head has chronic pain that persists in the patient despite using medications, ointments, poultices and bandages, especially if they have been cauterized with a single cautery as described without any benefit, then examine. If the patient's head has strong structure inherently and is not weak, but feels severe coldness, cauterize again slightly above the previous one. Then, scrape on every horn of his head until the thickness of the skin departs and its bone becomes exposed, which we have described. And scrape gently at the back of his head in the place known as 'the nape,' and be gentle here lest you expose the bone, for this area causes severe pain unlike any other scraping on the head, and I will mention this scrape in its proper place.
It is preferable that the curette used for scraping the horns of the head and the nape be gentler than that used for the middle of the head, and this is its shape.
Chapter Three.
On the treatment of non-chronic migraines.
If pain occurs in a head wound accompanied by headache and extends to the eye, evacuate the patient with diuretics for the head and use other treatments mentioned in the sections on diseases. If this does not succeed, there are two scraping methods for it. Either scraping with a cauterizing medicine or with iron.
As for scraping with the cauterizing medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, cut off its edges on both sides, and then make an incision with a wide scalpel in the painful spot at the temple until there is enough space under the skin to fit the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it disappears, then tie it with a tight bandage and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then untie and remove the garlic, leaving the wound for two or three days before dressing it.
Wrap it with cotton soaked in oil until the wound oozes. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills. If you wish, you may do this with some cauterizing drugs mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on cauterizing drugs.
And as for scraping with iron, it is done as follows: Heat the curette of this shape:
It is called the 'screw-shaped' curette because its tip resembles a screw with some convexity, and it has a small groove in its middle.
Then place it on the painful spot, hold your hand firmly, and turn the iron gently until you cauterize about half the thickness of the skin. Raise your hand to prevent burning the artery below and causing bleeding, then soak a bandage in saltwater and apply it to the site for three days. Then carry the bandage with oil, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this sister [hemorrhoid] with the sharp end of the iron and preserve it from cutting the artery in this non-chronic case particularly.
Chapter Four.
On Hemorrhoids That Do Not Heal.
If you have treated hemorrhoids with the remedies mentioned earlier, and they did not improve, and you see that the condition does not respond to the first cauterization either with medicine or fire... You should heat the iron until it turns red, then press it on the painful spot for about half a finger's length or similar, and remove your hand once to cut the artery and reach near the bone.
But beware of touching the moving joint lest it burn the muscle or its tendon, causing stiffness.
And be cautious of the bleeding from the artery you cut, as there is danger in it. Especially for one who is ignorant and has no experience, and leaves the job to others.
The treatment of sudden bleeding from an artery will be mentioned in its place in this book, God willing. If you find that the condition does not improve with this cauterization...
And if the patient's body is susceptible to fainting, treat him with cautery in the middle of his head as described, and dress the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on tying arteries with the double-edged iron; it is better and more effective than this one.
Chapter Five.
On Cauterization of Earache.
If ear pain occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other remedies mentioned earlier without relief, heat the iron called "al-Nokta" which has this shape...
Then heat it and then prick it around the entire ear, as it rotates, or around both of them if there is pain in both. And keep away from the root of the ear slightly after marking the spot with ink, and the cautery should be about ten points for each ear or thereabouts, then treat the spots until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six
On Cauterization.
The phlegm that is treated by cautery is of the type that occurs as mentioned in the sections on diseases.
And avoid cauterizing the type that occurs from bone and nerve spasm.
If you have treated this type of phlegm with irrigations, syrups, and ointments without success, it is not permissible to cauterize the patient three times. Once at the root of the ear, second below its tip slightly, and third where the lips meet.
Make your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area because relaxation only occurs on the healthy side.
And the shape of the cautery should be like this: one at the tip of the upper ear under the head's horn slightly, and another in the cheek, and its length should be about the length of a thumb.
Bring down your cautery hand until it burns through half the thickness of the skin. This is the shape of the cautery, which is a type of skillet that has been previously shown, though softer than that one, as you can see. The blade should have a little more thickness, then treat the spot with what has been mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven
On Cauterization for Chronic Paralysis.
If paralysis has persisted and you have treated it with what was mentioned without success, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize four times. Once on each horn of the head, once in the middle of the head as mentioned before, and once at the back of the head as described.
Recipe for cautery as mentioned earlier; additionally, you can also cauterize once on the stomach's mouth to make it better, then treat with what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eight
In Case of Forgetfulness
For phlegm that causes forgetfulness, it is advisable initially to administer large quantities of Iris and powdered Mastic for the brain.
Then shave his entire head and apply a plaster of mustard seed paste on its back, carrying it for several times until it falls off. This is also one of the treatments for forgetfulness.
Do this on the specified vein mentioned earlier; if it does not work, then apply three ligatures to the back of his head, starting from the top and going down to the nape of the neck.
Leave a space of about half an inch between each ligature; then treat the forgetfulness as mentioned earlier. If you wish to increase the number of ligatures and the patient can tolerate it, then apply the middle ligature again.
If you wish to increase the treatment further, apply additional ligatures on his ears; then treat him until he recovers. The cautery should be made of bronze and have the shape mentioned earlier.
Chapter Nine
On Paralysis and General Flaccidity
It is advisable to begin by purging the head with Iris and other mentioned remedies; then shave the patient's head, and apply ligatures: one at the center of the head, one on each ear, and one on the back of his neck.
And three more ligatures on the cervical vertebrae; if you need to apply more in cases of general flaccidity and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and persistent, then apply four additional ligatures on his back. Inform the patient about the cautery until it burns most of the skin and causes blistering; then remove your hand and treat him as mentioned earlier until he recovers, if Allah wills. The cautery should be made of bronze.
Chapter Ten
On Epilepsy
Only cauterize an epileptic whose seizures are caused by phlegm; it is advisable initially to purge his brain with large quantities of Iris and other remedies mentioned in the previous sections, provided that the patient is capable of taking medications.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medications, then use only the remedies of Garрагir and Mamlacith for the brain for many days before cauterization, along with improving his diet.
Then he shaves his head and applies the first cautery iron in the middle of the head as previously described, and another in the back. And on each horn of his head, an iron is applied; if the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, then cauterize with the irons mentioned in the chapter on palsy and relax the body over the cervical and lumbar vertebrae, using a golden iron as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the iron gentle like this:
Chapter Eleven.
In cautery for melancholia.
If the cause of melancholy is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, cauterize with the irons mentioned in the chapter on palsy.
If the cause of melancholy is an inclination towards black bile and the patient's body is moist and corrupt, then purify his brain as mentioned in the section on classification.
Then shave the patient's head and make a tight cap of linen like a crown. Then lower it onto his head while he is sitting cross-legged, holding it from all sides. Then take one spoonful of old sheep fat oil.
Then heat it gently on the fire until it is warm enough to tolerate being placed on a finger. Then pour it onto the patient's head within the crown and leave it until it cools. Do this once every week along with other good treatments until he recovers, if God wills.
If you wish, cauterize with many small punctures without holding the iron in your hand but rather applying it gently; this method moistens the brain moderately.
Then place a linen cloth soaked in sheep fat oil or chicken fat on his head.
Chapter Twelve
In cautery for water descending into the eye
If you find at the beginning that water is descending into the eye with the signs mentioned in the section on classification, then hasten to purify his head and warm him from all dampness. Make him sweat in the bathhouse for several days on an empty stomach.
Then pass a razor over his head and cauterize with one iron in the middle of his head. Then cauterize with two irons on the temples if the water is descending into both eyes at the beginning, or from one side only if it is in one eye only.
Then cut with the scalpel all veins and arteries underneath the skin; let the incisions be long and wide at the temples, and take care not to cause bleeding.
If you find any of it, cut it off at its source with whatever treatment is possible; we will come to the wisdom in tying off vessels and cutting them without causing bleeding.
Two deep incisions may be made beneath the bones of the occiput.
Chapter Thirteen.
On chronic weeping wounds.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and previously there were veins or arteries on the outside of the head that have since become hardened and obstructed by thick mucus, then cut the incision I described at the beginning of this section with the eye. Make an incision in the middle of the forehead, two on each temple, and two beneath the occipital bones. If more is needed, make additional incisions on both sides of the eye at the outer edge of the eyebrow with a small scalpel.
Chapter Fourteen.
On incision for blocked nostrils.
If the treatment mentioned in this section does not prove effective, then proceed as follows: apply cupping to the temples three times; then shave his head and make a midline incision on the forehead using an oily scalpel. Then make two incisions above the eyebrows with the iron scalpel, just beneath the hair; be careful not to cut the artery. This is the shape of the incisions:
Chapter Fifteen
On incision for drooping eyelid.
If the eyelid droops due to disease or moisture, cut one incision in the eyelid using this crescent-shaped scalpel.
If you wish, make two incisions above the eyebrows on both sides, a little way from the temples; each incision should be as long as the eyebrow.
Do not cut too deeply with the scalpel; only burn away about one-third of the skin. The shape of the scalpel should be like this:
Chapter Sixteen
On incision for drooping eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and irritate the eye, there are two types of incisions: Either cauterize with fire or cauterize with a burning drug, but if cauterizing with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to leave his symptoms undisturbed if he is among those from whom it will be removed until they become prominent and equal.
If it pains at the time of its growth, then tie his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until it grows. When it has grown and become even, place the patient's head in a vice, then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an oak leaf.
Begin the mark near the symptoms, then place under the eyelid a cotton wool soaked in egg white or sparrow's saliva, and heat with a heated iron having this shape.
Then cauterize according to the shape you have marked a little at a time many times until the surface of the skin burns in the shape of an oak leaf entirely, particularly and a sign that your work is healthy is when you see the eyelid has shrunk and the hair has risen from the flesh of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave him for three days. Then apply cotton soaked in oil until the scab falls off, then treat him with ointment until it heals if Allah wills. If any hair returns after some time and the eyelid becomes relaxed.
Then cauterize again on that place as you did at first. If the hair is on the lower eyelid, cauterize until it returns to its natural place without hurting the eye's hair. And cauterizing with a burning drug is that you instruct the patient to leave the symptoms undisturbed until they become prominent and even.
Then make from paper a shape like an oak leaf. Then take from ordinary soap and unslaked lime each one a weight of a dirham or similar, crush them both together thoroughly and do this quickly so that they do not dry out. Then spread it on the paper you made in the shape of an oak leaf and place it on one or both eyelids, then place under the eye cotton wool soaked in egg white and place the patient's head in a vice.
Then place your index finger on top of the drug and press it gently while moving it whenever the patient feels its sweetness like fire. Leave the drug when he stops feeling its sweetness, then remove it and wash the eye with water.
Observe, for if you find the eyelid raised as it is during cauterization with fire or cutting, except [raise it] with medicine until your work is complete and the eye is healed; then place cotton soaked in butter upon it until the burning sensation subsides.
Then treat it with the yellow ointment or another until it heals; and you must be extremely careful during treatment that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, for if the eyelid relaxes after days and some hair enters the eye.
Prepare the medicine on that spot where the eyelid has relaxed, especially as you did at first; then treat it until it heals. This is the figure of the willow leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size and smallness, so let your cauterization be according to that. There is no other correct way for one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the extraction of the nasal bone which protrudes at the eye socket.
If, after treating the nasal bone as mentioned in the sections on diseases, your treatment does not succeed, then cauterize it in this manner:
Order the patient to place their head in a vice and have an assistant hold their head firmly with both hands, ensuring that it does not move or shake.
Then place a cotton wool moistened in egg white or spittle on their eyes; then heat the cauterizing iron which has this shape:
Let it be hollow like the shape of a feather quill at one end, which is used for cauterization; if you wish, it can also have an open tip at the other end, or closed like a rod. However, this hollow shape is better for your work, if Allah wills.
Then squeeze the nasal bone if it is open and remove its discharge, then rinse it; or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus. Then immediately place the heated cauterizing iron upon it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, so that your hand does not accidentally touch the eye or disturb the patient, causing the iron to fall onto the eye's conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first attempt, otherwise repeat the cauterization if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a bandage with butter and treat it with the dry plaster until it heals. If forty days pass without healing, apply the caustic drug until the bone is exposed, as will be mentioned in its chapter, if God wills.
Another method of treating nasal fistulas, mentioned by some early authors, involves making an incision at the site of the fistula and then inserting a thin funnel into the same incision. This is its appearance.
Pour into it half an ounce of molten lead, holding the funnel firmly with one hand while pressing it tightly against the wound. Keep the patient still to prevent the molten lead from entering their eye. It is preferable to place a cloth soaked in egg white or water over the eye.
If the lead cauterizes the site of the fistula and heals it with remarkable healing, then the nasal fistula will be treated by the methods mentioned above. Otherwise, it will be necessary to perform nasal cautery and reposition the nasal septum into its proper place, as will be discussed in a later section devoted to this procedure.
Chapter Eighteenth.
On the treatment of cleft lip.
Cleft lip, often referred to as "hare lip," is common, especially in infants. If treatment with the methods mentioned earlier does not prove effective, then heat a small, flat iron on this shape:
Heat it over the fire until its hollow side is red hot, then place it on the cleft while rotating it until the heat reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with cupping glass until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Nineteenth
On the treatment of nasal fistulas occurring in the mouth.
If a nasal fistula appears at the base of the teeth, palate or roots of the incisors and swells, then bursts and becomes discharging, and treatment fails to improve it, then heat an iron on this shape that fits into the fistula.
Insert it while hot into the nasal fistula and hold it until the iron reaches its depth. Repeat this once or twice, then treat it with the methods mentioned earlier until it heals, if God wills.
If the matter has been severed and there is no pus, then exploration of the site is necessary, and removal of the rotten bone according to what will come in its chapter, if Allah wills.
The twentieth chapter.
About loose teeth and relaxed ones.
If the gums have become loose due to moisture and the teeth have moved, even after treating them with medication without success, then place the root of the pestle in your armpit, then heat the bellows that will come later, having placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the bellows with the heated spoon, holding your hand for a short while until you feel the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth, then release your hand and repeat this several times according to what you desire, then fill your mouth with salt water.
And hold it thus for a while and spit it out, for the loose tooth will become fixed and the relaxed gum will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry up.
The twenty-first chapter.
About toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or there is decay and medication has not helped, then there are two methods: either cauterization with fat, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, take beef fat and melt it in an iron spoon or a seashell, then take some cotton, wrap it around the end of a stick, dip it into the melted fat, place it on the painful tooth quickly and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, you can also dip a wick or cotton in cold fat and place it on the painful tooth, then put the heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
And as for cauterization with fire, take a copper or iron tube with some thickness to prevent the heat of the fire from reaching the mouth, then heat the bellows that will come later and place it on the same tooth, holding your hand until the bellows cools. Repeat this several times.
If pain departs either on that day or the following one, then it is necessary to fill the patient's mouth with good oil and hold him thus for a moment before expelling him. This is the image of the press.
Press using whichever end you wish, on whatever side is possible; this is the picture of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Second
In the pressing of pigs.
If pigs are due to phlegm and cold humors, and do not yield to ripening with drugs, and you wish a swift ripening, then use the hollow press whose image is shown here.
To extract smoke during pressing from the other end, place it protected on the same tumor once or twice if needed until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, adjust the press to its size.
Then leave it for three days, apply a linen cloth dipped in oil until what was burned by fire is gone, then treat with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Third
On pressing for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm prevails over the trachea, especially if accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first expercate with softening drugs, then press at the notch of the neck at the base of the larynx in the depressed place.
But be careful not to press on the larynx itself, nor burn more than a bit of skin. Then apply another press at the junction of the neck at its prominent end, and let the press be made in the manner described.
Then treat as mentioned above until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth
On pressing for lung disease and coughing.
If coughing and lung disease are due to cold phlegm, without fever or rigor, and the illness is persistent, then press with two presses on the lower sides of the chest at the empty depressions near the armpits, and another press in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cauterizing iron should be made as depicted. If you wish, it can also be made into a pointed cautery called 'the point,' whose image has been presented in the case of earache. The points are thirty in number or thereabouts.
Then treat it with what has been mentioned until it heals. A cauterizing iron can also be made with three branches as shown in the image, which is useful for cauterizing three areas at once in a single application. This is an image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth
In case of sciatica.
If the head of the shoulder joint is dislocated due to slippery fluids or has not set properly and becomes habitual, it should be reduced gradually without forcefully returning it at once. Then the patient lies on their back or on the unaffected side. The skin within the armpit is then raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery with two points, as shown in this image.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it pierces through to the other side, forming four points of cautery. Alternatively, you can use a cautery with three points, resulting in six points of cautery, with the points placed gently on the muscle.
This is an image of the cautery with three points.
And one more may be added to this number, resulting in eight points of cautery. Then apply the powdered frankincense with salt to the wound. Have the patient remain at rest and avoid moving the limb until it gains strength. Dislocation upwards occurs infrequently.
Cauterize above the shoulder joint with a single large round cautery, or several pointed cauteries if necessary. This will strengthen the joint and remove excess moisture, allowing the patient to recover.

Chapter Twenty-Seventh.
In Cold Liver Incision.
If the liver is afflicted with pain due to coldness and dampness or wind, such that it has deviated excessively from its natural state, despite treating the patient with what was mentioned in the previous sections without success. It is necessary for the patient to lie on their back, and three incisions should be marked with ink on this shape and size on the liver below the ribs where the human elbow ends.

And burn only half the thickness of the skin, no more. The patient should stand on their feet; if they cannot stand, they should lie down with their legs extended and arms raised. This is an image of the cautery.
And you may cauterize these incisions with the scalpel cautery if you are skilled and careful, taking care not to overdo it and burn all of the skin, reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin.
Chapter Twenty-Eighth.
In Abdominal Enlargement Due to Liver Tumor: Incision.

If the liver's surface is inflamed, accompanied by severe pain and leaving doctors at a loss for treatment, it is advisable for the patient to lie on their back. Apply cataplasm on the warm site using ink.

Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On Cauterizing a Hemorrhoid.
Among the first methods mentioned for cauterizing a prolapsed hemorrhoid is this: Take one long, dry root of asafoetida, about the thickness of a finger, dip it in oil, and heat it in fire. Then cauterize once between the neck and shoulder junction, two small cauteries below the ears slightly inclined towards the chin, and two large cauteries above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more between the fifth and sixth ribs inclined slightly backward, one in the middle of the chest, another above the stomach, three behind the shoulders between the scapulae, and two below the elbow on the inner side of the forearm.
And do not penetrate deep with the cautery, but rather apply it superficially on the skin. Some authorities mentioned using an iron incline-shaped cautery that is heated and inserted between the buttocks until it reaches the prolapsed hemorrhoid.
And the matter will come out as mentioned in liver cautery. With this method, either the patient dies immediately or the site develops non-healing ulcers.
Chapter Thirty.
On Cauterizing the Spleen.
If you treat the spleen condition mentioned in the treatment section without success, then cauterize it on three sides, all of which are correct. One option is to cauterize three or four times along the length of the spleen.
Form the liver cauteries in the shape mentioned earlier, with each cautery being about the thickness of a finger or slightly more. The same cautery technique described for the liver should be used here, without pressing too deeply. The patient should be lying on their back.
The other method is at the elbow, raising the skin that faces the spleen where the left elbow ends. The skin should be raised to the width of the body so that the cauteries fall along the length of the body.
Then insert both needles deeply until they pass through to the other side. Remove the cautery, resulting in four cauteries. If you prefer, use the three-needle cautery.
Then treat the cauterization site after it has oozed pus for many days. This is the most effective of all treatments mentioned.

The patient should not be deprived of other treatments after cauterization, as combining treatments expedites recovery. The shape of the iron cautery used for the back is as previously described, and the shape of the wax cautery used for the back is conical.

Then cauterize both legs twice on each leg, with the pointed end of the cautery iron and along the length of the leg once below the knee and another midway down the thigh and two on each buttock.
Leave the wound open without treatment for a long time until water drains from it, then treat it with the rest of what has been mentioned.

Chapter Forty-Four
In treating hemorrhoids.
If pain occurs in the hip area, caused by coldness and dampness, and the physician has applied the treatments mentioned earlier without success, then it is recommended for the physician to soften the thick humors with castor oil or Indian bdellium, etc.
Then cauterize and apply the cautery in two ways: either by cautery using burning drugs, or by fire cautery. The fire cautery has many forms; one of them is to cauterize three times on the actual hemorrhoid site in this manner.
Make each cauterization have a suitable depth, with about the thickness of a finger between each cautery. Use a spoon-shaped cautery, and if desired, cauterize once in the middle on top of the anus itself to make it four cauteries. If you prefer, you can cauterize using the circular cautery mentioned earlier for one hemorrhoid.
This is the description of cauterization for hemorrhoids if the pain is persistent and confined to the hip area without extending to the thigh or leg.
If the pain extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize twice on the thigh at the point indicated by the patient as painful. Also, cauterize once above the anus with a scalpel-shaped cautery about four fingers to the right side of it. The depth of the cautery should be equal to the thickness of the skin only.
If the pain extends towards the toes of the foot, cauterize three or four times, or more if necessary, at the point indicated by the patient using a pointed cautery. If the pain is beneath the knee and extending downwards towards the leg, cauterize once there using a scalpel-shaped cautery.
Be careful during all cauteries to avoid reaching and burning any large nerve or artery, as this would cause harm or disablement to the patient.
I have seen one or two cases where a cautery was applied too high near the anus, causing inflammation in the leg that reached up to the foot. The entire leg became swollen and decomposed, followed by diarrhea and death afterwards. If the pain is on both sides, cauterize in this same manner for both sides.
Some wise scholars have described this hip dislocation as resembling a cup made of iron, with its diameter being half a cubit. At its mouth, it should be slightly thicker than the stone of a date or even less so, and within that cup there should be another cup and yet another cup.
And the distance between each of these cups should be equal to the thickness of a thumb. The cups are open on both sides and their height is about one or two thumbs. An iron clamp is made for them, fitted precisely onto the cups.
Then it is heated in a fire until it turns red hot and the sparks fly off. It is then placed on the hip dislocation, with the patient leaning on their sound side, and three circular movements are made at once. It is left like this for three days, then smeared with fat and the wound is kept open.
And then it is treated with an ointment until it heals. The author of this book says that this type of dislocation is rarely used due to its severity and the horror of its appearance, but also because few people are able to endure it, except for those who have a good constitution and are willing to endure it in its proper place.
And as for reduction using burning drugs, it is done by making two cups similar to door hinges, made of copper or iron.
Their height should be about two thumbs or more, and the distance between them should be equal to the thickness of a finger. They are open at both ends, with one facing downwards and one facing upwards, and they fit together in this manner.
Then they are placed on the hip dislocation while the patient lies on their sound side. Press your hand firmly down, then pour hot nitric acid between the two circles and hold it for an hour or so until the patient can endure its burning sensation.
Until the burning sensation subsides, then lift your hand and wipe away the nitric acid from the hip with fresh water. Leave it like this for three days, applying fat to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, allowing pus to discharge until healed.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals, if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make him a bandage that holds water according to what you did for the hip joint. This is the description of the sharp water and I have proven it in the book 'Reform of Medicines.'
Take one part of natron (soda ash) and one part of unslaked lime, grind them together, place them in a new pot that has been perforated at its bottom with a small hole according to the size of the wick, and put another pot underneath it.
Pour fresh water over the natron and lime until it covers them by a finger's breadth, after squeezing them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water drains into the bottom of the perforated pot, then collect all that water and pour it onto another fresh batch of lime and natron.
And thus, at that point, it becomes very sharp and can be used for many surgical procedures and cauterization of various parts, as it has the effect of fire itself. Among the remedies that are also used to cauterize the hip joint is Thapsia, dates syrup, lime mixed with soap.
Galen claims a story about an ancient man who treated hip pain and sciatica, increasing his reputation greatly, and claimed that no other treatment was needed as it cured in one application.
Until he might have entered the bath paralyzed and exited cured. If fresh green Frankincense is not available, then take some recently dried one, pound it with a little bit of fat, and apply it to the hip joint where the pain is felt, or on the leg or thigh.
And bind it, leaving it for three hours or until the patient feels that the burning sensation has subsided, then enter him into the bath. If his body perspires, then enter him into the tub. The pain will go away and he will be cured, if God wills. If not cured, repeat the bandage after ten days.
If God wills, he will be cured. Know that none of these cauterizations should be used until after the body has purged itself. Dioscorides mentioned that burning goat hair is beneficial for treating sciatica.
He should be like this: wool is taken, soaked in old oil and placed on the deep space between the thumb and wrist, closer to the wrist. Then take dry donkey hide.
Kindle it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it extinguishes. Then take another one, continuing this process until the pain reaches midway between elbow and hip, and subsides by God's will. This type of cauterization is called 'cautery of the hide'.

Chapter Forty-Four
About gout and joint pain.
If the joint pains are due to cold humidities that settle in any organ of the body, if these pains occur in the legs, physicians usually call it gout specifically.
If the cold cause of gout is treated by various remedies mentioned earlier but the pains do not subside, then poultices are used to alleviate them. After urination, many warm poultices should be applied around the ankles and knees in this manner: use moderate-sized, oily poultices.
If it is necessary to cauterize the face, do so with a hot iron. If the pains ascend to the knees or other joints - which often happens - apply three or four such oily poultices on each side of both knees.
If more poultices are needed, apply them without pressing too hard with your hand, just enough to warm the skin slightly. If the pains ascend to the buttocks or back, use the poultices mentioned earlier for those areas.

Chapter Forty-Five
About cautery for hemorrhoids.
If hemorrhoids appear in the anus and some intestines or prolapse descend to the scrotum, especially if it is a recent onset, it is not advisable for the patient to fast that day. Instead, they should use mild laxatives to facilitate the evacuation of feces from the bowels.
Then, have the patient lie on their back in front of you. Order them to hold themselves up until the prolapsed tissue or intestines protrude, then push it back with your finger. Mark the location of the hemorrhoid under the anus using ink, resembling a semicircle with its ends pointing towards the upper body.
Then heat this iron to its shape.
Until it turns white and sparks are emitted, then return the pot or vessel to its center. Then place a servant's hand on the spot so that the pot does not slip out while the patient's legs are spread apart and placed upon a cushion. Another servant should then tie his legs together.
Another should hold him down by his chest, then lower the iron onto the same spot while keeping one's hand on it until it reaches the bone or repeat if not reached on the first try.
Be careful not to let the pot protrude while you move, lest it burn and cause either death or great harm to the patient. Know that if the heat does not reach the bone, your procedure has failed. It is advisable for children's pots to be of a size suitable for their bodies and likewise for adults.
Then treat the burned area with clarified butter three days later to remove its blackness, then apply other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound scars over.
It is advisable to make his diet soft during recovery so that the pot does not slip out during evacuation or urination. After forty days, if he wishes to rise, use a supportive bandage and keep it on for another forty days to reduce exhaustion from heavy food, drink, and loud shouting.
If this procedure is followed as such, God willing, the patient will recover completely. I will mention the treatment of hernias caused by incisions in their proper place, God willing. As for hernias that occur elsewhere on the abdomen and are beginning to develop, if you wish not to increase the size of the hernia opening, use a round iron of suitable size. Let what you burn be like two-thirds of its size, then treat it as mentioned earlier. God willing, it will not increase. Chapter Sixty-Four
On the Treatment of Goitre
If pain occurs in any organ due to a blow or injury, and that pain persists for a long time without responding to medicinal treatment, then cauterization with a branding iron should be performed. The size of the cautery must be determined according to the size, strength, and weakness of each organ.
If the pain and goiter are alleviated by this cautery, then no further treatment is necessary. However, if they persist, the cautery should be repeated because it is common for such pains to spread to nearby organs. Therefore, the cautery should be applied until the patient is cured.

On the treatment of leprosy.
