Chapter Fifty.
On the treatment of cancer.
If the cancer is at its beginning and you wish to stop it, ligature it with a circular band around it like a ring. Some sages have mentioned that a strong ligament should be tied in its center, but I do not recommend that because I expect it to become gangrenous, as I have seen happen many times.
The correct method is to suture around it in a circular fashion as mentioned earlier, or with many stitches.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On the treatment of boils.
If a boil appears and its healing is delayed, either due to excessive blood flow around it or because the patient is elderly with poor blood circulation or due to the time of year, use many small stitches placed closely together around it.
Then leave it alone; it will heal quickly. If you wish to cut it open, surround the area with a ligature as shown here.
And lower the ligature to the center of the boil until it penetrates the skin, making the stitch below the boil so that pus can easily flow out. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On treating ulcers.
If an ulcer is present in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, surround it with many small and large iron stitches according to what is appropriate for that location.
Then cauterize it on all sides until the decay is completely removed, leaving no trace. Leave it for three days and then apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas to remove any remaining debris and decay.
Then treat it with flesh-germinating ointments. If after three days the flesh grows back healthy and free from decay, otherwise repeat cauterization on any remaining decayed areas. The ulcer can also be treated with harsh medicines.
This method is faster and more effective than cauterization with fire, although the treatment of ulcers using harsh medicines has been mentioned in the relevant section [Chapter 56].
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On treating inverted and non-inverted warts.
This condition often occurs at the bottom of the feet; it is a rough growth that troubles people. Its treatment can be by cauterization with fire or using caustic water.
If you wish to cauterize with fire, take a hollow iron spoon shaped like the feathers of a vulture, fitting around the screw on all sides and thin-edged. Heat it over the screw, then rotate your hand with the spoon around the screw until the spoon reaches the depth of the screw.
Leave it for three days until it festers, then dress it with fine, salt-ground bread and leave the dressing overnight. If it adheres to its base, treat the wound area with a flesh-generating ointment until it heals.
Often, boils appear on the surface of the body, especially on hands and feet. Therefore, take a tube made of copper or iron or vulture feathers, place it over the boil or pimple, then pour a little vinegar into the tube.
Hold your hand while rotating it, gently pressing the edge of the tube onto the boil's root to allow the vinegar to seep into its base. The patient should endure the sting for a moment.
Leave it thus until the boil comes off completely. Do this for each one, until all are removed from the body. Then treat the areas with flesh-generating ointments.
Chapter Forty-Five
On cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If hemorrhoids occur due to cold or fever lasting a quarter day or longer, cauterize four or five times between the buttocks, with one cautery mark per knot and one on the back. And cauterize once on his stomach using a golden spoon. This will alleviate hemorrhoids and speed up recovery from cold diseases.
Chapter Fifty-Five
On cauterization of boils appearing on the body.
When sudden, ugly boils appear on the body due to cold, hard, corrupt materials, cauterize each boil gently with a burning tip of an oak twig or a long red-hot poker or an iron spoon as soon as they emerge.
And let the bloodletting cease immediately upon this sign, lest its beneficial effect be diminished and dissipated; the patient will only recover from it once he has evacuated by cupping.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On bleeding resulting from cutting an artery.
It often happens that blood flows copiously from a cut artery during an external wound, or when incising a tumor or evacuating an abscess, etc., making it difficult to tie off.
If this happens, quickly place your index finger at the mouth of the artery and press it tightly until bleeding stops completely, then heat some bronze forceps in fire until they become very hot.
Take one forceps, either small or large depending on the wound and location of the severed artery, and apply it to the artery itself immediately after removing your finger. Hold it until bleeding stops completely.
If blood gushes forth when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and the forceps cools down, quickly take another heated forceps from those prepared in the fire, continuing this process until bleeding ceases.
Be careful not to burn the tissues, causing further injury to the patient. Know that it is impossible to tie off a bleeding artery, especially if it is large, except by one of four methods: cauterization as mentioned earlier, or amputation if it has not already been severed. If the artery is ligated, its ends will retract and bleeding will stop; otherwise, it must be tied tightly with sutures or treated with medications that coagulate blood and compression bandages.
But attempting to tie off or compress an artery with cloth, hot objects, etc., will not stop bleeding except rarely. If this happens and there is no doctor or medication available, quickly place the index finger at the mouth of the wound as described earlier.
Press firmly until bleeding stops and blood coagulates around the finger. Keep it there with cold water continuously, even if it freezes your hand, until bleeding ceases completely. Meanwhile, assess what treatment is needed: cautery or medication.
The Second Chapter
In the case of the side (abdominal) wall, the belly, cupping, and surgeries etc.
Al-Khalaf mentioned earlier in the first chapter all diseases that are cured by cauterization with fire and burning drugs, their causes and reasons for occurrence, as well as their instruments and manifestations.
And I will follow this approach in this chapter to facilitate the student's understanding of the subject.
Before starting, you should know, O my sons, that this chapter contains more severe cases than those mentioned in the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, the warning in this chapter should be stronger because the operations involved are many and often lead to profuse bleeding, which is essential for life when opening a vein or an incision on a tumor, an abscess, a surgical treatment, or removing an arrowhead or an incision for extracting a stone. All of these cases involve danger and fear, and death may occur in many of them.
I warn you against being careless in matters that are doubtful for you, as there are various kinds of people in this profession with different types of illnesses. Some may be weary of their disease and desirous of death.
Due to the severity of his pain and the length of his illness, some may seem close to death. Others may offer you money in the hope of recovery from their fatal disease; however, it is not permissible for you to accept any payment from such people at all times.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness. Do not proceed with anything unless you are certain of its outcome through reliable knowledge. Always use the introduction of knowledge and warning in treating all your patients, explaining what ensures their safety.
For this, you will gain praise and glory, and be remembered with thanks. May God guide you, O my sons, to the right path and grant you success, for that is in His power. There is no god but Him.
This chapter has been arranged with sections from head to toe, following what was mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. May it be easy for you to find what you seek, if God wills.
The First Chapter
In treating water that collects in the heads of children.
This condition frequently occurs in newborns at birth if the midwife presses the baby's head too hard, and it may also occur for an unknown hidden cause.
And I have not seen this condition in anyone other than children, and all of those whom I saw quickly died from it. Therefore, I saw that it is better to leave it alone. I also saw a child whose head was filled with water, and the head swelled more every day. Until the child could no longer sit up due to the size of his head and the increase in fluid, and he died.
This fluid may accumulate either between the skin and the bone, or beneath the bone on the peritoneum. If it is accumulated between the skin and the bone, and the swelling is small, then a single incision should be made in the middle of the head, horizontally, about two fingerbreadths long, to allow the fluid to drain out. This is an illustration of the probe.
If the fluid has increased and the swelling is greater, then make two intersecting incisions like this:.
If the fluid is beneath the bone, its indication is that the fontanelles are open on all sides and water flows inwards when squeezed with the hand. This should not be concealed from you, and therefore three incisions should be made in the middle of the head like this:.
After the incision, all of the fluid should come out. Then close the incisions with bandages and compresses. After that, bathe him from above with vinegar and oil for five days. Then release the bandage and treat the wound with sutures and ointments. Do not tie the head too tightly and feed the patient dry foods that are not moist.
Until the organ is strengthened and the wound heals, another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling appears and where the water collects because it may be more in the back or front, or on the right or left side.
So aim for the incision where you see the swelling and the accumulation of water, make it as deep as you can, but be careful not to cut an artery so that there is no bleeding that causes death along with the discharge of fluid.
Chapter Two
Ligation of the two arteries behind the ears known as the "temporal arteries".
When a person has sudden severe pain in the eyes or chest, and this persists without being cured by medication, then informing treatment consists of ligating these two arteries.
It is necessary, if one wants to ligate them (the arteries), that the head of the patient be shaved with a razor, then the site be rubbed with a coarse cloth so that the artery becomes visible. Then, the neck of the patient should be extended by his garment, and look where the pulse appears and locate the two pulsating points behind the ears, for they are usually hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a sharp scalpel until bone is reached. This should be done with the head of the patient raised. Alternatively, one may insert the scalpel underneath the artery and lift it upwards for the cut, which should be about two fingerbreadths long.
If the vessel is cut, blood will flow out pulsatingly like a fountain onto the cloth in front. If the artery cannot be felt, then measure three fingerbreadths from the ear, mark it with ink and cut down to bone. It is preferable that...
Then, tie off the wound with four ligatures. Some may require more or less depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their arteries. Then examine the wound to ensure that all of the peritoneum has been cut away from the bone.
So that no inflammation occurs, then close the wound with a bandage made of linen cloth and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three
On ligation of the arteries in the ears.
If a person suffers from chronic headaches, sudden severe pain in the muscles of the ears, or persistent intense headaches due to hot and humid conditions, and various medicinal treatments have not been effective, then we have tried ligation of the arteries of the ears or their branches, as described.
The procedure for ligation consists of shaving the hair on the patient's ears, and then locating the visible artery in the temple, which can be felt pulsating. It may not be apparent in some individuals.
If you are suffering from intense cold, wrap the patient's neck with his cloth then rub the area with a cloth or apply warm water until the pulse becomes clearly visible. Then take the lancet that looks like this. Then gently cut the skin with it until you reach the artery, then insert a tweezers and pull it upwards until it exits the skin and remove any membranes underneath from all sides. If the artery is thin, use the tip of the tweezers to tie it. Then cut off a section equal to the distance between its ends and do not cause bleeding. If it does not bleed, remove six ounces of blood from three places.
If the artery is very large, then tie it in two places with a strong linen thread or catgut to prevent gangrene from occurring before the wound heals.
Then cut off the excess between the ties at that time or later. If you wish, cauterize it with a heated skinnер blade to burn its ends. This serves the same purpose or even better as mentioned before.
However, if the patient has a fever or is of irritable temperament, cauterization may not be beneficial as it dries out the body fluids. Therefore, after tying off the arteries, pack the area with sterile cotton and dress it properly. After healing, treat it with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and bandage it until it heals, if God wills.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, immediately cut the artery either by cauterization or filling the site with powdered myrrh and pressing your hand until bleeding stops. If that doesn't help, apply pressure with your finger until bleeding ceases.
Then wash the area with very cold water to numb the pain and dress it properly. It is easier to cauterize veins than arteries using this skinner blade after marking the sites with ink.
I press firmly until it reaches the bone and the vein breaks at two points so that its ends are separated, for it will not adhere to this instrument. This is an image of the forceps.
The edges of the scissors resemble two small points except that they are much duller than a knife because if they were sharp like it, cold would come upon them quickly and the flesh would not be cut off quickly. Therefore, they should be heated with fire.
Cut the flesh quickly, for this action is better than all other actions and easier, and let there be between the scissors the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four
In treating constant hot tears flowing to the eyes.
If tears are continuous and their flow is from the veins on the skull outside, without benefit from medicinal treatment of medicine, and you see the patient's face to be red. And he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead like that of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, with their corners inflamed and their eyelids sticking together due to the intensity of tears. Therefore, the first resort in treating this is to order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead along its length. The first incision is about two fingers' width long, midway between the eyebrows and the tip of the nose. The second is slightly further away from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third is on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the cheekbones. Leave about threefold fingers' width between each incision. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry rags to absorb blood.
Then insert a sharp-pointed probe like this one: From the incision near the temple to the middle incision, and scrape off all the skin and fascia that lies between them from the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third one, and then remove this probe. And insert into the first incision another instrument called the sharp-edged scissor-like tool with one side sharp and the other blunt, like this:
Its sharp side should be turned upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its flat side towards the bone; it is pushed until it reaches the middle slit, cutting all vessels descending from the head to the eyes without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then, do this from the middle slit to the other slit. After a moderate amount of bleeding has flowed, squeeze the places where the hardened blood was cut. Then, in each slit, place a cotton wick moistened with a mixture of rose water and oil or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur. On the third day, dissolve the suture with warm water and use copious irrigation. Then treat it with basilicon plaster mixed with rose oil and other wound remedies until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five
On treating tears and discharges to the eyes from within the head.
When someone has frequent, sharp, continuous, hot discharges that make both eyes appear bloodshot and small, weaken their vision, cause the eyelids to become inflamed and shed tears. And there is severe, painful headache and frequent sneezing. Know that these symptoms indicate that the discharges come from many deep vessels; the best treatment for this condition is as follows: Order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful headache and frequent sneezing. Know that these symptoms indicate that the discharges come from many deep vessels; the best treatment for this condition is as follows: Order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly across, starting the incision from the left temple to the right, reaching the bone and keeping your hand away from the chewing muscles of the temples until the bone is exposed and all the blood is wiped off with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with a cotton pad soaked in rose water and oil or roll some linen threads between them. Then tie it from above with bandages. Moisten the bandages with the same mixture to prevent inflammation. If inflammation occurs, do not scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with the dry regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as making a plaster of two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, applying it to promote flesh growth in wounds like this.
Chapter Six
On the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear belongs to four types: either a metallic stone or something resembling stone like iron and glass, or a plant-like substance such as chickpeas and kernels, or a liquid thing like water and vinegar.
Or an animal, in which case if a stone or a type of stone falls into the ear that does not grow there, receive it with the ear facing the sun. If you see only the stone, rub some violet or sesame oil on it, then try to remove it by moving your head or sneezing vigorously while pressing on the tragus. And block the nostrils upon sneezing after placing a bandage made of cloth or wool around the ear and pulling it up towards the cheekbone. Many times, this treatment will remove it. If not, then try to remove it with the gentle hook whose shape is shown here.
If it comes out with the hook or not, then try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent hollow tube. If it still does not come out, then make a tube of copper and insert its end into the opening of the ear carefully.
And seal around the tube with soft wax mixed with oil so that there is no way for wind except through the tube, then pull it out forcefully with your hook. Many times, this will remove it. If not as described earlier, take a small amount of Nabatian gum or prepared pitch used for birds and place it at the end of the reed.
After wrapping it tightly with cloth, then insert it into the opening of the ear gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out despite all that has been described, hurry to make an incision before inflammation or spasms occur.
The prescription for the incision: first, let the patient bleed from the vein of the forehead according to his strength, then let the patient sit in front of you and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear where it is fleshy on its lower side.
The incision should be crescent-shaped so that it reaches the stone, then remove it with whatever tools you can. Then stitch up the incision immediately and treat it until it heals.
If the object inserted into the ear is one of the tumors that grow and swell, try to remove it using the method mentioned earlier. If it does not yield for removal, then take a fine, sharp scalpel of this shape.
Attempt to cut that type of tumor inserted in the ear with it, provided you are certain that the tumor has become softened by the moisture of the ear until it becomes like small fragments. Then remove them using a syringe or a soft probe.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove them. As for water that has entered the ear, one should not attempt to blow their nose forcefully with a reed first and then plug the ear with some cotton wool while lying on the affected side until the water comes out.
If not, take many small, smooth stones along the length of the fingers, warm them over a fire slightly, and insert one into the ear while standing on one leg on that side. Then strike it with another stone.
Continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water comes out. Water may also be removed by taking a reed or feather, inserting one end into the ear while holding the other end in fire until it burns, then replacing the feather.
Repeat this several times until all the water is removed or drawn out using a syringe as mentioned earlier. As for removing insects that have entered, if they are small like lice, treat them with what was mentioned in the previous section.
If the body of the creature is large and visible to the touch, try to remove it using a probe and tweezers. Its removal is easier than any other thing that may enter the ear. As for removing worms produced in the ear, if they are treated with what was mentioned in the previous section on earwax but no improvement is seen,
It is not necessary to look into the ear in the sun. If you see any worms, remove them using a probe or tweezers. If nothing appears, take a syringe of this shape.
Insert the narrow end of the syringe into the ear up to a point that it can tolerate, then strongly suck with it several times until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the syringe with wax as mentioned earlier.
If the mentioned [disease] is not expelled, then use the collyria that we have mentioned and which earlier practitioners have tested for killing worms. You will find this in the treatise on collyria. Use this instrument to drip melted fats and medications into the ear. This is its appearance:
It appears to be made of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, you may line the interior pipe with tightly fitting brass, or alternatively, take a reed and wrap its tip tightly with cloth.
Then place the fat or serum or desired medication into the pipe while it is in the ear. Then insert the reed with its cloth from above and squeeze gently until you feel that the fat enters the auditory canal.
While the patient is lying down, ensure that what you drip into the ear has been warmed slightly in fire. Be careful that it is not too cold or too hot, as the ear cannot tolerate this.
Chapter Seven.
On the Treatment of Sudden Deafness.
Some children are born from their mothers' wombs with unperforated ear drums.
It may also happen to some people that there is a blockage in the auditory canal due to an injury or flesh growing therein. This blockage may be deep within the ear canal and invisible.
If it extends slightly out of the canal and is visible, then the one deep within the canal that is not visible is usually difficult to remove. However, if it is visible, do not expose the patient's ear to sunlight. If you look into the ear and see a visible obstruction, open it with a gentle probe like this:
The tip should be slightly blunted to prevent injury to the ear. If the obstruction is flesh that has grown in the canal, grasp it with a gentle forceps and carefully pull it out until all of the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear canal, take a smooth, gentle hook, warm it slightly in fire, and insert it into the canal. If you feel the obstruction while the patient feels heaviness in their ear, gently pull on it.
Take care not to injure the ear's artery. Then, insert into the ear a wick of suitable size for the wound's width, dipped in Egyptian mercury ointment. Do this repeatedly until the wound heals, or use a wick moistened with water and sprinkled with ground natron.
Use it. If you find the ear has become inflamed and hot, do not remove the wick but replace it with another one dipped in rose-oil ointment until the inflammation subsides, then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Then use the rest of the treatment mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eight.
In treating warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts appearing on the eyelids may be moist or dry. Do not grasp them with forceps or pincers and cut them off at their roots, then apply powdered natron to the spot.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a hot copper coin and press it firmly after cutting. It often grows back if some root remains; burning the roots with fire prevents its recurrence.
Chapter Nine.
In treating sudden inflammation of the eye's eyelids.
It often happens that something resembling coldness and stiffness appears in the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called 'coldness' – a thick dampness gathered in the upper and lower eyelids. If the coldness is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to all sides, it is easy to treat by making an incision across it.
Then carefully scrape it off from all sides until it is removed, grasp it with forceps and cut it. If cutting cannot be done immediately, wait until the eyelid heals after cutting, then treat it until it recovers.
If it is small, there is no harm in leaving it alone as the ointment will dissolve and heal it. If the coldness is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, turn the eyelid over and grasp the coldness with forceps without making an incision, then cut it off from all sides.
If the eyelid is cut through, no harm will come of it; then wash the eye after cutting the bridle with salt water and treat the site with a dressing that promotes healing until the wound heals.
Chapter Ten
Regarding the treatment of hemorrhoids appearing on the eyelid.
Hemorrhoids are fat that appears in the upper layers of the eyelid, especially among young boys. They cause heaviness of the eyes and frequent illnesses during summer and make it difficult for them to look towards sunlight. So that tears may flow quickly
Therefore, they are always seen leaning on their faces and sleeping with their eyelids beneath the eyebrows wet, swollen and disfigured by prominent hemorrhoids. When you press the area with your fingers, you will feel the hemorrhoids between them. The procedure is for the patient to place his head in a sling.
Then take a linen cloth, prepare from it a wick and make from this wick a circle around the size of the hemorrhoid on all sides. Then place it on the hemorrhoid and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the hemorrhoid in the center of the circle.
Then cut through the center of this wetness with a sharp lancet horizontally, making sure not to make the cut larger than a fisted wound. As for depth, do not cut all the way through the skin until you reach the hemorrhoid.
In most cases, the hemorrhoid will protrude from the cut like a white fat piece. Gently pull it out using a cloth wrapped between your fingers while turning your hand left and right until it is completely removed. Be careful not to increase the cut lest the lancet slips into the eye and injures it.
If the hemorrhoid does not appear in the first cut, do not increase it much gently until the hemorrhoid appears. Then remove it as mentioned before. Next, dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the site while pressing gently. Some people use crushed salt instead.
Then place it in the cut to dissolve any remaining moisture. Treat the area until it heals. If inflammation occurs, treat it with musk powder until it heals.
Chapter Eleven
On Kinds of Eye Diseases
If superfluous eyelashes grow on the eye out of their natural course under the natural lashes and stick together, they harm the eye and cause diseases such as continuous tearing, drooping of the eyelids, opacity, and thickness to the point that this can lead to blindness.
Eye diseases occur in four forms: either by cauterization with fire or by applying sharp medicine as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization; or by cutting and suturing, or by cauterizing with caustic.
It is necessary to place the patient's head in your lap and then flip the eyelid with your left hand. If it doesn't flip over, insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and exit upwards near the lashes, then pull the thread up while holding the eyelid.
Then flip it with a spatula, then cut inside the eyelid without touching the excess hair using curved scissors from the larger angle to the smaller one. Then tie the thread and place a small cotton pad or gauze under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an ash leaf, but it should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid as people vary in this regard; some may require cutting off a suitable amount from the eyelid depending on how droopy it is.
Some may require less cutting depending on how droopy the eyelid is. Then cut along the two lines you've marked using scissors, starting from the larger angle to the smaller one, making sure each cut is close to the natural hair line, like the thickness of a spatula.
Then insert the probe into one of the skin corners, then dissect all of it. Then stitch up both edges using a thin wool thread and needle. Clean the blood and stick the remaining threads on the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do this, otherwise it does not matter. Leave the stitches and threads for about three or four days, then remove them. If you prefer not to stitch it up, let the wound dry and scar on its own, as the eyelid will raise upon healing and closing. Stitching is better.
This aspect of disfigurement has been mentioned by the ancients, except that it causes harm to the patient and is not beneficial. There is another aspect of disfigurement which is that one should teach the eyelid to assume a shape like that of an oak leaf as we have described. Then lift the eyelid with three sutures, arranged separately or grouped together in this manner
Then trim the remaining excess eyelid with small scissors in this manner, cutting carefully
If you are unable to tie the sutures and are not proficient in doing so, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the center of the shape, then insert another thread near the larger opening and a third one near the smaller opening. Gently gather the threads between your fingers, then raise your hand gently lifting them
Raise the skin taught over it all as described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If there is a hot swelling at the time of cutting or splitting, calm that swelling with iodoform or similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid turns inward, in which case it is preferable not to use cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire or strong medication. These methods are more harmful to the patient than beneficial.
Cauterization with a hot iron should be done in this manner: invert the eyelid, make an incision on its inner side as described, then fashion two thin rods of iron or wood, their length equal to that of the eyelid and width less than that of a lancet.
The tips of these rods have been cut from both ends where the threads will be attached. Then gather them gently over the remaining part of the eyelid and pull them tightly from both sides, leaving them in this manner for a certain period of time until the skin dies, blackens, and decomposes.
Until it falls off by itself. If this takes too long, cut it with scissors and treat it until it heals. When the wound has healed, the eyelid will elevate and the eyelashes will not be injured. Here is a diagram showing how to make these rods, their length and width are as follows:
The Eleventh Chapter
On Removing Foreign Bodies from the Eye with a Needle
If it is one or two hairs, then take a fine needle and thread through it a thin, smooth silk thread; gather its ends and tie them with a very small knot, let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then insert another thin thread through the knot at its end, attach one of its ends to it, then place the eye close to a source of light; for this is a delicate operation. Then enter the needle with the thread into the base of the superfluous hair.
Push it through the eyelid swiftly until it comes out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards and insert that hair into the needle if there are one or two or three hairs no more.
Then pull your hand gently until the hair comes out with the needle from above the natural hair, if you only pulled the needle and the hair did not come out, then pull the needle downwards with the thread that was inserted into it until the needle comes out of its upper hole.
Then put the hair back into the needle and pull both gently until it rises up in its upper hole and becomes visible to the eye, then cut the thread and remove it; keep the eyelid closed for two or three days until the hair is secured in place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either add a long natural hair to it so that it can be pulled up with it, or leave it alone until it grows longer, then pull it up.
Chapter Thirteen
On Treating Spasms That Occur in the Upper Eyelid
Some people are afflicted with a spasm in the upper eyelid, called "ptosis"; this condition is either natural or acquired. The acquired kind results from injury, inflammation, ulceration, or similar causes.
The procedure is to cut open that adhesion, separate its edges, and insert between them a cotton thread; tie it until healed. Do not use drying or constricting substances in treating it.
If you do this, the constriction will worsen instead of improving. Instead, use relaxing agents like fenugreek seeds soaked in a paste of lentils and linseed oil mixed with Diacillin ointment and some olive oil. Apply this mixture to the bandage.
And its treatment is to strive by all means not to return it to its original state. The procedure mentioned earlier is a method of correcting the constriction, not restoring the site to its original condition.
Chapter Fourteen
In treating lower eyelid entropion
This type of entropion, which occurs at the lower eyelid and is truly called 'straphismus,' can be either congenital or acquired. The latter may result from an injury, incision, or inflammation.
The procedure involves taking a needle with a double thread and inserting it into the flesh, passing from the left to the right, until the thread emerges at both ends of the flesh. Then, pull the flesh upwards using the needle and cut it with wide scissors.
If the eyelid does not resume its normal shape, take a probe and place it on the site of the incision. Then, invert the eyelid and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, with their ends meeting at the points of the previous cut, forming an angle. So that when joined together, it will resemble this shape:
Then, remove the excess flesh at its sharp lower edge near the eyelid. Next, sew the separated parts together using two silk sutures with cotton thread. Then treat it with the relaxing agents and bandages mentioned earlier until it heals.
If the entropion is due to a previous incision or stitch, it is necessary to make a simple incision without hair at the inner canthus as mentioned earlier. Then separate the eyelids using bandages as described.
In summary, the treatment of entropion, whether upper or lower, should be performed according to its appearance. Entropion can have varied presentations, and a skilled surgeon will find a way to manage it accordingly.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the same disease indicates what is suitable for it from work and tools in most cases.
The Fifteenth Chapter
In the adhesion of the eyelid with the mucus or the cornea.
It may happen to many people that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing its easy movement and hindering its natural functions. Therefore, one should insert a sharp-pointed probe beneath the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or extend it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a pair of scissors that are not sharp-pointed to avoid hurting the patient during the procedure and damaging the eye with their sharpness. The cutting should be like peeling off the adhesion.
And ensure that once the eyelid has returned to its natural shape and the adhesion is completely healed, you should apply a saline solution to wash the eye or administer eye drops made from camphor diluted in water. Then separate the eyelid from the eye using a cotton wick.
And place a wet cloth soaked in egg white over the eye, and after three days use medicated tampons until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen
On Cutting the Foreskin and Circumcision
The foreskin has two types: either it is nerve-like, resembling a thin tough membrane like the peritoneum; or it is non-nervous and resembles hardened whitish mucus that sticks to the iron when touched and can be cut with scissors.
And a probe cannot be inserted into either type. Both types start from the larger opening and gradually extend until they cover the pupil, preventing light and eye movement. To cut it, place the patient's head in a clamp.
Then open his eyes, lift the eyelid with your hand, grasp the foreskin with a slightly curved forceps and pull it upwards. Take a needle and insert a horse or cow hair thread into it, then bend the needle tip slightly and pierce it into the middle of the foreskin.
She pierces it with a needle and ties it with a thread, pulling it upwards. She scrapes the side of the lid that is near the eye, as if she were unrolling it with the shuttle, until she reaches its end, then cuts off what remains at the root of the eyelid with fine scissors or small forceps, leaving the natural flesh of the eyelid intact.
Lest there be a constant flow of tears from its severed part, for she stretches the lid alone with the needle or with thread as mentioned before, then scrapes it smooth with a rounded forceps like this:
And she should be cautious not to touch the corner of the eye with the forceps and cause a wound that affects the eye. Then, after cutting, she places some powdered salt or zinc oxide in the eye and leaves it for a day before treating it as necessary until it heals.
If the eyelid is not nerveless and she cannot insert a needle into it or fasten a thread, then often this type of eyelid is treated with medication until it heals as described in its section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth forceps with a weak grasp like this: And scrape the eyelid gently with it from above, if it becomes loose and crumbles under scraping, then immediately pour some zinc oxide or red lead or powdered salt into the eye and leave it for a day.
Then proceed with the procedure until it is all gone, except if you encounter a hot tumor in the eye, leave it alone and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume the procedure until it heals. As for the skin of the eyelid.
If the eyelash causes severe harm to the eye, tie that eyelash with a thread and cut off some of it without excessive cutting lest there be constant flow of tears. Then pour red lead or zinc oxide into the wounds until they heal.
Chapter Seventeen
On the Cutting of Warts and Excess Flesh That Grows in the Eye.
Some people's eyes may develop red flesh that is layered until it covers the visible part or approaches or overflows onto the eyelids, and perhaps the eyelids are inverted like a pomegranate flower.
The procedure is to place the patient's head on a support, then open their eyes and remove all that red flesh with appropriate tweezers.
Or grasp it with a forceps or needleholders, then cut off the first flesh first until all of it disappears by cutting, taking care not to harm the eye during the procedure. The cutting can be done either with the scissors we described for removing the prolapse of the rectum, or with small scissors. As those used to remove polyps as shown in the illustration in the following chapter.
After the cutting is complete and all the flesh has been removed, fill the eye with ground salt or drop into it red rose water and other suitable medications. Then hold cotton soaked in egg white against the eye from the outside to prevent inflammation.
If some flesh remains, bleeding is excessive, and there is fear of inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with medications to alleviate inflammation. Then resume the procedure once the inflammation has subsided until the eye heals completely. Do likewise for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, it is not advisable to perform this procedure on a weak eye due to illness or to attempt it visually without first ensuring that the eye is suitable for such an operation.
Chapter Eighteen.
On Removing Polyps from the Eye.
If polyps are red veins that grow on the eye, preventing vision and weakening the eye over time. Therefore, first examine if the eye containing the polyp is strong and free from other diseases besides the polyp.
Then proceed with the removal, which involves commanding the patient to place their head on a support. Then grasp those veins with one or two tweezers, depending on your skill. The tweezers should be fine and curved like this:
Or use two tweezers attached together in one body like this:
Then, carefully grasp those veins with fine forceps gently and wipe away the blood occasionally until you see the eye; for then those veins will have gone from it and the bleeding will have stopped. Be careful not to harm the eye with the tips of the forceps. Let your work take half a day facing the sun.
And be steadfast in your work so that you do not cut anything but those veins. For when you are finished, then cauterize the eye with green or red ointment to eat away any remaining vessels. If you cannot complete it all at once, then dress the eye with what alleviates its pain.
Leave it for a while until its pain subsides and the inflammation is safe, then resume this procedure with the eye until it heals completely from this distressing appearance of the forceps.
Leave it [and the patient's head should be on a cushion with their tears dried and the eyelids separated so that the eye is exposed], for the essence of the procedure is to open the eye using tweezers or a triangle held by an assistant.
And the patient's head should be on a cushion with their tears dried and the eyelids separated so that the eye is exposed. You may start with grasping from above or below the eye, but it is better to grasp two vessels in the upper eyelid, one at the outer end and the other at the inner end.
Grasp them with the middle and index fingers, then insert forceps underneath them. Hold them between the thumb and index finger and gently pull your hand until all vessels become visible. Cut them from the smaller lower end using scissors or a fine knife if you wish.
Tie the eyelid away from its corner and expose all vessels until they rise on the orbit without cutting the tarsus, for tears and blood will cloud your vision.
Then cut with the scissors what is beyond the margin above and below until nothing remains except at the canthus, if possible lift it from the canthus with a hook for it is better, but sometimes it may not be easy in some people.
And if you cut it, let its edges rest for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is alleviated, then resume work on it.
Chapter Nineteenth
In restoring the reed to the nose.
Physicians call the reed "nasa", if treated with cautery or a strong burning medicine as mentioned earlier, and it does not heal, there is no cure except to cut into the boil at its maturity and extract all the moisture or pus that is in it.
Until the bone becomes visible. If the bone becomes visible and you see decay or darkness in it, scrape it with this tool shaped like this:

Place it on the decayed part of the bone and rotate it between your fingers while slightly shaking your hand until you know that the decay has been removed.
Repeat this several times, then tighten the area with dry constricting drugs. If the wound heals and flesh grows on it, and the duration has passed without recurrence or inflammation occurring in it after forty days, then:

So that the patient feels wind coming out of it when he holds his hand over his nose. Then, apply dry constricting drugs to the wound as mentioned earlier. If flesh grows and the area solidifies, then the matter that was escaping outward will return into the nostril, making it easier for the patient, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty
On restoring prolapsed eyeball.
If the eye prolapses completely and there is no defect in sight or loss of vision, make the patient comfortable. Then separate him from the crowd and place a pillow behind his head without any restriction, then gently pull down the eyelids with tweezers.
Then you place on the eye a poultice made of onion, resin, frankincense and musk. Then you hold the eye from above onto the bandage with many benefits. The recipe for the benefits is that you take soft flaxseed and fold each cloth into four folds to make many of them.
Then you place one on top of the other onto what the eye needs then you tie it with a strong cloth tightly and repeat the cupping without any condition. Then remove the cupping and leave the eye tied for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as mentioned before. If you see that the eye has returned to its original state, otherwise repeat the bandaging, poultice and tightening until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One
On Cutting the Thread Vein.
If a vein in the layer of the eye protrudes and becomes visible through the eyelids like a grape, causing disfigurement to one's appearance, one should desire to cut it off. The procedure for this is as I will describe.
It is done by inserting a needle into the base of the vein from below upwards, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the eye and pulling it through. Leave the first needle in place.
Then cut the site where the doubled thread was inserted and tie some of the vein around the needle tightly, then remove the needle. Place a piece of wool moistened with egg white on the eye until the threads fall off along with the vein. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, the vein should be tied tightly from all sides with a piece of cloth to make the eye protrude more. Then use a fine probe to reach the bottom of the eye and drain the egg white moisture until it falls onto its original site and dries up. Then tie it tightly until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two
On the Treatment of Epilepsy
This condition known as epilepsy is when water-like drops gather in the eye without any cause. The procedure for this is for the patient to sit on an upright chair, then take their head in your hands from both sides.
Move it until you see the drop reach your eye, then fix it and do not let it go; light will be released and the patient will see objects as they were before. If it does not reach the bottom of the eye, know that it is water. If it does not settle as described,
Alternatively, seat the patient before you, then take a fine lancet and pierce it slightly above the corneal conjunctiva where it touches the limbus until matter comes out.
If it comes out, wash the eye with warm water mixed with husk or hot water with barley and husk, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three
We have mentioned the types of water in the previous sections and which one is suitable for irrigation with detailed explanation. So take it from there on health, then seat the patient facing you near a light source like sunlight.
And bind his sound eye and pull it strongly, then lift the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if it is the left eye or your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the cannula near the limbus with some force in the same direction as the white of the eye towards the inner canthus, then push the cannula strongly while rotating it until you feel that it has reached an empty space.
The cannula should penetrate to a depth equal to the distance from the sclera to the limbus of the eye. You can see the cannula through the cornea due to its clarity, then bring it up towards the location where the water is present.
Then press it down repeatedly. If water flows out immediately, the patient will see clearly and the cannula will remain in the eye. If water rises, push it back down without removing the cannula.
If it settles and does not rise, gently remove the cannula while twisting it slightly with your hand, then dissolve some pure salt in water and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then apply a compress or damp wool outside the eye.
The flesh is red and white, and a healthy eye is tied to it. However, we and the people of our time use ground cumin mixed with egg white instead. It is necessary to take the lancet called the Brid which has this shape:
Pierce only the skin of the inflamed area with it, do not probe deeply. Rather, lightly touch the opening with the scalpel and then insert the probe as mentioned earlier. Once you have completed your task, prepare a patient to lie down comfortably on his back in a dark room.
The patient should refrain from all movements and coughing, eat only soft foods that agree with his nature, not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage should remain in place until the third day, then it should be removed in that dark room and his vision tested.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage until the seventh day. It is not permissible to do this during treatment or immediately after probing; rather, avoid it before water rises rapidly due to intense looking.
If there is a hot swelling, do not remove the bandage before the seventh day. Rather, treat it with something that reduces the swelling until it subsides, then uncover his eyes and place a veil over his face to gradually accustom him to light while he remains in the dark room.
He should gradually leave the room and resume his activities. Know that the probe cannot be dispensed with without repeated observation. I have heard from some Iraqis that they use a hollow probe in Iraq through which water is sucked.

Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
There may grow in the nose different kinds of excess flesh, some resembling many-legged scorpions, others being hard and dark-colored cancerous growths, and still others being soft and not dark-colored.
If the flesh is neither tender nor cancerous, the patient should sit facing you towards the sun. Open their nostril and insert the probe into that flesh. Then pull it outwards and cut what was reached with a sharp, fine knife from one side only.
Know that all the flesh has been removed if there remains any part which you are unable to cut. Then scrape it gently with one of the gentle instruments until nothing remains. If blood gushes forth or a hot swelling appears, leave it.
Treat it as you would a malignant tumor. Quickly cauterize until bleeding stops and all flesh is destroyed. Then insert into the nostril water or liquid after cutting, ensuring the nostril opens and moisture reaches the throat.
Know that it is healed if moisture reaches as it should. If not, know that there remains hard flesh near the hollowed bones which the instrument could not reach. Then take a coarse cotton thread.
And tie many knots on it, keeping each knot about a finger's length or less. The patient should gently insert one end of the thread into their nostril using tweezers until it reaches the nasopharynx.
And bring out one end at the mouth and tie both ends together. Many children do this in schools, as it is easy for anyone who wishes to do so.
Then use the knots on the thread to scrape the flesh until you know that it has been cut by the knots. Then remove the thread and leave a wick soaked in Egyptian wax ointment in the nostril for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all remaining flesh, then insert a piece of lead tubing into the nostril until healed. If further treatment is needed to dry it out, use that method.
This is an illustration of the dropper used for instilling medicines into the nostrils:
A small open funnel made of silver or copper, like a lamp with an opening for its wick and channel.
If you wish, make the tube closed like a pipe and spread the linseed oil flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when heating oil or whatever liquid or solid substance you wish.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In the cases of warts on the tip of the nose.
It often happens that a wart grows at the tip of the nose, becoming large and inflamed over time until it becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is advisable to cut it off at its first appearance and excise all of it, then apply either cautery or the burning medication that acts as cautery. If cutting off was delayed until it became large, observe if it is hard, solid, and dark-colored with little sensitivity.
Do not apply the iron cautery to it, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen this tumor cut off only for great ulceration to remain on the patient. But if the tumor is soft to touch, not dark-colored, cutting it off is possible in all cases. So discharge the patient and cut off [the wart] without hesitation or delay, then treat the site with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Sewing the Nose, Lip, and Ear. If detachment occurs from a wound or something similar, know that in such cases, the chances of successful repair are slim except for some people.
It is advisable that when any of these cases appears, observe if the wound is fresh with blood. If so, sew up the wound and treat it until it heals. But if detachment has occurred and both edges are dry and each part is sound, then scrape off all parts with a thick skin layer until they bleed.
Then sew up the edges and pull them together, sprinkle with fine linen and powdered wax, apply a bandage of healing plaster over it, and leave it tied for two or three days before removing it.
And change the dressing and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves, then treat it with plaster until healed. The stitching method for joining detached edges is either with needles as described in abdominal suturing, or with thread as explained there.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In the extraction of warts that appear on the lips.
It often happens to many people that small, hard tumors resembling currants appear inside their cheeks; some are smaller and others larger. Therefore, you should turn the cheek and cut around each tumor, then attach it with forceps.
And cut around them on all sides, then fill the wound with powdered zinc until bleeding stops. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the wounds with a compress until they heal.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
On cutting excess flesh from the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which ancient physicians called 'apollis.' You should attach it with forceps or grasp it with tweezers and cut it at its base. Then let the time flow or the bleeding stop, then place powdered zinc on the wound.
Or one of the dry, tight bandages. If that flesh returns again after treatment, cut it off again and cauterize it, for it will not return afterward.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On scraping teeth with iron.
It often happens that rough, ugly scales accumulate on the surfaces of teeth both inside and outside between the gums. These can become blackened, yellowed, or greened to such an extent that decay reaches the gums and disfigures the teeth.
Therefore, have the patient sit before you with his head in your lap. Scrape off the tartar and scales from the tooth that appears rough like sand until nothing remains. Do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other impurities until it is clean.
If the first scraping does not remove all impurities, repeat it on another day and a third time until you achieve your goal. Know that molar teeth require different shapes of scrapers according to what is needed for your work.
For the scraper used to scrape inside a molar tooth is not the same as that used for outside, and one used for between molars has another shape. Have all these different shapes of scrapers ready at hand:
Chapter Thirty
On Tooth Extraction.
It is preferable to treat the tooth from its pain with all possible means and delay extraction as it has no substitute behind if extracted, for it is a noble organ; therefore, extraction should be resorted to only when unavoidable.
If the patient decides on extraction, make sure that you are able to clearly see and identify the painful tooth. Frequently, the patient may be mistaken about the source of pain and think that it is in a tooth other than the actual cause. They may have a healthy tooth extracted without relief from symptoms, as we have seen many barbers perform this error repeatedly.
Once you have correctly identified the painful tooth, you should then circumcise around it with a sharp knife, cutting through the gums on all sides until you can loosen and move the tooth slightly with your fingers or gentle forceps.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps and stabilize the patient's head between your knees. Gently pull the tooth straight out without breaking it; if it does not come out easily, use one of the instruments to gently push it from different sides until it loosens and can be extracted.
However, if the tooth is decayed or root canal treated, it is necessary to pack the cavity with a gauze and plug it securely using a thin, rounded end of a probe before grasping it with forceps. Additionally, make sure to circumcise around the gums again on all sides afterwards.
Be cautious not to break the tooth while extracting, as this would leave a part of it in the patient's mouth, causing more trouble than the original pain. Avoid performing extraction recklessly like unskilled barbers who act hastily without following these instructions.
Many disasters happen due to the negligence of untrained practitioners, the most common being breaking the tooth and leaving some or all of its roots inside the patient's mouth. Some may also extract along with part of the jawbone, or cause bleeding after extraction. If bleeding occurs, apply pressure to the area and crush some gum tragacanth to pack into the wound.
If not, then cauterize it if the remedy does not help. The first kind of forceps should have long legs and a short handle to prevent bending while gripping the tooth.
The handle should be thick so that it does not slip from your grasp or bend. It should be made of Indian iron or steel, with well-smithied jaws that fit closely together for a firm grip.
The legs can also be made like an awl to make it strong and steadfast.
