Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the removal of impacted teeth.
If a tooth has grown not in its natural course, it has ruined the figure, especially if this happens to women or children. It should not be left unattended if the tooth has erupted from behind another tooth.
And if it cannot be extracted nor pushed back, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this tool that looks like this:
This tool resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian iron. Make many cuts over several days to break the hardness of the tooth without disturbing other teeth. But if it is protruding and movable, push it back using this tool that looks like this:
It is made entirely of iron, with a very thin edge like that of a needle made by this tool. Warm the tooth gently for many days without disturbing it until it falls out, then remove its roots using a forceps.
If a tooth has broken off some of its part causing pain to the tongue while speaking, it should also be polished until its roughness is gone and becomes smooth so that it does not hurt the tongue or interfere with speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth are loose due to an injury or fall and cause difficulty in chewing food without falling out, even after treatment with tightening medications.
The solution is to bind them with a silver or gold thread, with gold being preferred as silver tarnishes and decays over time. The thread should be of medium thickness, neither too thin nor too thick to fit between the teeth.
It is an image of intertwining that you take the thread and insert its twist between the two sound teeth, then sew with one end of the thread between the loose teeth until you reach the sewing to the sound tooth from the other side.
Then you repeat the sewing to the side where you started from and pull gently with wisdom so that it does not move at all, and your pulling of the thread should be at the roots of the teeth lest it slip away, then you cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors and gather them together and twist them with tweezers and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose one.
Lest the tongue be harmed, then you leave it like this tied until it lasts. If it loosens or its tension breaks, tighten it with another thread thus forever. And this is an image of the teeth and their configuration in intertwining between two sound teeth and two loose ones as you see:
And the single tooth or both may return to their place after falling and intertwine as described and remain. Indeed, this is done by a skilled craftsman, and he may carve bone from cow bones to make a shape like the tooth and place it in the spot where the tooth went missing. And he pulls it as we said, so that it remains and enjoys it,
Chapter Forty-three.
In cutting the frenum that appears under the tongue preventing speech.
This frenum that appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which man is born, or accidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure is that you open the patient's mouth and his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut this frenum crosswise until the tongue is released from its hold. If there is some stiffness and complexity and it was due to the healing of a wound, make an incision with a scalpel crosswise until the frenum is severed and the ties are dissolved.
And beware that the incision is not too deep lest you cut a vein there causing bleeding. Then rinse with rosewater or vinegar and cold water after the cutting, then place under the tongue a wick of cotton held by the patient every night.
To prevent it adhering again, if bleeding occurs, apply powdered gum arabic to the spot. If blood prevails, cauterize the place with a glass cup suitable for that purpose. Then treat him with all available remedies until he recovers.
Chapter Thirty-Five
On expelling the tongue-eating louse.
Sometimes under the tongue, there appears a tumor resembling a small toad that prevents the tongue from its natural function and may even fill up the mouth. To treat this, open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the tumor.
If it appears reddish or black and hard, not causing the patient pain, then do not treat it for it is cancer. But if it tends towards whiteness with moisture, pierce it with a probe and cut it gently with a fine scalpel on all sides.
If bleeding overtakes you during the procedure, apply powdered gum arabic until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until it is completely removed. Then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat him with all appropriate remedies until he recovers.
Chapter Thirty-Six
On treating tonsillar swelling.
And any tumors that grow in the throat, including those similar to the tonsils that appear inside the throat, if treated with the methods mentioned earlier but do not heal
If the tumor is hard, dark red and not painful, do not treat it with iron. If it is red and its origin is thick, also do not treat it with iron for fear of bleeding. Leave it until it ripens, then either bursting spontaneously or incise it.
If it is white, rounded and its origin is thin, this is what should be cut. First look before operating to see if the heat of the tumor has completely subsided or somewhat decreased.
Then seat the patient facing the sun with his head in your lap, open his mouth and hold a spatula between your hands like this shape:
Make a spatula from silver or copper, thin like a blade. Use it to press down the tongue and locate the tumor. Then take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, gently pulling it out as much as possible without tearing any tissues.
Then she is cut with this instrument, whose shape resembles scissors but whose ends are bent inward so that each of its two sharp blades lies close to the other. This instrument should be made from Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with these scissors whose shape is like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other, so that after cutting one gland, cut the other in exactly the same manner. Then, after cutting, rinse the wound with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, rinse with water in which pomegranate peels or fig leaves have been soaked until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals. Sometimes other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut these as mentioned for cutting the tonsils.
A woman was treated for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, pressing on the soft palate and almost blocking the throat. She could barely breathe through a narrow passage and it prevented her from eating or drinking.
She was close to death and would not have lasted another day or two. The tumor had grown until two projections emerged from her nostrils, so she acted quickly and inserted a hook into one of them and pulled it out, removing a satisfactory piece.
Then she cut where it was reached from the nostril, and did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. She opened her mouth, held down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat cleared immediately, and she began to drink water right away. She also started eating and gained strength gradually. We continued to cut off parts of the tumor for a long time, but it kept growing back in place of what was removed.
She burned the tumor inside her throat with cauterization, which stopped its growth. Then she traveled away from that place and I do not know what happened to her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On Cutting a Tumor on the Tongue
If it is called a uvula when it droops down to the throat and swells, being white and rectangular, it is called an 'axis.' But if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a 'uvula.' When treated according to what was mentioned in the division [of diseases], yet the treatment did not succeed.
I saw that the hot tumor had subsided and was thin, so it should not be cut. If it was gathered together, round but without length; if it was bloody or blackish in color or insensitive to touch, then it should be cut.
So, in this case, the patient should sit facing the sun and press his tongue with the instrument mentioned earlier. Then insert the needle into the uvula.
Pull it down and cut it with one of the instruments mentioned earlier for cutting tonsils. Do not cut more than necessary, as excessive cutting will harm speech.
After cutting, use the treatment mentioned for tonsillectomy until it heals. If the patient is hesitant to cut it, do not use any trick without caution; the only way is with a sharp medicine: put the patient's head in a sack.
Then press the tongue with the instrument mentioned earlier. Take some of the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with uncooked flour to make a paste neither too thick nor too thin, and fill this instrument which looks like this:
The tip of the instrument for applying the medicine is like a spoon's cavity. Place it on the uvula with the patient lying on his side so that saliva does not flow back into his throat.
Hold the medicine in your hand and squeeze it onto the uvula for half an hour until it appears blackened and the burning sensation subsides. If you wish, dip a cotton swab in the medicine and apply it with a spoon.
Insert the spoon with the cotton swab into a tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Repeat this process until you achieve the desired cauterization. Leave it for three or four days, after which it will dry and fall off. If more medicine is needed, prepare it again.
After cauterization, wipe around the navel with a linen soaked in oil and dab around it to remove any medication, then rinse with cold water. Treat externally with bandages and internally with irrigation until it heals; this method is safer than cutting and far from frightening.
The tongue may also be treated gently without cauterization or cutting. It is treated with incense prepared in this manner: take equal amounts of fennel, saffron, savory, sage, chicory, and other similar herbs, grind them together into a powder, then mix it with vinegar and use for fumigation.
And heat the vessel over a gentle fire, using bellows to fan the flame. The cover of the pot should have a hole in its center onto which is placed the condensing tube made of silver or brass:.
Insert the end of the tube containing the burning charcoal into the patient's mouth such that the steam reaches the tongue through the tube until it becomes moistened, then repeat this process several times until the tumor subsides; but beware of applying this treatment at the very onset of the tumor.
For this method often increases the swelling; it is preferable to use it when the hot tumor has subsided. If you do not have this apparatus, take a reed and insert its end into the mouth, covering it with an eggshell at the tip to prevent burning of the patient's mouth.
Because the eggshell prevents the steam from burning the mouth; this is a good and safe method.
Chapter Eighty-Three
On expelling splinters and other foreign bodies lodged in the throat.
It often happens that a bone, fish spine or other object becomes lodged in the throat; one should remove what is visible by pressing the tongue down with an instrument held towards the sun.
Then examine the throat to see what is hidden there. If you cannot see it, do not make the patient vomit before his food has digested in his stomach, for the lodged object may be expelled by vomiting or the patient may swallow a piece of cloth or a stem of leek.
Or the patient may swallow a dry piece of bread or a piece of soft sea sponge tied to a thread, which he should then swallow; once it reaches the point of the spine, quickly pull out the string, repeating this several times.
Frequently the splinter becomes embedded or the bone comes out; if it does not come out as mentioned, then use an iron tool of this shape:
This tool should be slightly thicker than a straw and have a hook at its end, which the patient inserts gently into their throat while tilting their head back to avoid touching the windpipe and causing coughing. The doctor may also insert it with his hand,
Having the patient insert it is preferable, as they know better where the affected area is located. They can push it down or pull it up gently according to what feels comfortable until the splinter comes out,
Chapter Thirty-Nine
On removing a bone or splinter stuck in the throat.
If treating with medications as mentioned earlier does not help, then look into the patient's throat at sunrise after depressing their tongue with the instrument described above.
If you see the splinter, then extract it with a small forceps or gentle tweezers. If you cannot grasp it, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the splinter. Then insert a red-hot iron into the tube.
Repeat this several times, with the patient enduring thirst throughout the day. Then give them a mouthwash of cold water without swallowing any of it, and move the water around in their mouth occasionally with their hand.
If the splinter does not come out, fumigate the throat with bull's urine or camphor using the instrument described earlier. Repeat this several times until it falls out.
The procedure for fumigation with incense involves taking a small amount of burning embers in a covered container with a hole in its center. Insert the tip of the instrument through this hole, then place the incense and cover the patient's mouth with one end of the tube to ensure that no smoke escapes until they feel the incense reaching their throat.
If the splinter does not fall out, repeat the fumigation several times while the patient endures thirst and consumes salt and garlic but avoids drinking water. It must come out this way. Here is an image of an instrument used to extract the splinter from the throat if it can be seen:
This is similar to a crab's claws, as you see, except that it has this curved end that enters the throat and its tips resemble a bird's beak; when it grips something, it does not let it go.
And another instrument with which the tongue is pressed down, it being a wide iron ring fitting the mouth above the tongue; when placed in the mouth standing on its side, the mouth remains open unwillingly and the patient cannot remove it at all until it is taken away.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth precisely in this manner.
Chapter Forty.
In it, he speaks about speaking concerning (the nature of) tumors and their dissection.
The types of tumors are many and varied, as mentioned one by one in this book. They differ from each other with regard to two aspects: the nature of the tumor itself and what it contains of fluids.
The second type differs according to the locations where they occur in the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is not like one occurring in the buttocks, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy part is not like one occurring in a joint; each has its own ruling regarding treatment.
Among tumors, some should not be dissected until the pus in them has ripened and become complete. Others should be dissected while they are still fresh and have not fully ripened, such as those that occur near joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its duration is prolonged until it becomes corrupt around it, it may spoil the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing lameness in that limb. Or the tumor may be near an important organ.
For if you delay dissecting it until it ripens, that would harm the important organ nearby, or if it is near the buttocks, then it should be dissected while still fresh and unripe. However, a tumor that is near the buttocks must be dissected even if it is not completely ripe, so that the depths do not become putrid and penetrate into the intestines, causing perforation.
It is preferable to know the appropriate time for dissecting tumors that have fully ripened, which is when the tumor is at rest and free of pain, fever has subsided, redness and swelling around it have decreased, and the head of the tumor has become soft.
It is necessary to tie off the artery at a lower point from the tumor, if possible, so that it may be easier for the blood to flow downwards or at the thinnest part of the tumor and most prominent vein. Let the artery run along the length of the body if the tumors are near the hands or feet.
And in smooth places where there is no bend, the muscles, tendons, nerves and blood vessels are located. In places that bend, follow the artery accordingly. If the tumor is in fatty places, it is better to leave the artery alone.
Until the abscess is completely dried out, as we have mentioned. If you tie off its artery beforehand, copious amounts of pus will flow from it, causing much harm and filth, and perhaps its lips will become hard or its opening narrow. Some tumors may be tied off across the body when necessary or according to what the organ requires.
It is necessary to use a single tie for small tumors, and a wide tie or many incisions according to the size of large tumors. Some tumors may require excising the skin and cutting if it has become like rags.
And sometimes the skin may die, as seen in many books and manuscripts. Some tumors require an incision with three angles, while others are cut like the shape of an oak leaf or a scrotal tumor.
And some tumors require a round or crescent-shaped incision and similar ones. For tumors that have no head, like flat smooth tumors, it is necessary to tie off with a simple tie only. If the tumor is very large and has collected much matter, do not tie off suddenly so that all the pus does not come out at once.
Instead, remove some of it first, then tie off the tumor for another day, and remove some pus again. Repeat this process gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, pregnant, a child or an elderly person, for the vital spirit often dissipates with the sudden release of pus, and the patient may die while you are unaware. Therefore, be very cautious in this matter.
After removing these tumors, it is necessary to cleanse the wound and examine it. If the tumor's incision is small or if there is only one simple fissure, then use bandaging made from cotton or linen. However, if the tumor is large and the abdomen has many wounds.
It is necessary to insert a wick into every fissure until they reach each other. If some of the skin or flesh of the tumor has been cut off, it should be stuffed with dry cotton wool or linen bandage and tightened for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar soaked in a linen cloth and apply it to the site of the bleeding repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, then you should use the procedures and treatments and dressings that we have described in many places throughout this book and its divisions. If it is wintertime and the site of the tumor has many veins, then warm honey and oil should be applied.
Apply it to the site, but if it is summertime and the places are fleshy, then you should bring the poultice soaked in cold water and oil or vinegar and oil, all of which should be cold until on the third day as we said earlier, you should remove the tumor and cleanse it.
And use in its treatment something similar until it heals. This is all you need to know about the general treatment of tumors, and regarding the detailed method we have mentioned for each tumor how it should be treated concisely.
Chapter Forty-One.
In the fissure on tumors that appear in the scalp.
There appear on the scalp small tumors which are of the type known as 'salves' and are enclosed in sacs, having various shapes like that of a chicken egg. There are many types of these, some fatty and others containing moisture resembling mud.
And some contain moisture like dropsy or soup, and others are hard and solid. None of these pose any danger when incised and removed unless a vein is encountered during the incision.
To operate on it, first probe it with the tool that comes next, called the 'Al-Midas', until you know what is inside. If it contains fluid, then make an incision along its length as shown here.
Begin the incision from point ~b~ to point ~g~. Once the fluid is drained, remove and discard the entire pouch that contained it. Leave nothing behind, for remnants often recur.
Soak a bandage in powdered myrrh if available, or salt water. Fill the wound and leave it overnight to dissolve any remaining pouch. Repeat with the bandage twice more if necessary.
Until you ensure that no pouch remains, then treat the wound with myrrh until it heals. If the tumor contains fatty tissue, incise as shown here:
Make a cross-incision and release the adhesions on both sides of the wound. Remove it entirely from all directions, exerting effort to extract the omentum that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, perform as described later. Also, do this if the tumor is adherent to the incision.
And this is the treatment for what was mentioned. Incising a adherent tumor is easier as it has less blood and fluid. Some head tumors may have no fluid at all, as in the case of:
An old woman's head tumor. I found it like hard stone, rough and white. It could not be broken despite trying to crush it. Other acute head tumors are different, such as those found in children's heads.
If at the root of the ears, incise all of them simply and make their bottoms slanted to facilitate discharge of matter downwards. Then treat them according to what is suitable.
Chapter Forty-two.
In incisions on pigs, these tumors often appear in the neck, armpits, and groins. They can also appear elsewhere on the body, singly or in multiples, sometimes merging with one another.
Every pig, whether it is among the liver or tumors of the head as we have described, has its own unique sac. There are many types of these pigs, some hard and others containing fluids, while still others are harmful and do not respond to treatment.
I have seen some with a good condition upon touch, appearing close in color to skin, moving towards all sides without being attached by nerve or vein nor artery nor deeply embedded. It is necessary to cut it with a simple incision from top to bottom of the body in this manner from line ~g~ to line ~b~.
And peel it off from all sides, stretching the skin with forceps or two or many if needed as we described for tumors of the head. Remove it little by little without cutting any vein or nerve, using a not-too-sharp scalpel.
Lest your hand slip and cause unnecessary cutting or disturb the patient, if you cut a vein or artery, preventing you from proceeding, then apply powdered tragacanth or some cauterizing material to stop the bleeding.
Tie the wound and leave it until the inflammation of the tumor subsides, the wound settles and begins to heal. When bleeding stops, return to your work until finished. Then examine with your index finger if there are any remaining small pigs; remove them and clean them out.
If there is a large vein at the root of the pig, do not cut it from its origin but tie it with a double tight thread and leave it until it falls off naturally without harm. Then fill the wound with boiled cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment and treat it.
But if all the pigs are cut, then bring together the edges of the wound and sew it immediately after ensuring that no fragment remains. If you find that a bone fragment is left behind
Then seek to cut its origin and base using sutures and cauterization as mentioned earlier. As for pigs containing fluids, tie them off with a simple ligature at the point of maturity appearing above, making the ligature below as we have said.
Then, after the bone, use the Egyptian ointment and similar ones to eat away what remains of the corruption until you see that the wound has healed. Then treat it with ointments suitable for flesh until it heals completely.
Chapter Forty-Three
In cutting the throat due to a swelling occurring inside the throat
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat but I have not seen anyone in our land perform it, and this is their opinion. However, those with suffocation should avoid cutting the throat as they do not benefit from it.
In order for all the veins and lungs to be clear, but those with a hot swelling in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no cause in the trachea, it is necessary to perform the throat incision to escape the harm that comes from suffocation.
If we want to do that, we should cut the throat below three or four circles of the trachea, making a narrow incision like the one in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage at this spot.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is hesitant, he should extend the skin of the neck with a probe then cut the skin until he reaches the trachea avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then cut the peritoneum we described, and identify the trachea by the mucus coming out of it along with what bubbles up and the loss of voice. Leave the wound open for some time until the time when suffocation was feared has passed. Then bring together the edges of the wound from the skin.
Then cut it alone without the cartilage, and then use medications that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book said: "This passage we quoted means only if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these swellings mentioned.
When the patient is near death and feels suffocation, they resort to cutting the throat so that the patient can breathe on the wound spot some breaths and be saved from death. They were commanded to leave the wound open until the course of the disease passes, which takes three days or more.
Then they ordered him to stitch the wound and treat it until it healed. I myself witnessed a servant taking my sword and stabbing his throat, severing some of the windpipe. He was brought to me for treatment, but he expired like a slaughtered animal. So I opened the wound.
I found the blood issuing from the wound slowly, so I knew it was not an artery or vein that had been severed. Wind was escaping from the wound, so I cleansed it and sutured it, treating it until it healed. Nothing else happened to the servant except a slight hoarseness of voice.
And after some days she returned to her former state, hence we say that cutting the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter Forty-Four.
Regarding the incision made on the tumor that appears externally in the neck, which is called the 'goiter of the neck.'
This tumor called the 'goiter of the neck' is a large tumor the color of the skin, and it is common among women. It has two types:
Either it is natural or acquired. As for the natural one, there is no remedy for it, but the acquired one has two types: one resembles fat tissue, and the other resembles a tumor caused by an obstruction of the artery. In its incision, there is danger: It is not permissible to operate on it with iron unless it is small, provided that it has been examined and probed with the probe to find that it resembles fat tissue and is not attached to any vessels. Then it should be incised like fatty tumors. And extract it along with its contents if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it and treat the site as necessary.
Chapter Forty-Five.
Regarding Incision on Types of Tumors:
Tumors are many in kind, and all their types have been mentioned in the division. Here we must explain the difference between a tumor (salwa) and an abscess (khuraj), for it is confusing. I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and intense pain until the swelling subsides. The ulceration is completed, and thus the fever and inflammation cease. The condition does not come with heat or fever nor pain, and it contains a peritoneal pouch that is peculiar to it, matching the color of the body. Its beginning resembles measles but becomes like a boil, sometimes larger, sometimes smaller.
There are two types of this condition: fatty or containing moisture. If there is moisture, its colors are many as mentioned in the previous sections. When treating an ulceration, it is necessary to probe and examine it first using the tool called the 'al-Midas', as shown in the following chapter.
Measure the size of the tumor, then remove the 'al-Midas' and observe what comes out. If moist discharge of any color appears, make a simple incision as mentioned for other tumors. But if no moisture appears upon removing the 'al-Midas', know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-shaped incision on the ulceration as I have shown you. Suspend it with forceps, gently tear the skin from all sides, and if possible, preserve the pouch along with the ulceration.
If the pouch tears during the procedure and cannot be removed intact, cut it out piece by piece until none remains. Otherwise, if any part is left behind, the ulceration will likely recur.
If you are unable to remove all of it, cauterize the wound with some sharp caustic material upon completion. Suture the wound and apply something cold to reduce inflammation. Treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the ulceration is large, sew its edges together using adhesive materials.
If bleeding occurs, either profuse or otherwise, quickly cauterize the area with powdered pitch. Leave it for two to three days until the wound becomes inflamed and bleeding stops, then proceed to remove any remaining ulceration.

From line ~b~ to line ~j~ Then excise all the fat, then fill the wound with the fatty ointment, then join the edges of the wound by suturing and treat it until it heals. If the breast droops downward and relaxes on its bone as it appears in women.

Chapter Forty-Nine
In incising a tumor that appears due to an artery or vein, known as an aneurysm.
If the artery is injured and the skin above it is torn off, a tumor often appears from this. Similarly, an aneurysm may also appear in veins.
The signs by which it is known whether a tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it will be rectangular and congested towards the depth of the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you will feel as if it has a creaking sound.
If it is from a vein, the tumor will be round and protruding on the surface of the body; operating on these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body. It is advisable not to neglect their treatment with iron.
And those in the extremities or head should also not be neglected; if it is a pulsating swelling of an artery, make a longitudinal incision on the skin over it with a scalpel, then open the wound with scissors and free the artery from its surrounding tissues.
Until it is fully exposed, then insert a needle underneath it and pass it to the other side. Tie the artery with a doubled thread at two points as shown in the illustration of the arteries in the ears, then cut with scissors at the point between the ligatures until all blood flows out.
The tumor will dissolve and then use the treatment that produces pus until the slough falls off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a lacerated vein, grasp as much of it with the skin as possible in your hand, then insert a needle underneath this point and pass it through with a doubled thread until it exits on the other side. Then tie it securely with the thread as described for tying a grape, tying both ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads may slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the point where the first needle crosses.
Pull the threads at four points, then cut open the tumor in the middle until its contents come out. Cut off a piece of skin and leave the rest tied up, then apply a dressing soaked in wine and oil. Then use treatment with poultices and ointments until it heals,
Chapter Fifty
On the tumor that appears due to nerve torsion.
Similarly, a tumor may appear in the artery or vein due to injury or excessive fatigue; however, this is more common in the elbows and knees.
In every joint where movement occurs, it is a movable tumor resembling the color of other body parts and is usually painless. However, when pressed firmly, the patient may feel numbness similar to that caused by pressure. This type of tumor does not collect deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in all directions except towards the front or back. Therefore, tumors present in joints should not be treated with cautery as this may cause scarring. Tumors on the head or forehead are an exception.
Incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it off at its origin. If it is large, tie it off with thread and then excise it. Sew up the wound and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One
On the amputation of umbilical hernias.
Many people may develop umbilical hernias, also known as congenital hernias, which resemble mushrooms due to their thin stalk and thick head. Some of these hernias are small while others can become quite large.
I have seen a man with two hernias in his abdomen, each resembling mushrooms. They had thin, white bases that had ulcerated and were constantly wet with moisture. I removed them, and one weighed about eighteen ounces while the other weighed about six.
To operate on them, examine the patient. If they are moist and have a white, wet appearance with thin bases, cut them off using a wide scalpel. Have cautery ready nearby as much bleeding may occur during removal.
If bleeding is profuse, cauterize the wound. If the patient is frightened by the use of cautery, take a strong lead thread and tie it around the hernia with such force that it begins to strangulate. Leave it for two days, then tighten the thread further until it completely cuts off blood supply to the hernia.
Whenever a mole is affected until it breaks off and falls off by itself without any pain, if the mole has a thick origin, there may be some fear and hesitation in cutting it off, especially those located in the abdomen. However, it can still be cut halfway or partially.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of attempting to cut a mole that is dark-colored, insensitive, and appears swollen as it is a cancerous tumor. We will mention more about cancer later on.
Chapter Fifty-Two
In treating anal fistulas.
Anal fistulas have many causes, either from a tear in the peritoneum on the abdomen that protrudes through it, or from the intestines as seen in other fistulas, or from blood flowing from a vein or artery as mentioned earlier, or from wind that becomes trapped within.
If it is due to a tear in the peritoneum with the protrusion of the hernia, then the color of the tumor will resemble that of the skin and will be soft without pain. It will appear different depending on its position. And if it is due to the intestines protruding, its position will differ greatly from what has been described. If you press on it with your fingers, it disappears but then reappears. It may also make a gurgling sound and become more prominent during exertion or bathing. If it is caused by moisture, it will feel soft.
If it is caused by blood, the tumor will appear dark in color along with these signs. If it is due to flesh that has grown out of place, the tumor will be firm and solid and will remain at a constant size.
If it is caused by wind, it will feel soft to the touch. If the anal fistula is due to blood from an artery or vein, or wind, you should not hesitate to treat it, for there is fear and hesitation in this matter as I have told you earlier in the chapter that mentions tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the anal fistula is due to the intestines or a hernia, you should order the patient to stand upright with their legs apart and then mark around the entire fistula with ink. Then command him to lie on his back before you, then make a wide incision around the tumor with a scalpel at the point marked with ink, then insert a large probe into the center of the tumor upwards, then bind the site of the incision tightly with a strong silk thread.
Then tie the ligature in a knot, then open the center of the taut tumor above the ligament and insert your index finger inside it, and seek out the bowel. If you find that the bowel has been taken by the ligament, loosen the knot and push the bowel back into the abdomen. But if you find a diverticulum, dilate it with a probe.
Then cut off its stalk, but if you encounter an artery or vein, ligate it neatly and return to your task. Then take two needles and insert into them two strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision made around the tumor in a crosswise manner, having passed through them. Then pull the tumor at four points with the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the site until the adherent flesh rots and falls off by itself, or cut it off if it has softened and rotted. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals, if the tumor is a fibroid attached to flesh.
Or if it is from moisture, then cauterize the tumor as mentioned and remove any blood or moisture found therein, then treat it with what heals the site.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the Treatment of Cancer.
We have mentioned in the section on types of cancer and how they can be treated with medications, and the warning against treating them with iron lest they become ulcerated. We have also mentioned cancer occurring in the uterus and the warning against its treatment.
The ancients mentioned that when cancer is in a location where it can be completely excised, such as in the breast or thigh and similar organs that are suitable for its complete removal, especially if it is small and newly developed, then it should be approached. But if it is large and has spread, then it should not be touched.
To the extent that you are able to cure one person from it, and I have not seen anyone before me who has reached this goal. The procedure for it when it is suitable as mentioned is to advance cautiously and remove the black matter several times, then dissect it if it is in the vessels filled with blood.
Then the patient is seated in a chair that allows him to work, then he receives in his tumor the curettes suitable for it, then scrape from every side with the skin upon thorough examination so that nothing of its origin remains, and let the blood flow without cutting quickly.
Squeeze the places and draw out all thick blood with your hand or whatever tools you have at your disposal. If profuse bleeding occurs during the procedure due to cutting an artery or vein, ligate the vessel until bleeding stops, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Five

It is not advisable also for the cut to be on the side that the patient wants to lie down on lest waste flows towards that weak place, then the skin is entirely pierced with the tool and the tool is inserted into that cut and your hand is raised.

Due to the skin that holds the hole on the peritoneum which I told you to stitch up in that manner, then reuse the tool another day if you see the patient improving.

Chapter Fifty-Five
In treating children born with unperforated or constricted urinary passage, or with the opening in an abnormal location.

Chapter Fifty-Six
On bubbles appearing in the foreskin, navel, scrotum, and their union and adhesion to one another.

Chapter Seventy-Five
On the purification of boys and treatment of what afflicts them.
Circumcision is merely a separation of connection like other surgeries, except that it is done intentionally and is especially used on boys. Therefore, we must outline the best method for it.
The easiest way leading to safety is what I say: The ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not practiced among them; rather, it is something we have adopted.
By experiment, I found that the majority of craftsmen and surgeons use cautery with a hot iron or scissors, and they use bandages and sutures made from thread or cut with fingernails. I have tested all these methods.
I found no better method than cautery with scissors and suturing with thread, for cauterizing with a hot iron often harms the skin because the skin has two layers, so one may cut the upper layer while leaving the lower layer intact.
Thus, one must cut again causing renewed pain. Cauterizing with a file does not ensure cutting the tip of the glans because it may enter its hole. Using fingernails might tear the skin and ruin your work, especially if the skin is thin.
Many are born thus and do not require cautery. I have seen this. As for cauterizing with scissors and suturing with thread, experience has shown me its superiority because the upper blade is equal to the lower blade in cutting.
So when you press both blades of the scissors together evenly and at once, the thread becomes like a wall surrounding the glans skin on all sides without any error. Initially, it may seem difficult.
Especially since it may seem to someone with little understanding that you are merely tying a thread around the glans and leaving it until the next day, then removing it and sewing it up however you can. Then, he stands before you with his body upright.

Allow some blood to flow, as this is beneficial and reduces inflammation of the glans. Then wipe it with a wet cloth and sprinkle on it powdered burnt mallow seeds or fine barley flour; these are the best remedies tried or found effective.

Chapter Eighty-Five
On treating retained urine in the bladder.
Retained urine in the bladder occurs due to obstruction by a stone, blood clot, pus, or growth of flesh etc. When treated with the remedies mentioned in their respective sections,
and the urine does not flow, and you find that its retention is due to a stone lodged in the neck of the bladder, make the patient kneel down facing the ground, then have another person mount his back, pulling his abdomen backwards. The patient should hold himself steady as much as possible.

It is made of silver, smooth and thin like a bird feather quill, slightly bent, about one and a half spans long. It has a gentle funnel at its head; take a doubled thread, tie it securely to the tip with cotton or wool.
Insert the thread's tip into the catheter, trim any excess cotton with scissors to fit tightly like a cork. Then grease the catheter with oil, soap foam, or egg white and have the patient sit on a chair.
The patient empties their bladder using warm water or oil, then insert the catheter gently into the urethra until it reaches the bladder neck. Bend the catheter upwards towards the scrotum, then advance it inside.
Once the urethra is bent, push the catheter further until it reaches near the bladder. Lower the penis and insert the catheter into the bladder; you'll feel it enter an empty space.
This is done because the urinary passage has a bend. Then pull the thread with cotton gently to draw urine towards it, remove the catheter and let urine flow freely.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.

Chapter Fifty-Five.

The occurrence of this aneurysm results from an incision that appears on the extended peritoneum over the abdomen in the region of the kidneys, causing the gut to herniate into one of the twins.

However, if it is due to a tear in the peritoneum, it occurs suddenly and causes severe pain abruptly. In this case, the hernia appears as a difference under the skin near by.

Chapter Ninety-Six
In Castration
Castration is forbidden in our religion/sharia.
Therefore, it was not appropriate for me to include this in my book, except that I mention it for two reasons: first, so that it would be in the knowledge of the physician if asked about it, and secondly, because we often need to castrate some animals for our benefits.
The castration of animals such as horses, cattle, cats, and the like is done in two ways: either by cutting or by crushing and severing. The method of castration by crushing is performed by seating the animal in hot water until its genitalia relax and soften and droop downwards.
Then crush it with your hand until it becomes soft and not distinguishable to the touch. As for castration by cutting, one should hold the animal tightly and squeeze the scrotum with the left hand, then tie the spermatic cords and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles come out, then cut them off after peeling them and do not leave any peritoneum on them except for the thin peritoneum that is on the vessels. This method of castration by cutting is better than that by crushing.
Because perhaps something remained of the twins, so the animal desired mating, then the wound was treated until it healed.
The Seventieth Chapter.
In treating hermaphrodites.
A hermaphrodite in men appears in two types: one that is evident externally or within the scrotum between the testicles, resembling a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum.
And as for women, there is one type that appears above the vulva on the pubis like a small penis of a man protruding externally, and another like female genitals. The treatment for these three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women should have those superfluous flesh cut until there is no trace of it left, then treat it like other wounds until it heals. But for the second type in men which emits urine from within the scrotum, there is nothing to be done nor any hope.

The Seventy-Second Chapter.
On treating hemorrhoids.
Hemorrhoids are when a woman's vagina is not open or the opening is small, either naturally at birth or acquired later due to certain causes such as excess flesh growing there.
This is a thin or thick membrane, which may be found either in the depth of the womb, or on its sides, or above it, or below it. It prevents coitus and pregnancy, and hinders delivery; sometimes it may obstruct menstruation. Its presence can be determined by inspection from the midwife.
If the septum is visible and close, then if it is not visible, search for it with your fingers or a probe. If the septum is made of thin peritoneum and is close to the cervix, immediately puncture it, pressing both thumbs on the cervix as if making a pout.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread, then she pushes the cervix forcefully until the thin peritoneum ruptures and the septum opens. Then take some wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the site to prevent adhesion. The woman should engage in coitus daily to prevent the site from adhering again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, do not cut it with a broad knife like an almond leaf. If the septum is made of flesh, cauterize it with caustic substances and remove it. Keep with you drugs that stop bleeding without causing pain, such as senna, silphium, and honey mixed with egg whites.
Then use a wide lead pipe to prevent rapid adhesion of the wound. Hold it in place with bandages made of dry linen, and treat it with other medications for healing until it heals. Sometimes flesh may grow again in the uterus, do not cut it according to this description.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, no iron should be used for a cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
In the treatment of piles, warts, and red pimples that appear on women's genitals.
As for piles, they are the swelling of the veins' openings until much blood flows from them constantly. When piles become hard and lumpy, they become warts. The types and signs of piles have been mentioned in the previous sections, and we will mention them here as well.
Tumors and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and not visible to touch, cannot be treated with iron; those that can be touched at the mouth of the uterus are treatable.
After a woman enters a cold house, she should stretch out the warts with a needle or coarse cloth and cut them off at their roots. Then she should apply one of the bleeding remedies without causing pain.
Like qaciyya, shi'an, and butter. Then raise her legs towards the wall for a moment before lowering them into cold water. If bleeding persists, seat her in a mixture of samak herb, pomegranate husks, and figs. Mix barley flour with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back until the bleeding stops; if not, press hard on her backbone and breasts. Then take cotton wool, dip it in a decoction of tarragon or coltsfoot leaves, or olive branch sap with fig syrup, and apply it to the wound.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for red boils, they resemble acne heads and should not be cut like warts; treat them until they heal.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the polypus that appears in the uterus.
Many types of tumors appear in the uterus, such as cancer, hardened growths, ulcers, fistulas, eating ulcers, warts, cracks, and hot tumors. All these diseases and their types and symptoms have been mentioned previously.
It is not necessary to mention in this chapter the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, especially if it collects for a long time. If its pain at the beginning is severe with two blows and inflammation and fever.
If it appears red and can be touched, do not rush to treat its center. Treat it with what ripens it until these symptoms subside. Then seat the woman on a chair with two seats.
She brings her legs together toward the lower abdomen, with thighs apart, and places her arms underneath her knees, securing them with a suitable bandage.
Therefore, the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument for opening the mouth of the womb, an image of which will be shown later. It is necessary to estimate with care the depth of the woman's womb beforehand so that what enters from the instrument does not exceed its depth.
If the woman is harmed, then if the instrument is deeper than the depth of the womb, pads should be placed on the lip of the neck of the womb to prevent the entire instrument from entering its depth. The coil that runs inside the instrument should be from the upper side.
You hold the instrument, and the servant turns the coil until the neck of the womb opens. When the discharge appears and touches the hand, if it is thin and watery, then cut its head with a wide scissors until all the matter is expelled.
A soft wick should be placed in the wound, dipped in rose oil or green olive oil with a little myrrh. The wick should exit from the incision in the neck of the womb and be placed on the outside on the uterus and pubic area. Clean wool dipped in hot water boiled with cumin should also be used.
Then treat it after two days with the appropriate ointment until it heals.
The womb and wound can be washed by injecting water and honey, then with hot water boiled with frankincense or long pepper. Then return to using the ointment, especially if the discharge is hidden inside the womb.
It is not necessary to treat it with iron, but rather with what has been mentioned in this section.
Chapter Fifty-Nine
On teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses that emerge abnormally.
The midwife should first know the signs of natural childbirth: when a woman feels pressure downward, breathes heavily, and desires to push.
If the fetus comes out quickly, know that this is a natural birth and it will come headfirst with or without the umbilical cord. If you see these signs, do not press her belly to speed up its emergence.
If the afterbirth descends upon his head, it has descended with him. It should be cleaned from that excess in a thorough manner, but apart from this birth, any other is unnatural and condemned because the child may emerge feet-first or hands-first before the head.
If one hand or foot emerges first, or the head and one hand emerge together, or if it comes out folded over or turned on its back, or in any other condemned form, then midwives must be skilled and proficient with all these matters, taking care to avoid mistakes and errors.
And I have explained all these forms and how to deal with each one, so that you may rely upon them and stand upon them. If the child emerges head-first in the natural manner but it is difficult for the woman and hard on her, causing her strength to weaken, then seat her on a chair and order women to support her.
And anoint her womb with cooked fenugreek and damp cotton wool. Then, the midwife should insert a small lancet between her fingers and cut the umbilical cord until its moisture flows out, then press her belly until the child descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with fenugreek juice mixed with shark oil. After the injection, she should be made to sneeze and cough using ginger root, holding her mouth and nose closed until the child emerges immediately. If one of the child's hands emerges first, they should be gently pushed back in a little at a time.
If it is impossible for the hands to enter, then place the woman on a platform and raise her legs up high. Shake the platform on the ground while she holds on so as not to fall during the shaking. If the hands do not emerge and the child is dead, then cut off its hands and pull out the rest of the body.
Or tie its hands with a cloth and pull them gently until it emerges. If the child emerges feet-first, then push its feet up and turn the child slightly until it is straightened out, then take one of its feet and stretch it out little by little.
If it comes out to the thighs, order her to sneeze and blow into the vessel with a syringe, for it will come out. If not as described, then gently pull back the fetus little by little until it regains its natural shape, at which point it will exit smoothly.
Take the juice of mastik, walnut juice, shea butter, and gum arabic, mix them all together in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen. Afterwards, make her sit in lukewarm water until she feels relief.
If you see that her lower part has softened, make a suppository from myrrh for her to hold. After some time, seat her on a chair, then sneeze her and plug her mouth and nose, gently pressing her lower abdomen until the fetus comes out at once, with its legs and arms extended.
If it comes out in this manner, gently insert your hands to bring out its arms. Then, make the woman lie on her back with her hips raised and her head down while lying on a bed. She should hold her hands close to her chest before gently pressing above her belly. Remove the pillows from under her legs to lower them. If the fetus does not come out, grasp both of her legs together and shake them vigorously. Then, gently press above her hips until the fetus moves up. The midwife should then insert her hand and adjust the fetus.
Make the woman sneeze until the fetus comes out, coming out crooked with one of its hands extended. If you cannot put its hand back, then make the woman sit up and help her walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down on her side.
Lay her on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then apply the ointment made from the herbs described earlier. If labor progresses, seat her on a chair, then treat her by putting back her hand and adjusting the fetus to its natural shape. Make her sneeze and order her to sneeze until it comes out.
When the child emerges facing upwards with its arms outstretched and its face towards its mother's back, it is necessary for the midwife to grasp the child's hand, then gradually turn it a little at a time, carefully placing it until it is upright. Then proceed as previously described with the fat and wrappings.
Then have her move a little at a time until she does so, then seat her on the chair and instruct her to lean towards the right side. Then have her strain and push with the kandis, for the child will emerge standing upright on its hip.
If it descended to the mouth of the womb with the placenta in this manner, then tear the placenta with your fingers until it is freed from its moisture. If it adheres to the child's face and neck, cut it away at the root lest the child should be suffocated and die. Then push the child back inside until it assumes its proper position.
Instruct the woman to strain repeatedly, for it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or multiple fetuses is known to occur frequently, with three or four sometimes being born and surviving, although this is rare. As for five, it is contrary to nature and they never survive.
And they are surrounded by a single placenta, even if there are many. They are separated by a thin partition attached at the root of each one, and they emerge like a single fetus in a natural and easy manner, or in an abnormal fashion as previously described.
If some prove difficult at birth and assume the unnatural shapes mentioned earlier, treat them as described. Let the midwife be gentle, proceed with care and patience, and adapt her methods to whatever situation arises in order to ensure safety.
It has been mentioned that fetuses are found in various numbers in the womb, ranging from one to two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten. I have personally witnessed a woman who expelled seven and another who expelled fifteen, all of them being formed.
With the grace of the All-Knowing, this is all that should be known by a midwife. For perhaps such a case will come upon her one day, and she should deal with it appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven
On the Extraction of the Dead Fetus
If treating the dead fetus as mentioned in the section did not result in its expulsion, and it became necessary to proceed with manual intervention, then first examine the woman. If she is strong and there are no symptoms that would cause concern for her well-being, have her lie down on a bed on her back.
Her head should be tilted downwards, and her legs elevated. An assistant should hold her on both sides or secure her to the bed so that her body does not slip when pulling out the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of her uterus with a moistened cloth, applying spittle and decoctions of coltsfoot and fenugreek seeds ground with flaxseed. The midwife should then apply this mixture to her hand before gently inserting it into the uterus. She should then search for a suitable spot where she can grasp the fetus with forceps.
If the fetus is breech, insert the forceps into its eyes, cheeks, mouth, throat, or beneath its beard or umbilical cord, or in places close to its ribs or buttocks.
If the fetus is presenting by its legs, insert the forceps into the groin or central part of the ribs. Hold the forceps with your right hand and curve it between the fingers of your left hand. Gently insert your hand and grasp one of the mentioned locations with the forceps.
Continue inserting the forceps until you feel empty space. Then insert another or even a third forceps on the opposite side if necessary, ensuring equal traction and preventing it from tilting to one side. Pull gently in a straight line, but also move it from side to side.
This should be done gently, similar to how a cork is extracted from a bottle. During this process, occasionally relax the pulling force if it becomes stuck on one side. If necessary, the midwife can apply some grease to her fingers and insert them from one side to rotate what has become stuck. Once part of the fetus has been expelled, relocate the forceps to other positions.
Once the fetus has been expelled, gently raise it a little until its entire body emerges. If one of the hands comes out first and cannot be pushed back due to tightness, wrap it with a cloth and continue pulling until all of it exits. Then cut off the umbilical cord.
If the head of the fetus is large or pressed upon in its exit, or if there is water gathered in it, then you should introduce a hooked probe between the fingers.
And tear open the head so that the water comes out or draw it out with the instrument called the forceps, the image of which is found along with other images in the chapter following this.
Similarly, if the fetus has a large head, then you should split open the skull or draw it out with the forceps as mentioned earlier. Then remove the bones using tongs. If the head comes out but is compressed at the neck, make an incision until the moisture in the chest is relieved.
If the chest cavity is not restored, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is warm or there is heat in it, then compress it until moisture flows out of it.
If the fetus is presented by its feet, then pulling it out is easy and pushing it back into the mouth of the womb is simple. But if it is compressed in the abdomen or chest, then pull it out using a cloth wrapped around your hand and cut open the abdomen or chest until what is inside flows out.
If all other organs are removed and the head remains lodged, then let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she should enter her hand into the neck of the womb, seek out the head with her fingers, and pull it towards the mouth of the womb. Then insert a hook or two hooks and pull it out.
If the mouth of the womb is closed due to a hot tumor, then do not be harsh upon it. Rather, use moist compresses and apply poultices. Then sit in warm water for relaxation.
As for a fetus lying on its side, if it can be turned, then use the methods mentioned earlier for a live fetus. But if it cannot, then cut and remove it. Make sure to leave no pieces of the placenta inside. After you have finished treating it, apply the treatments mentioned earlier for hot tumors in the womb.
If there appears bleeding, let it flow into warm water and use other remedies. I have seen a woman who became pregnant, but the fetus died within her womb, and then she became pregnant by it again.
Then the other fetus also died, and after a long time she appeared with a tumor in her womb which swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called upon to treat her, but despite treating her for a long time the wound did not heal. So I applied some powerful drawing ointments.
Then a bone emerged from the location, then after some days another bone emerged. I was amazed by this as the belly is not a place for bones. I suspected that it was from the dead fetus' bones. So I searched the wound and found many skull bones of the deceased fetus.
I continued this until I removed many bones from her and the woman was in good health, living thus for a long time with only a small amount of pus coming from the location. But I have recorded this case here as it is unusual.
Because it contains knowledge and guidance for what the practicing physician may attempt in treatment.

And their ends are shaped like fingers, and they are one and a half cubits long. In the middle of the wooden parts there are two projections of the same wood securely attached to them, being half a cubit or slightly more in length and about two fingers or more in width. These are the projections.
These are the ones that enter into the mouth of the uterus to dilate it when you operate the screws, picture of another similar instrument but smoother and lighter:
It is made from ivory or ebony wood in the shape of forceps, but with two projections at its end as you see. Each projection is about one cubit long and two fingers wide. If you wish to dilate the uterus with it,
I seat the woman on a bed with her legs dangling, spread apart between her thighs. Then I insert these two ropes, tied together at one end, into the mouth of the womb while holding the other end below her thighs. Next, I open my hand with the tool as one does with tweezers, opening the womb to the desired extent.
