Chapter Eighty-Seven.
In removing the afterbirth.
If the afterbirth remains inside after delivery, order the woman to hold herself back and then plug her vagina with a tampon. Pull your hand on its mouth and nostrils until it comes out, otherwise cut as much of it as you can.
And make a hole in the cover and place inside it open herbs such as fennel, sage, mint, thyme, purslane, and cumin seeds. Soak them in water and heat them on fire. Then place one end of a silver tube on the hole of the pot's cover, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb. Then plug as mentioned earlier, and the afterbirth will come out quickly. If it still remains after this treatment, order the midwife to dip her left hand in sesame oil or ox-gall.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta with it. If you find it, grasp it and gently pull it out a little at a time until it comes out. If it is adherent deep in the uterus, then insert your hand as described previously until you locate the placenta, then gently pull it out bit by bit with caution.
Lest the uterus fall out due to excessive pulling, it is preferable to gently push it towards the sides, left and right, then gradually increase the force of traction. It will then come away from its adhesion. If the mouth of the uterus is closed, we have described the treatment by cauterization and ligation.
But if it does not come out despite all that has been described, then avoid excessive force in retrying to pull it out. Instead, tie what has emerged from the mouth of the uterus to the woman's thigh, and apply the quartremmer ointment. It will rot it after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out until, if it rots, a foul odor will emanate from it to the stomach and head, causing harm to the patient. It is therefore not advisable to use this method.
Therefore, some earlier practitioners have tried using incense and dried figs as suitable replacements for the quartremmer method in cases of menstruation retention and placenta expulsion, among others.
Make it from brass or copper in the shape of a funnel. Insert the narrow end into the vagina and place the wide end on the fire with incense burning on embers. Hold it until the incense burns out, then replace it with more.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
In treating an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with an unperforated anus, blocked by a thin membrane. The midwife should not pierce this membrane with her finger but rather use a sharp lancet to cut it without touching the muscle.
Then apply a linen pad soaked in honey and oil. Treat it with ointments until healed. If you fear that it may close up again, insert a brass tube into the hole for many days, then remove it when the child wants to pass stool.
Sadd al-muq'adah may also appear due to the fusion of a wound or tumor. It should be cut open, then treated with the aforementioned treatment and dressing.
Chapter Eighty
In treating hernias that occur in the lower region
Hernias that occur in the lower region are a complex and thickened formation near or within the space of one side of the rectum, appearing as single or multiple prolapses.
If this complex breaks open, a watery white fluid or thin pus may escape from it. Some hernias may prolapse into the bladder or intestines and remain unruptured; however, prolapsed ones can be identified by the passage of feces or wind upon attempting defecation with an enema.
Furthermore, worms may emerge from the hernia, and there can be additional prolapses if it is situated within the space, prolapsing into the bladder, urethra, groin, or perineum. Hernias that prolapse into the rectum are also considered non-prolapsed.
Insert your index finger into the anus and use a thin probe made of copper or iron to enter the hernia if there is no covering; otherwise, use a thin lead probe or horsehair suture if there is covering.
Until you feel the probe or hair within your finger, if nothing emerges from the hole, no feces, wind, or worms as mentioned earlier, then know that it is not prolapsed and proceed with treatment.
If the hernia prolapses into the bladder or urethra, it can be diagnosed by urine emerging from it and failure of medications to adhere. If it prolapses into the groin or perineum, the probe will reach that location.
If there is no bony covering, pain in the affected side, constant discharge of pus, lack of improvement with treatment, and failure to adhere with ointment, all prolapsed hernias are incurable.
The treatment of prolapsed hernias is futile for those who attempt to reduce them without knowledge. However, non-prolapsed and non-chronic ones can be cured by the use of iron as described and proven by me, which involves placing the patient on their back in front of you.
Splay his legs upwards and incline his thighs towards his belly, then insert the copper or lead probe if it is in the navel until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the bladder, do not insert your index finger into the bladder.
If you feel the probe has passed through on its own, exposed without feeling any fat or flesh between your finger and it, then know certainly that it is perforated. Do not waste time with it, for there is no cure in this case as we have said.
They say that it sometimes heals in some people, rarely. One of the treatments hoped for success is to heat a thin iron with fire according to the width of the navel and insert it into the navel until it reaches near the bladder, then repeat this process twice or three times.
Until you know that all the excess flesh has burned, resembling chicken feather tubing. Then treat it by twisting with melted fat until the burned flesh comes out, then apply burning ointments and it will heal.
Otherwise, it will not heal without other treatment ever. If the probe was inserted but did not reach your finger in the bladder, and there is a thick barrier of flesh or peritoneum between them, and you see the navel above the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the navel. Then pass through the incision with the probe still in the navel until you reach where the tip of the probe ended, and remove it. Then cleanse all the excess flesh resembling chicken feather tubing so that nothing remains.
Then treat it with cauterizing ointments until it heals. If bleeding overpowers you and hinders your work in cutting that flesh, the best thing to do quickly is to cauterize with fire or sharp medicine, for cauterization by fire combines two beneficial states: burning the excess flesh.
Stop the bleeding, dry the wound, then treat it by twisting with melted fat or sulfur powder mixed with oil until it discharges. Then remove the burned flesh in the discharge, and afterwards treat the wound with cauterizing ointments that are effective on solid flesh.
These are the medications in which constipation and thirst occur. If they become severe, recovery will not occur unless it is treated. If the wound has reached deep into the bladder and is distant from the body's surface, insert your finger into the bladder and search with it.
If you feel the bladder wall and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between you and it near the urethra, then the only course of action is to hope for the best. In this case, one of three approaches can be taken: either cauterize it as mentioned before.
Or, cut it until you reach its base, allowing you to insert the probe and administer treatment from close by. Do not cut too deeply lest you sever the muscle surrounding the bladder, causing involuntary urine leakage. Then treat it as mentioned before, recovery may occur.
Or, pierce that barrier after cutting it close to the bladder as mentioned before, either with a probe or another sharp instrument. Then clean out all the affected flesh in the incision as much as possible.
Then sew up the entire wound along with the incision and upper opening until it heals, leaving the urethra open inside the bladder. This will be easier for the patient. The urethra may remain like this.
If a probe is inserted into the urethra and it is near the surface of the body with the skin and bladder wall, take a probe like this: pierced at one end, shaped like a saddle stirrup.
And insert into it a thread made from five or so strands. Then insert the probe with the thread into the urethra until it reaches its base. If it is open near the edge of the bladder inside.
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the bladder and pulling out the thread's end. Bring both ends together, tie them tightly, and leave it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, pull it tightly until those tissues between the thread's ends break off and fall away. Then treat the wound until it heals.
If the nostril of the patient is not dilated, dilate it as much as possible without going too deep lest you cut the muscle. Then proceed as mentioned until it heals.
A sharp-pointed instrument is used to cut the nostrils, with one end being very sharp and the other not so as to avoid unnecessary cutting.
Chapter 81.
Treatment of hemorrhoids that bleed and fissures, and their repair.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red grapes, or they resemble small and large bunches of raisins, from which blood always flows.
Some hemorrhoids are single, some are multiple, and some are external to the anus. Those that are external usually have little moisture and discharge yellow water or a small amount of blood occasionally, appearing similar in color to the skin.
And the treatment for those inside the anus is to order the patient to evacuate their bowels and cleanse themselves until the hemorrhoids appear. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or pinch them with your fingers, and cut them at their base if they do not stick due to moisture and relaxation.
Remove it with a coarse cloth or a piece of linen soaked in vinegar, or pull it out with your fingers, then cut it off. After cutting, sprinkle some sharp salt to act as a cautery, or cauterize it as mentioned earlier under the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with other medications until it heals. If the anus does not expel, inject the patient with a solution of myrrh to wash out its contents and facilitate expulsion when they evacuate their bowels. The external hemorrhoids are easier to handle.
And for external hemorrhoids, grasp them with your fingers or attach them with forceps and cut them off. Then treat them as mentioned until they heal. And if cutting with iron is disliked, use a ligature by taking a thread, doubling it, and inserting it into a needle.
Then you draw the mole upwards and pierce it with a needle at its base from the other side, wrapping both ends of the thread under the needle as it is inserted, and pulling the mole tightly. Then you tie the knot and remove the needle. Do this for all moles except one that you leave unexcised.
To cause a drop of blood to flow from it, then you place on the navel a cloth soaked in rose oil or carrot oil and apply it there, commanding the patient to remain still until it falls. Once it has fallen, treat the moles with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
As for the treatment of piles, it often appears from the hollows of the anus and constipation. If it persists without improvement despite medication, then scrape it with a sharp blade or rasp until it becomes moist and the outer skin that prevents adhesion is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as is proper. If it does not heal, then scrape it more harshly than the first time until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it again; it will recover.
Chapter Two Eighty.
In treating inverted and non-inverted splinters, dry moles, and warts.
A splinter is nothing but a round node on the skin's color, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body but especially beneath the feet and toes. It causes pain upon walking.
It is necessary to incise around the splinter, grasp it with forceps or a tweezers and remove it from its root. Then treat it as mentioned earlier in its chapter. As for dry moles, each one is a small, rough, tangled, round node on the surface of the skin.
They often appear on the hands of children. Its treatment involves pulling out the mole and cutting it off or excising it with a silk thread or hair until it falls. You may cauterize it with fire or apply strong medication as mentioned earlier.
The inverted and non-inverted moles can be removed using the instrument mentioned earlier for treating warts. As for warts, they are also small, tangled nodes on the skin's surface that penetrate deeply into it. When exposed to cold, one feels a sensation similar to that of ants biting.
A tube should be taken from the feather of a goose or a strong eagle's feather and made into an instrument to encircle the mole around its circumference, then rotate the hand with the tube until it cuts through what surrounds the mole.
And inserting it into the depth of the flesh and removing the mole from its root, you can do this with a copper or iron tube as follows:
The upper part of the tube should be closed and thickened to make it easy for the fingers to hold and twist. You can cauterize the mole beforehand as mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the shapes of instruments used for injecting enemas, purgatives, and cataplasms.
And the syringe can be made of silver, bronze, copper, or gold, and it can be plain or engraved. They should be made in various sizes according to their intended use. And the syringes used for treating young children with narrow or constricted anuses should be very thin. The shape of a large syringe:
The upper end of the syringe where the bulb is attached should be wide, as shown. There is a rim at the point where the bulb attaches, and the lower tip that enters the anus should be smooth and rounded with two small holes on one side and one larger hole on the other.
The width of the holes should be about the thickness of a barley grain or slightly thicker. The bulb that holds the medicine should be made from an animal's bladder or a ram's caecum, with a diameter of one sherva.
And it should be pierced with many holes around its circumference, about the thickness of a finger apart. Then insert a thin thread made from twisted wool (ten or twelve threads) into these holes and gather up the bulb like a small sieve. After filling it with medicine, tie the syringe tightly between the two stones at its head. Then use it to administer the enema. This is a diagram of the bulb and the hole:
To administer the enema, fill the bulb with warm medicine without making it too hot or too cold. Then have the patient lie on their back and raise their legs towards the ceiling.
Place beneath it a board or cloth, then anoint the tip of the syringe with oil or egg white or mallow juice or similar. Then insert it gently into the rectum. Then the servant takes the tube in both hands and squeezes it firmly until all its contents are emptied into the bowel.
Then remove the syringe and have the patient place their legs against the wall, if urged by the medication to expel it. If they pass the entire night in this state, it is most beneficial for the treatment of diarrhea. If used to treat dysentery and expedite its discharge, prepare another dose until recovery.
If there is haemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, let the syringe be very gentle and smooth, like those used for children. This is a diagram of such a soft syringe:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned the treatment of wounds similar to burns earlier, but here I will mention the treatment by hand and medication in detail. Know that surgical wounds differ according to the cause of the surgery and the location of the wound.
There are many things by which surgeries occur, such as a stone cut, sword or knife cut, spear stab, stake wound, arrow and the like from many other things.
As for wounds according to their locations on the body: those that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver and similar organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some simple head wounds, as they serve as a model and standard for other wounds. I begin with simple head wounds.
I say that when a simple head wound occurs, not involving bone fracture, and it is from a stone cut or similar, having only grazed the skin but with a large wound, fearing hot swelling for the patient, quickly tie off the bleeding point. Do not delay this. Let out blood according to his strength if it does not prevent bleeding, especially if the blood coming from the wound is slow. Let the ligation be on the opposite side of the wound.
Warn the patient against overeating and drinking, especially harsh foods. If prohibited from eating solids, let them reduce food intake and avoid meat and hot drinks. Apply a compress of cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with the aforementioned drink if inflammation occurs at the wound.
If the inflammation has been cured, apply to the wound if it is fresh with its own blood without exposing it to the corrupt air that I shall describe later. But if it has already been exposed to such air, apply some ointment.
Until the pus discharges, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges do not meet, sew it together as I describe in abdominal surgery.
If the skin has been stripped from the bone and hangs loosely, cut it off. Then treat the wound with medications that promote the growth of hard flesh to replace the skin, and advise the patient to consume foods rich in nutrients like heads and fish.
If gangrene appears on the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all the rotted part and discard it. Then treat the wound. If there is an artery or vein bleeding profusely that has not stopped despite medication, search for and ligate the artery.
If it is struck but does not heal, cauterize it with a scalpel and ligate it. If necessary, when the aforementioned remedies fail, cut until bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and numerous, simply treating them by applying this corrupt air to them while they are fresh.
Before exposing it to the air, the corrupt air dressing is prepared by mixing one part of frankincense, two parts of gum acacia, and three parts of quicklime or unslaked lime. Grind all ingredients together, sieve, and apply firmly on the wound until well adhered.
The dressing is secured with blood, then the wound edges are approximated from above and left undisturbed until inflammation subsides. The wound should not be changed until it heals. Alternatively, quicklime alone can be used if frankincense and gum acacia are unavailable.
He can also perform this act with his finger in many large surgeries, if he mastered its placement and tension. But if the surgeries are fresh with blood, it should not be changed by air; however, if it has been altered somewhat by air, especially in summer.
There is no purpose in this bandaging as mentioned by carrying on it some prepared ointments or applying a dough made of barley flour with water and honey until the pus comes out, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
But if the wound causes a fracture of the bone, and it is slight, bind it with plaster. I have mentioned the treatment of head fractures in the section on surgery of the neck; if the wound is on the neck, its treatment is not different from that of a simple head wound.
But if he cut the nerve or artery, then if it is a nerve, there is no way to treat it except by avoiding its treatment with anything that constricts strongly like ginger and quicklime etc., for they harm the nerve and paralyze it.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection with the brain which is the noblest organ. Therefore, what is used to treat it should be warm remedies like washed purslane soaked in sweet water for several times dipped in oil or rose oil.
If you have also washed it with sweet water and other mineral stones on this condition, and ointments. But if the wound is large, use suturing or approximate its edges with pins until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a cavity underneath where pus has accumulated, then drain it at its lowest point. If an artery is severed in the wound and you notice blood flowing profusely, then ligate or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has cut some or all of the tonsils and the uvula is exposed, then sew up the skin edges over the tonsillar stalk without touching the tonsils. Then place the uvula in its natural shape and tie it gently and leave it for some time.
Then he treated it with plaster which had adhesive and drying properties until it healed. If the wound had affected the bone, he searched it; if there were any fragments of bone in it, he extracted them all or as many as possible, and if he could not manage to do so, he left them until the wound softened and healed and their removal became easier.
If the wound was tender, he applied the poultice we have described. If it became inflamed in the wound and hardened like bone, then treat it as mentioned in his chapter on surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you find it has a cavity, observe: if wind passes out of it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have a cavity but is still moist with blood, do not apply any poultice at first nor press it lest blood be trapped inside it.
It may extend to the heart and kill the patient. But apply an adhesive plaster and change it twice a day. If you do not have any plaster, place a wick of old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb any moisture that comes out, and let the patient sleep with the wound downwards.
If three days or more have passed since the injury and the patient has not experienced convulsions, rapid breathing, shortness of breath nor other signs of deterioration, know that the wound is safe. Treat it by cauterization and other remedies until it heals.
If you cannot see it and the air persists in escaping, know that it has become hard. Treat it as mentioned in his chapter. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with what has been mentioned under suturing.
If it is large or filled with poultice, if it has affected the bone and some fragments have broken off, search the wound and hasten to extract those fragments if they are clean. If not, leave them until.
until the wound heals for it becomes easier to remove them. As for other wounds that occur in other organs, their treatment is judged by what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five
In surgical procedures for abdominal incisions and the extraction of intestines and their suturing.
A wound that exposes the abdomen may be large, small or medium in size; a large one may allow the exit of intestines or several segments thereof, making their reinsertion and suturing more difficult.
Even a small wound can pose difficulties of its own, making closure challenging; intestines may swell and thicken if not immediately replaced into the abdomen, causing difficulty in their reinsertion. Therefore, a medium-sized wound is preferable as it does not pose these challenges.
Know that abdominal suturing can be performed in four ways: the two common methods are suitable for abdominal closure and other general surgeries, while the other two specialized methods are primarily used for abdominal closure.
And I will describe each method to you individually with explanation, saying that if the wound is small and a portion of intestine has exited, making its replacement difficult, this may be due to either the small size of the wound as mentioned earlier, or because the intestines were exposed to air before being replaced, causing them to fill with air.
In such a case, it is advisable to warm the intestines by moistening a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone or adding hot water until the swelling and gas subsides and the intestines can be easily returned to their position.
Alternatively, a solution containing lint seeds (Trigonella foenum-graecum) can be used to dissolve the gas, as it is more effective than water alone. Once the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with hot water boiled with sesame seed or lentil to facilitate their reinsertion into the abdomen with ease.
If the intestines still cannot be replaced even after these treatments, make a small incision in the wound using an instrument designed for cutting ligatures. This procedure should only be performed as a last resort when other methods have failed. The instrument has the following appearance:
The tip of the instrument should be slightly blunted, not as sharp as a lancet, and resemble a small tweezers in appearance. When the wound is widened enough to allow the entrance of the intestines, they should not be replaced in their natural shape.
If possible, its proper place is preferable. If the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs raised higher than their head. If it is located in the upper abdomen, they should be positioned with their head and chest elevated above their feet.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always elevate the side where the surgery is being performed higher than the other side. This should also be done in major and minor surgeries.
As for small surgeries, position the patient as best you can. Then have a skilled assistant nearby to hold all of the wound's edges with their hands and pull its lips together, gradually revealing more of it to the surgeon for suturing.
This is the general method of suturing: Take as many needles or needle holders as needed according to the wound's width. Leave a length of thread equal to the thickness of a finger at the end of the wound, then insert one needle without threading it into one edge of the skin. Pass the needle through both edges of the skin and the peritoneum beneath it until it emerges from the other side, bringing together the skin edge and peritoneal edge to form four layers. Then tie the thread securely around the needle with a double knot on both sides to close the wound edges tightly.
Next, leave a length equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, threading it as done with the first needle. Continue this process with needles of medium thickness until the entire wound is closed.
Do not use very fine needles as they quickly cut through flesh, nor very thick ones as they are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, medium-sized needles should be used, and do not prick the needle too close to the skin edge.
To prevent the flesh from breaking quickly and the wound from opening before it can heal, do not stitch too loosely or too tightly. Then cut off the needle tips to avoid hurting the patient during sleep, and use cotton bandages on each side to hold the needles in place.
Leave it until you learn that the wound has healed, for this type of stitching with needles is most suitable in small wounds because they may be sewn with one or two needles or similar.
And as for the characteristic of suturing for novices, it is that he should gather together in one stitch all four layers - namely, the skin edges and the peritoneum - using a moderately thick thread on a needle; then, once this has been done, he should withdraw the needle from where he began.
The thread should be tied at the top of the wound to resemble the stitching used for tying down cargo, leaving one finger's width between each stitch.
With this suturing method, an abdominal wound was closed for a man whose abdomen had been stabbed with a knife. The wound was over a span in length and about two spans of the middle intestine had prolapsed from it, being centrally located.
After keeping his intestines outside the wound for twenty-four hours, he returned them and sutured the wound. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered completely. He lived many years afterward, functioning normally in all respects. Physicians considered him to be incurable.
It is remarkable that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines. Instead, I applied boiled cotton to the wound twice daily and cleaned it with honey water until it healed.
One of the specialized types of suturing is as follows, according to Galen's words: begin at the skin and insert the needle from outside inwards; once the needle has pierced through the skin and entered the straight muscle fibers running along the entire length of the abdomen...
Leave the edge of the peritoneum in the side where you entered the needle, and pass it through the other edge from inside out. Then, pass the needle again into this same edge from outside in.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and pass the needle through its other edge from inside out, also passing into the edge of the quantum on that side until it pierces all.
Then he also began from this side of the eye, drawing a line along the edge that is towards the liver on the other side, and brought out the needle from the skin near it, then returned it into that skin, drawing the edge of the liver on the other side along with this edge.
And he brought it out from the skin on his side, and did this again and again until the entire incision was stitched in that manner. The description of this stitching is like the sewing together of a garment's seams.
That he should stitch the peritoneum once from one side with the skin edge and leave it once on the other side until emptied. As for the second type of special stitching mentioned by Galen, this is his exact words.
And that he should stitch it like those who stitch the parts together, gathering each part to its counterpart. The difficulty in this is that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the liver to its other edge, as I have described to you.
It is necessary to insert the needle into the edge of the liver near you from outside alone and pass it inside there alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside in the other edge of the liver on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than common stitching, and it is the stitching that was mentioned earlier; this is Galen's exact words. Some have said that the abdomen can be stitched with two needles. And that you should insert a single thread into them and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then insert the other needle from the opposite side so that it passes from your side according to the stitching of the sacs.
Know that wounds, if they are in the middle of the abdomen, their stitching is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its ruling in treatments is like that of other surgeries. If you reach the wound while it is still fresh with its blood before the air has altered it, return the intestines and stitch and mend them, then apply meat broth compresses.
If the wound has been exposed to air, apply some of the dressings you have prepared until it suppurates and the threads fall off, and the peritoneum and membranes adhere. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have any prepared dressings, apply from the beginning sterile gauze and change it twice a day as I instructed until it heals. In most cases, there is no need for further treatment if the wounds are simple; however, be cautious lest the wound share pain with the main organs.
It is preferable to soak a clean piece of wool in warm olive oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the thighs and armpits. If there is pain or foul smell in the abdomen, this often occurs; therefore, give him an decoction of black hemlock.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into its cavity, know that thick intestines are easier to heal while thin ones are harder.
As for the intestine known as the 'fasting' one, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it frequently due to its abundance of veins and bones, and the thinness of its wall and its closeness to nerve tissue. However, if what emerges from the wound is the turbidum and it is still soft, replace it according to how you would replace the intestine.
If it has passed some time and the turbidum has turned green or black, then tie it above the place where it has turned black with a thread to prevent bleeding. There are veins and arteries in the turbidum. Then cut what is below that ligament.
Leave the ends of the thread attached from below the wound, outside it for ease in pulling and removing it when the turbidum falls off and the wound suppurates. As for a wound that appears in the intestine...
Some people may become accustomed to it (the wound in the intestine), as I have seen a man who was pierced by a lance in his abdomen, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. It became constricted and became an opening through which feces and wind escaped. I treated him without expecting healing. I did not cease applying the plaster until it adhered and the place became sound. When I saw that the place had become sound, I feared that some evil might happen to the patient inside his body; but no such evil happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. He eats, drinks, copulates, takes a bath, and performs his service. Some who have experimented with it mentioned that when it appears in the bowel, it is usually a small wound, and should not be sutured based on this description.
Large-headed ants are to be taken, then the lips of the wound are brought together and one ant is placed with its mouth open on each lip. When it clamps down and closes its mouth, its head will adhere and not come loose; then another ant is placed nearby.
And this is done with several ants according to the size of the wound, then the wound is dressed and sutured. Those heads will remain adherent in the bowel until it heals and the patient suffers no harm at all. The bowel may also be sutured with fine thread.
This one which adheres from the animal glue after entering a needle: take the end of this thread from the glue, then pull it through with tweezers and tie a thin, twisted linen thread to its end, then insert that thread into the needle. And with this glue-thread, the bowel is sutured and then returned to the abdomen. This method of suturing with ants and glue is only for those who are desperate and hopeful.
But if the tear is large and wide, especially in one of the delicate intestines, there is no remedy nor any cure for it at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
Regarding the treatment of catarrh and nasal polyps.
Know that every wound or tumor, if it becomes chronic and does not heal, and continues to discharge pus without ceasing, is called generally in any organ, 'catarrh,' and we call them 'polyps.'
It is actually a solid, tangled, white substance without pain, having a hollow like the hollow of a bird's feather.
And thus some call it 'feather,' for sometimes it is moist, continuously secreting pus, and at other times the moisture may cease. This moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
I previously mentioned, in the section on surgeries, that every ulcer will not heal nor will flesh grow in it unless there are nine causes: one of them is either insufficient blood in the body, or its thickness, or because there is solid flesh inside and around its edges preventing good flesh from growing. Or it may be due to many discharges and impurities, or because the ulcer itself is foul and its matter is of poor quality. Or it may be due to an unsuitable remedy being applied, or there has been a contagion of this type in the town, or there is something particular about this locality.
Or it may be due to many discharges and impurities, or because the ulcer itself is foul and its matter is of poor quality. Or it may be due to an unsuitable remedy being applied, or there has been a contagion of this type in the town, or there is something particular about this locality. This has been mentioned regarding the city of Saragossa, where recovery from illnesses is difficult and healing of tumors is delayed naturally, either because there is one or several bones in it. All these causes and their signs and treatments with medications have been explained in the section.
I should now inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and catarrh by manual operation. Know that polyps can occur in all parts of the body, some ending in large veins or arteries, others in nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, intestines, bladder, ribs, vertebrae of the back, or joints with complex structure like hands and feet. Some also approach important organs.
If polyps have reached these states, they are among the difficult-to-heal diseases that do not accept treatment and are troublesome and confusing. But if there are polyps that have not reached these places mentioned and desired for treatment.
Take a probe made of copper or iron, if the polyp is straight, use it to explore. But if there are branches in the polyp, use a thin lead probe because lead softens its body.
If it enters crookedly and turns towards the branches, if the polyp has many openings that you cannot discern with the probe, then inject one of its mouths with water; the moisture will flow towards the other mouths.
Then, meticulously examine the site as much as possible to determine if there is a bone or nerve present, or if the cavity is deep or shallow, or if it has one opening with multiple entrances. Make note of all this based on your patient's tolerance and discomfort when you press on the location.
And other such indications and causes of tumors, once you have accurately established all this, then proceed to treatment with confidence. If it is found that.
If the cavity is superficial and close to a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the aforementioned locations, then incise the cavity as previously described and remove from it any excess flesh, spoiled flesh, and clots.
And remove any flesh that grows inside its mouth or warts and the like, treating it until it heals. If the cavity is deep and straight, do not cut too deeply into it but rather clean out all of its spoiled flesh.
Then apply the cautery bandage using the hot method, inserting it into the depths of the cavity that you have reached with the probe. Repeat this procedure until the cavity is completely freed from corruption.
Then apply ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal, repeat the procedure as previously described. If the cause of the cavity is bone and you are certain of this, then explore and incise it as instructed, provided there is no impediment to doing so.
If a vein, nerve, or principal organ is exposed and some corruption and blackness are found on the bone, scrape it clean until its corruption disappears. Then treat it with what adheres to it until it heals. If it does not heal and continues to discharge pus as before.
Then know that you have not completely removed all the corruption. Expose it again and scrape it clean thoroughly, then treat it. If it heals, good. Otherwise, know that the corruption is deep-seated, so expose it again.
And scrape and clean it thoroughly with all your effort. If the bone is small and corrupted, try to extract it using suitable forceps.
If there are many bones, then meticulously pull them all without leaving any behind to your utmost ability. If something from the ligaments stands in your way and cutting them poses no danger, cut those ligaments and flesh if present, and free the bones.
If you are unable to extract the bones promptly, apply something that will cauterize the wound and leave it for some days until what surrounds those bones in flesh and ligaments has decomposed. Then preserve the wound so that the cut does not heal during your procedure.
But if you fear this, use a linen cloth soaked in powdered sulfur mixed with oil, or a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian pitch or one of the green ointments. For when these ligaments decompose and the bones become loose, it is easy to extract them.
If there is a single large bone like the thighbone or shinbone and only its end has rotted, strip it bare completely until that blackness and decay disappear. Then close the wound. But if a significant portion of it has rotted and the decay has reached the marrow, then it must be removed.
And cut off all of it until the decay ends. Then treat it so that it heals, and I shall tell you about a man who had gangrene in his leg as an example for your treatment, this man was elderly.
Around thirty years old, he had pain in his leg for a cause that moved from within the body until the matter reached the leg and it swelled greatly and became red. There was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of physicians until the abscess burst open.
And much matter flowed from it, and his treatment was poor until the leg putrefied and became full of many holes all discharging pus. Physicians treated him for nearly two years, but none were skilled in manual surgery.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with much matter flowing from those holes like a great river. The man's body had wasted away and his color was yellowish. So I inserted the probe into one of those holes until it reached the bone, then I explored all the holes.
I found that some of them communicated with each other from all sides of the leg, so I made an incision on one of those openings until it revealed some bone which was decayed and had turned black and rotten to such an extent that the hole extended into the brain. Then I exposed what was visible.
He removed the decayed bone, believing that there was no other decay besides what he had cut out and exposed. Then he applied medicinal poultices for an extended period to try and heal the wound, but it did not adhere. He then returned and reopened the wound above the first incision.
I found the decay connected to the bone, so I also exposed what was visible of it. Then I tried to cauterize it but it did not heal nor adhere. So I continued cutting pieces from the bone until I reached about a span [of length].
After removing it with its root, he cauterized it with medicines which healed quickly and completely. However, this repeated procedure was necessary due to the patient's weak condition, his inability to endure, and fear of death since he was constantly suffering from severe diarrhea.
And in the place of the bone, solid flesh grew and his condition improved. He regained his strength and was able to move around without any further complaint from walking. If a broken bone is found in a part of the body,
It is not necessary to expose it in this manner. Instead, take a bandage and tie one end around the protruding part of the bone. Then command someone to pull it upwards while another stronger bandage made of wool is wrapped around it.
Then tie it to the flesh underneath the bone and pull its end upwards so that the flesh is pulled downwards. While exposing the flesh at the place where you want to cut without damaging it with the saw, place a piece of wood or board under the bone from below.
Otherwise, the saw would not hesitate to cut the decayed bone. It is better to make the incision slightly above the site of decay so that there is no decay inside the bone cavity and thus you will not have to cut it again.
If the bone is decayed and not protruding but connected to itself, with decay in its center or some parts of it, expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the lever from below, then separate it from the first side where the decay is.
Once you have separated it from the first side, separate it from the other side. The separation should be a little away from the decay, as we have mentioned. If the decay is at a joint, cut off the affected joint and separate the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the decay is between two joints, there is no cure but excision. If the decay is in the hand's metacarpus or foot's metatarsus, its treatment is very difficult, but you should scrape off whatever decay appears and excise and clean it as much as possible.
And however you proceed, if you are not hindered by blood vessels or nerves, know that the saws and chisels for cutting these bones are numerous according to their position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and fragility. Therefore, you should prepare a specific tool for each type of operation.
And know that the operations themselves may indicate the type of tool needed if you have a long experience and knowledge of the arts of this profession, for mastering the profession and observing many cases of diseases. He may infer what is suitable for himself from tools for each disease, and I will illustrate for you at the end of this chapter a number of instruments.

The Seventy-Seventh Chapter.
In severing limbs and separating bones.
If the extremities are putrefied, either due to an external or internal cause. When treated with medications and the treatment proves ineffective while the putrefaction spreads throughout the limb without ceasing,
It is necessary then to cut that limb where the putrefaction has reached, to save the patient from death or a great affliction worse than losing the limb. A sign of this condition is when the limb turns black as if it had been burnt by fire.
Or putrefies after turning black until the putrefaction spreads to other limbs, involving the whole body. In this case, cut it off immediately. Similarly, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of venomous creatures like scorpions or snakes or spiders, etc.
If the corruption or bite is at the tip of a finger, cut off the finger without delay, lest the putrefaction spread to the hand. Similarly, if it has taken hold in the hand, cut it off at its root from the wrist, and do not wait for the putrefaction to spread into the forearm.
Similarly, if it has taken hold in the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. But if the putrefaction has reached towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that would mean death to the patient. Instead, employ other treatments according to one's ability.
Do the same for the leg if corruption takes hold in the toe, cut off the toe at one of its joints. If it has taken hold in the foot, cut off the entire foot. And if it has risen to the knee, cut off the thigh at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it alone and for the patient to submit to death. The method of cutting or opening the member: tie a band below the place you intend to cut and another band above it.
Extend one servant the lower band downwards and another servant the upper band upwards. Then strip the flesh between the bands with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or open. It is necessary to place strips of linen on all sides.
So that the saw may not cut into the correct place causing additional pain and heat. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly plug the place or apply some absorbent cloths to stop the bleeding then return to your treatment until finished.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I inform you of an example of this condition in a man's leg which I will describe to you: blackness with heat like fire appeared in his leg.
The decay first appeared in his toe, spreading to the entire foot. The man cut it off at the joint himself when he saw the decay progressing and felt intense pain and heat. It then healed.
After a long time had passed, the decay appeared again in his index finger. He came to me and I applied various medications to it but the decay did not stop, instead it spread to the other fingers.
He then asked me to cut off his hand but I refused, hoping that the decay could still be cured. I also feared that he might die if his hand was amputated because his condition was critical. He eventually gave up and went home.
I heard later that he cut off his entire hand himself and it healed. I tell this story so that it may serve as a guide for similar cases in the future.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
Treatment of fistulas and their injection with medications.
If a tumor appears in some fleshy member and persists for a long time until it bursts open or bursts out, then all the contents are emptied and the place remains empty like a vessel.
The skin that is like a cloth has been cauterized, and its defect was not so severe as to affect bone, nerve, or ligament. Therefore it deserves to be called 'protected', but it would not have deserved to be called 'sound' had its defect extended such that it affected one of these members.
At this point it is called 'sound' or 'healthy', and its treatment is to cut away all of that skin as it curves, especially if it has been cauterized and become like a cloth. Ensure that it does not adhere to the site due to its defect.
If you wish for the skin to adhere and its defect has not reached that extent, there is a good amount of flesh in it, then treat it by injection. Look into the protected area; if the pus that comes from it has an offensive odor, cauterize it with Egyptian medicine.
This is made by mixing equal parts of honey, oil, and vinegar in a vessel and cooking it over fire until the medicine thickens like honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, heat it up, inject it into the protected area, tie its mouth shut, and leave the medicine inside for two hours.
Then squeeze it out, doing this repeatedly until the protected area is cleaned and the offensive odor goes away. If you do not have this medicine, you can use ash water instead, by taking either grapewood or oakwood ashes and pouring water over them.
Then clean it and inject with it until you are sure that the protected area has been rinsed. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, mix some powdered cinnamon into honey and water, then inject this mixture.
Or inject with a mixture of honey and wine, as honey cleanses and rinses while wine adheres to the protected area, especially if it is a fine vintage. Once you have done this several times and ensured that the defect has gone, then inject something that promotes flesh growth.
Such as taking the green ointment, dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine, or injecting some of the other medications and ointments whose properties have been described earlier. If the mouth of the protected area is too narrow for the tip of the syringe.
Widen it slightly with iron or insert a thread soaked in Egyptian ointment or St. John's Wort ointment until it expands. Similarly, if the mouth is also wide, gather its lips by suturing and leave an opening sufficient for the entrance of the syringe.
And if the mouth from which pus issues is raised upwards, incise it at its lowest point so that the pus may flow downwards, for when pus accumulates in a cavity it prevents flesh from growing there.
But if you are unable to make the incision downwards as desired, then raise the organ slightly so that pus may flow out easily according to what is feasible for you and ensure that no pus remains trapped within it at any time.
And as for the cauterizing medicines that are applied to the cavity from outside and how they should be placed, take one of the cauterizing ointments suitable for your purpose, spread it on a cotton cloth which should be large enough to cover the entire cavity. Then, using scissors, make a larger opening opposite the mouth of the cavity. Take one of the soft ointments on another cloth of the same size as the opening and apply it at some distance from the mouth of the cavity to facilitate the exit of pus. Do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
For several days, remove the dressing at the mouth of the cavity at regular intervals to allow the accumulated pus to escape and to determine whether the pus being discharged is abundant or scanty, turbid or clear.
And also examine the site of the cavity itself to determine whether its owner feels any pain there or not, and whether it is swollen or not. If you find that the treatment has progressed in this manner and that the matter being discharged from the mouth of the cavity has become serous...
...then know that the cavity has healed or nearly healed. In this case, leave it for another day or two and when you open it on the following day or third day, change the dressing and renew the ointment. If after a long time serous fluid is discharged from the cavity, do not despair of its healing.
Curing is near because among people are those whose flesh's growth in wounds is delayed, and others whose opposite is that; so if it emerges from the cavity after many days as undissolved pus, know that the cavity has not adhered. If the adhesion of the cavity was delayed.
If its case is prolonged, increase the drying of the remedies. Let them be remedies that are moist in consistency but dry in power, like the ointment of bees if it has had too much addition of wax; and the most effective for treating this is to take the ointment of bees.
Apply rose oil and sprinkle the moderate, consistent old wine upon it then knead it with it and use it. And do this action if the ointment of bees is not available; take honey, if you have it, cook it until it froths and use it, or take myrrh, frankincense, and bdellium grind all together and sprinkle on the honey while it is on fire. Then boil it on a cloth and wrap it around the cavity or apply the honey that has been cooked until it froths, sprinkle drugs upon it, place the cloth upon it, and wrap.
It may be used in such a case the long asafetida, the blue lily root, and the dust of nard, frankincense, and storax either separately or together ground and sieved then sprinkled on the honey previously described and used. If the cavity remains prolonged without being cured by this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
When it affects the bones or any other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment of the 'Nassour' as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Eight.
In the treatment of [cases involving] dislocations and fractures, removal of the supernumerary toe, and separation of adhered fingers.
Dish is excessive flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb, either of the hand or foot, and may also grow on other fingers. If its case is prolonged without treatment, it becomes inflamed and hot.
Or it may putrefy and become corrupt until it consumes the root of the nail, perhaps all of it is spoiled. And perhaps the corruption reaches the bone so that it has a foul odor, and the tip of the finger becomes bare and its color darkens. If you have treated it with what we mentioned in the section without success.
Firstly, cut off all the excess flesh that grows from the nail using iron. Then cauterize the wound afterwards; cauterization in this case is quite beneficial. However, if the bone is sound and the nail also is sound.
And the angle of the nail that has caused flesh to gather and become ingrown should have a thin probe placed beneath it, lifting it upwards, and then this flesh should be cut away gently.
And apply caustic medicines on the remaining flesh until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the decay has affected the bone, cut away that bone and remove it, for it will never heal as long as there is putrid bone in it.
If you find that gangrene and decay are spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of its joints as mentioned earlier, then treat it until it heals. But if the nail receives a blow or wound causing severe pain, do not lance the boil at first.
Then cut the nail with a sharp pointed instrument in an oblique manner from top to bottom, making sure not to reach the flesh underneath the nail. This would cause severe pain for the patient and lead to excessive growth of flesh at that spot. Then treat the place with something that relieves pain.
As for the extra finger that appears in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or there may be bones and nails within it. Sometimes its growth is at the base of a joint between fingers.
Or its growth may be at one of the joints of the finger, and those that grow at the joint do not move, while those that grow at the base of the finger might move. If it is only flesh, cut it off easily at its root with a wide pointed instrument.
But those that grow at the root of a joint are difficult to treat and should not be cut off. But if it grows on one of the joints of the finger, first cut away the surrounding flesh in a circular manner towards the bone, then chip away the bone with one of those chisels suited for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals, but as for the fusion that affects the fingers together, this often occurs and is usually congenital, resulting from the merging of a wound or burn. Therefore, do not cut into that fusion.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then place between them a cloth or linen soaked in rose oil so that they do not fuse quickly and are separated, or insert a thin sheet of lead between them until the site heals properly.
Similarly, if fusion affects some fingers at the palm, cut through that fusion according to what is feasible and beneficial for the shape of the member.
The Ninety-First Chapter.
In cutting off the testicles.
Varicose veins are twisted, hard, swollen veins filled with black fluid that occur more commonly in the body's limbs, particularly around the feet, ankles, and calves. First, vigorously shake the body to expel the black matter.
Then apply a bandage to the patient's leg, and regarding treatment with iron, there are two methods: either incise and remove the black blood, or cauterize the vein and remove it entirely. If cutting is chosen, first warm the leg with hot water.
Until the thick, coagulated blood liquefies, then bind the healthy leg above its thigh down to below the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, two, or three places with wide incisions.
Then squeeze out the black blood from below the leg towards the top, and from the top towards the bottom until you remove as much blood as is sufficient or as much as the patient can tolerate. Then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that cause hemorrhoids, and repeat evacuation and bandaging.
If the veins become filled and cause harm to the patient, then treat it as follows: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then enter him into a hot bath until the leg becomes reddened and the vein becomes visible or swollen due to vigorous exercise.
If he does not prepare a bath to warm the limb, then cut the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or below it near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and scrape away the vein from both sides until it becomes visible to touch.
When it appears, you will see it red and bloody. Once freed from the skin, it will appear like a cord. Then insert a probe beneath it until, when raised and brought out of the skin, it is suspended by a smooth blind forceps. Then cut another incision near that one about three fingers wide.
Then scrape the skin off the vein until it becomes visible. Then raise it with the probe as before and suspend it with another forceps as before, or cut another incision or several incisions if necessary.
Then tie and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull and tie it until it comes out of the second incision. Then pull it up to the incision above it, doing this until you pull it out from all incisions. Once completely out, cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and tying, then insert a strong doubled thread needle and tie it. Then pull it out while inserting the probe beneath it and rotating your hand in both directions until it comes out. Be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be difficult to tie it.
After tying it all, place on the wound sites wool soaked in a solution of wax or rose oil or olive oil. Treat him until he recovers. If the vein is twisted and has branches with turns towards different directions.
If it is not straight as we mentioned, then do not cut near each of its branches and points of torsion. Suspend them with forceps until you tie them all. Be careful when cutting not to cut or injure the vein, for this will make tying difficult.
Image of the incision used to tie the vein:
Image of the Blind Needle.
Unlike other needles, it should not have a head nor be sharp-pointed lest it damage the nerve. It should be thick and smooth because if it is thin it may cut the nerve.
Chapter One Hundred And Ninety.
In sewing the median nerve.
This nerve originates in the legs, especially in hot countries such as Hijaz and other Arab lands with little rainfall. It may also originate elsewhere on the body besides the legs, due to humoral imbalance occurring beneath the skin.
As it is said, this nerve also arises from the inside of living creatures such as snakes and worms, and from lice and other parasites generated between skin and flesh. The beginning of its occurrence is indicated by severe pain in the leg, followed by swelling at that spot, then the nerve emerges from the site of that swelling.
It seems like a plant or animal root. When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing between one and two dirhams and tied, leaving the lead suspended from the leg.
Whatever comes out of it, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it becomes too long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder, but do not cut it from its origin before it has all come out, lest you cause contraction, entrance into flesh, inflammation, and bad ulceration at the site.
Therefore, it should be handled and pulled out little by little until it all comes out of the body. Sometimes this nerve can come out to a length of five cubits (approx. 2.5 meters) and ten spans.
I have heard that in one case, it came out to a length of twenty cubits (approx. 10 meters). If it breaks during treatment, insert a reed into its hole and bind it with a long bandage to the body until all matter is emptied from it. Then try to heal the site with drugs for several days.
Then treat it as a tumor. This nerve may have many branches, especially if it appears at the knee joint or the thigh itself, with multiple mouths from which numerous branches emerge. Treat each branch separately according to what has been mentioned earlier.
Chapter Two Hundred and Ninety
In the fissure, there occurs a worm that is generated under the liver, known as the disease of oxen.
This disease is called in some of our lands the disease of oxen because it often affects oxen; it is a small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, moving throughout the body both upwards and downwards.
It can be perceived by feeling her movement from organ to organ until she ruptures the skin at some point and emerges, formed from the corruption of some humors like other worms, animals, and moles in the belly.
Her harm is expected to be such that if she moves through the body, rises up to the head, and reaches the eye, perhaps she will open it and come out, thereby destroying the eye; this happens often. If one wishes to treat her and remove her, then it is only possible when her movement can be perceived.
It is advisable to tie what is above and below her with a strong band, then cut upon her and remove her. If she has buried herself in the flesh and cannot be found, cauterize the place with fire until she is burnt. Her greatest harm is expected to be to the eye, as mentioned earlier.
But if she has reached the head near the eye, tie below her tightly on the forehead, then cut upon her and remove her. It is advisable for the patient to treat himself by purging his body of corrupt humors with laxative drugs and avoiding foods that generate corruption.
Chapter Three Hundred and Ninety
In the fissure, concerning the disease known as the napfer.
This disease called in our land 'the Napfer' is a pain that appears in some organs, then moves from organ to organ; I have seen it as described.
I was called to a woman who was ill in some of the towns, and she uncovered her arm for me; I saw a slight swelling in the vein of her forearm. After a while, I saw that swelling moving with the vein like a worm upwards towards her shoulder blade as swiftly as quicksilver.
If it moved from one place to another, the pain left that spot and settled in her shoulder blade. Then after a short time, it flowed throughout her body until it reached her other arm; she told me that her whole body was rotating as I had seen.
I was amazed by the swiftness of its passage from one member to another, for I had never before seen this disease manifest itself in such a manner until I saw this group suffering from pain that moved from one limb to another; nor had I ever witnessed it with my own eyes as I did in this woman.
I did not deem it possible except that the woman was from the desert-dwelling people, having a dry body and visible veins; hence, the swift movement of the pain became apparent. It is unlikely to appear in this manner among those who live in comfort with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, if it appears visibly as mentioned earlier, then promptly compress above and below it, and incise until that congested wind breaks forth; then cauterize.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by shaking the body and using remedies that disperse wind and break up congestion, such as ground rhubarb and colocynth, and similar drugs.
Chapter Four and Ninety.
On Removing Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and the locations in the body where they strike, and as for their differing types, some are large while others are small; some have hollow glass tips while others have solid ones; still others have three points or four.
And some have tongues while others have splinters. As for those that strike vital organs, they fall into two categories: either the organ is hollow, such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar ones; When an arrow strikes one of these organs and you observe the signs of impending death that I will describe later, it is not permissible to refrain from removing that arrow from it, for death usually ensues. But if these signs of ill omen do not appear, nor is the arrow hidden deep within the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. Signs of a brain injury include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting, if the arrow penetrates the bone and injures the membrane covering the brain.
But if these signs of ill omen do not appear, nor is the arrow hidden deep within the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. Signs of a brain injury include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting, if the arrow penetrates the bone and injures the membrane covering the brain. And redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, spasms, loss of consciousness, and vomiting may occur. Blood might issue from the nostrils or ears, speech might be lost and voice impaired. A white, watery fluid resembling gruel may emerge from the wound, accompanied by a meaty serum-like discharge.
If these signs appear, desist from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not yet exited; as for the signs of an arrow, when it hits the heart being near the left breast and feels as if it has pierced something solid not something hollow.
Perhaps the arrow has a pulsating motion, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know that death is inevitable in such case; and if the lung is wounded, frothy blood will issue from the wound.
If the vessels following the neck become swollen and the patient's color changes, with rapid breathing and a desire to inhale cold air, know that if the arrow has pierced the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the small ribs, causing intense pain.
And all the members of the trunk sigh and move, and if the liver is pierced, intense pain follows with blood resembling liver in color issuing from the wound; and if the stomach is pierced, some undigested food may issue from the wound.
It is evident that if the arrow hits the abdomen and some excrement or intestines protrude from the wound, there is no hope of recovery nor removal of the arrow. If the bladder is pierced, urine will flow.
And if something protrudes from it externally and the pain becomes intense, know that the patient is doomed. As for other members such as the face, eye, neck, shoulder, arm, back, thigh, leg, etc., they are generally easier to deal with.
When the arrow does not hit an artery or vein and is not poisoned, I shall tell you of some cases that I have witnessed concerning such arrows for your guidance: Once an arrow struck a man in the eye socket at the root of his nose.
I removed it from him through the ear on the other side, and his eye remained unharmed. Another Jew had an arrow struck under his eyelid, with only its small tip visible.
A large iron-tipped, square-pointed arrow of composite material was extracted from the Jew's thigh. There was no ear injury and no evil occurrence in his eye. Another arrow, an Arab one, was removed from the neck of a Christian.
A second arrow, having two ears, was extracted from between the shoulders of a Christian. It had become lodged in his throat and was gently drawn out until it was removed. The Christian survived and was healed. Another arrow was extracted from a man's stomach; we thought he would die from it.
After about thirty days, without any change in his condition, the arrow was removed and he recovered. No misfortune befell him. I saw another man struck by an arrow in the back; the wound became inflamed.
After seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his thigh. I saw a woman struck by an arrow in her belly; the wound became inflamed but the arrow remained and did not cause her any harm or interfere with her natural activities.
I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face; the wound became inflamed but caused him little pain, and there are many like this. I also extracted an arrow from a soldier of the sultan's guard that had struck him in the middle of his nose, slightly deviating towards the right side.
The entire arrow had disappeared, so I was summoned to treat him three days after its removal. I found the arrow wound very narrow; I dilated it with a thin probe and he felt no pain. However, he complained of discharge and pain beneath his right ear from the wound.
I hoped that the discharge was from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a medicated dressing with drawing power and waited eagerly for the place to swell and show signs of the arrow. But when I incised him, nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had reached that spot.
So I continued the dressing for many days without any result. Then the wound closed over, and the patient was distressed at his inability to expel it until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me. Then I applied a strong, eating medicine for several days.
Until the wound opened and I explored it, I felt the tip of the arrow's thin shaft adhering to the bone. Then I increased the incision with that sharp medication until the tip of the arrow became visible to us, and it remained with me for a period of about four months.
When the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I tried to pull and move the arrow, but it did not yield. So I continued to soften it and manipulate it with various instruments until one day I securely grasped it with forceps, as shown at the end of this book.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose cannot be restored, but I straightened it and the wound adhered and the patient healed completely without any harm whatsoever. And I inform you of how some arrows are removed to make this a criterion and an example for what has not been mentioned.
For the details of this craft and its particulars cannot be grasped by description nor can they be comprehended from a book. Only the skilled artisan can measure the small by the large, and what is present by what is absent, and infer new works and new instruments for unusual emergencies.
If one masters this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the organs into which they have penetrated in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry or from the opposite side. Those that are extracted from where they entered.
Either the arrow is prominent at a point of inflammation and can be pulled out without delay; if it does not yield, one should leave it alone until the surrounding flesh decays and becomes easier to grasp and extract. Similarly, if an arrow has lodged in bone.
If it does not yield to extraction, leave it alone for a while and then attempt to pull and manipulate it daily; if after several days it still does not yield, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone on all sides with a fine awl until the space around the arrow is widened, then grasp and extract it.
If an arrow has lodged in the skull and extended into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of the symptoms mentioned have appeared in the patient, do not attempt to extract the arrow; leave it alone until the matter clarifies after a few days.
In drawing out the arrow and removing it, if it is deeply embedded and does not yield to traction, use the drill around the arrow as I have described for you, then treat the area until it heals. But if the arrow has disappeared into a part of the body. And if it has disappeared from sensation, search for it with the probe. If you feel it, draw it out using some of the instruments suitable for this purpose. But if you are unable to do so due to the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow's penetration, and there is no bone, nerve or vein in its path.
Then cut him open until the wound is enlarged and you can grasp the arrow and draw it out. If the arrow has two barbs holding it, free the flesh they are embedded in from all sides as much as possible. If you cannot do so by breaking off the barbs and twisting them out, then use any means available to free yourself.
And when you attempt to draw out the arrow from any location, twist your hand with forceps in all directions until it is freed, taking care not to break the arrow as this will make extraction difficult. But if you cannot do so after a time.
Leave it for some time until the flesh surrounding it becomes gangrenous, then return to it as extraction will be easier at that point. If you encounter bleeding during this process, use the treatment mentioned earlier in its chapter. Take great care not to cut any vein, nerve or tendon, and use caution.
Free the arrow using every possible means, but do so gently and carefully, as I have described to you. When drawing out the arrow, ensure that the patient remains in the same position they were in when it was struck.
But if you cannot do this, use whatever means are available. As for the arrow exiting from the opposite side, either some of it will be protruding outside or you will feel its tip through the skin near its exit point.
It opened on him, let it be opened to the extent that the forceps can fit inside, then pull it out; if it sticks to the bone, twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and makes space for itself, then pull it out.
If not, leave it alone until it comes out on its own. If the shaft of the arrow is still inside, use it to push it out; if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use a pushing tool, insert the hollow instrument into the tip of the arrow then use it to push.
If the arrow is hollow, use a tool that fits into its cavity to push it out; this will make its exit easier. If the arrowhead is poisoned, do not cauterize the flesh where all of the poison has accumulated if possible, then treat it with what is appropriate for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder or flank and is close to what can be felt with the percussion hammer, and if you can open it, do so; carefully avoid cutting any veins or nerves, then remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary, treat it until it heals. Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:
The ends are shaped like the beak of a bird, crafted such that when they grasp an arrow or other object, they do not release it. They can be made in various sizes: large, small, and medium, according to the size of the arrowhead, its thinness or thickness, and the width or narrowness of the wound.

Image of the solid instrument:
This has a blunt tip like a pestle, making it easy to insert into and push through a hollow arrow.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
On the letting of blood vessels.
The vessels that are usually let blood from the body, numbering thirty: of which sixteen are in the head, including the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the temporal veins and the two arteries visible on the temples; also the two veins at the angles of the eyes known as the nazar veins.
The vein standing in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins on either side of the neck, and the two veins on either side of the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins beneath the tongue.
And the veins that are cut in the arm and hand are five: one is the Qifal, which is on the lateral side and is commonly called the vein of the head; the Akhal, which is the middle vein composed of a branch of the Basilic vein and a branch of the Qifal, and is commonly called the vein of the body.
The Basilic vein, which is on the medial side and is also called the axillary vein; it is commonly known as the vein of the abdomen and the arm cord, which lies on the forearm and appears clearly above the thumb; and the Asilum.
And the vein between the index and middle fingers has two branches; in the leg and foot there are three veins: one is beneath the knee on the lateral side, the second is the saphenous vein located at the heel on the medial side, and the third is the nerve vein located at the ankle on the lateral side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them. And as for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is in the treatment of chronic fevers, migraine, and head sores; and how to cauterize them will be described.
It is necessary to shave the head of the patient and rub his nape with a rough cloth at the location of the veins, then choke him with a cloth until the veins appear behind the ears in their lower positions on the head; then search for them with your finger and where you feel their pulsation underneath your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a sharp lancet, known as the Neshil, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone; then lift your hand with the vein and skin above, and cut the vein along with the skin in an everted manner, the length of the cut being about two fingerbreadths.
After releasing the desired amount of blood, then tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal. As for the arteries in the temples, severing them is beneficial for chronic headaches, severe migraines, and persistent eye inflammation accompanied by intense discharge.
To locate them, have the patient tie a cloth around their neck until the pulse of both arteries becomes clearly visible and palpable under your finger. Then, using a scalpel, gently raise the skin above each artery from top to bottom with your index finger.
