And inserting the scalpel underneath, raise the artery upwards and cut it as you did with the other arteries. Release a sufficient amount of blood, then loosen the patient's neckcloth and place your finger on the artery for a moment.
Then apply a bandage and compress, tie it firmly from above, and leave it until healed. The mentioning of these arteries and their cutting and ligating has been discussed in the first book. As for cupping the frontal vein, its benefit is for chronic facial complaints such as boils, ulcers, and blackheads.
And to locate it, have the patient tie a cloth around their neck until the vein becomes visible. Then take the instrument called 'al-Fas' (the axe), which looks like this:
Place the protruding spike at the head of al-Fas on the vein, strike it from above with a comb or similar object, allowing blood to flow according to your desire. Then loosen the patient's neckcloth and bind it until healed.
It is permissible to use a wide-bladed scalpel, but it should not be too sharp-pointed like other scalpels. Its edge should be slightly blunted to avoid damaging the nearby bone, as the blade may break if it is thin.
And the two veins at the angles of the eyes are beneficial for eye diseases such as scurf, redness, and inflammation, as well as facial diseases. To locate them, have the patient tie a cloth around their neck while you stand above their head and cut along a slight diagonal using a small, wide-bladed scalpel.
If the place has no flesh in it, then if the lancet is thin-pointed it may break. Send blood until satiated and apply a bandage. Leave them overnight and remove them. As for cupping of the nose to draw off blood, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And for severe headache and facial diseases such as the red boil appearing on the nose, especially if chronic, the method of treating it is: Have the patient tilt their head back. Then, using your left hand, grasp the patient's nose while holding a thin, long lancet in your right hand.
And insert it into the center of the nostril itself between the partitions of the nose, for the vein does not appear there by touch. Blood will issue immediately. It is necessary to press your hand on the lancet a little and send blood until satiated, then bind it overnight as it heals quickly.
And as for the buboes, their cupping is beneficial for shortness of breath and the onset of leprosy, and black diseases that appear on the skin's surface such as black patches, boils, and bad sores. The method of treating them is: Have the patient tilt their head back while seated, then have the cupping practitioner stand at the patient's head.
And the patient sits on a chair. Then draw blood from both sides for a wide cupping, then bleed them moderately or according to what you see is needed. Then do likewise with the other vein and remove the band.
And tie both veins moderately so the patient does not choke, and leave them overnight as the wound will heal. And as for the veins on the legs, their cupping is beneficial after cupping the head, as it helps with mouth ulcers, tooth decay, gum disease, cracked lips, and bad sores.
That appear on the nose and around it, and the method of treating them is: Have the patient sit facing you and tie a scarf around their neck. Then pull back their lips and look for the veins which one appears on the right side of the lip and the other on the left side. Then bleed from their darkness.
Similarly, around them are small black veins that should be cut off. If you are unsure about this, go for the largest and most obvious one; do likewise for the two veins in the lower lip.
And as for the two veins underneath the tongue, it is beneficial to cauterize them after treating the lymph nodes in the throat for diseases of the uvula and mouth. The method of cauterizing them is: have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and look beneath it on one side for a vein and on the other side for another vein, both appearing dark.
Cauterize them and be cautious not to linger in cutting them, for underneath them are arteries. Thus, excessive bleeding may occur from those arteries. As for the three veins cauterized at the elbow, it is customary to do so in most people, and cauterization occurs on both sides.
Either cauterize with a wide, curved or olive-shaped iron heated over a fire, or cut with a scalpel. Here is the shape of the wide, curved iron:
It should be wide as shown to fit the purpose of opening swollen, filled veins that are prominently visible and contain thick, dark blood. Here is the shape of the olive-shaped iron:
This scalpel is narrower with a sharper point, suitable for cauterizing thin veins containing yellowish blood. Here is the shape of the scalpel:
This scalpel suitable for cutting comes in various widths and thinnesses according to the size of the veins, and it can be used to infer others; it is well-known among craftsmen.
And of these three veins, cauterizing the basilica vein is beneficial as it draws blood from diseases under the throat and neck leading to the chest and abdomen. The cauterizer should be careful and leave some part on the neck because underneath it lies an artery. If one errs and pushes the iron too deep, it may cut that artery.
So cauterizing it with a heated iron should not be done, but rather cut with a scalpel. If the basilica vein is not clearly visible, avoid it and proceed to another one or ask for some of its branches.
And if you want to tie off its place, it will be clear, and cut it with the fine scalpel as mentioned earlier. But if you want to tie it specifically at that location, before tying up the arm, examine the site until you recognize the pulse, then mark its location with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein with a fine scalpel as mentioned earlier, ensuring that the cut is away from the artery's location. When you see swelling at the site marked with ink upon tightening the ligature, know that it is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see blood gushing like a boy's urine, and it is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. In this case, quickly press your finger on it for some time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, which happens often, then tie up the arm.
And leave him, warning the patient not to neglect it. Let it rest on his neck without moving until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are unable to control it with medication, ligate the artery if its ends become constricted and the bleeding stops.
Or take a skinny needle and split it, taking one half and tying it around the vein tightly with thread and sutures for another day. If bleeding stops, then treat it as mentioned earlier using catgut ligatures to stop bleeding; otherwise, cauterize its blood supply.
Know that if the wound is small and the ligature on the arm is secure, it is beneficial to tie off the dark-colored vein because it draws blood from above the head and below the body since it consists of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein as mentioned earlier.
It is necessary for the ligator to be on guard against it, as there is a nerve below it. If the scalpel goes too deep and hits the nerve, numbness will occur which may not recover entirely, although this nerve is sometimes palpable.
If it is not palpable in some people and thin so that it cannot be seen, then make your incision with a fine scalpel avoiding the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, cut it lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, its ligation is beneficial because it drains blood from the head.
Eye diseases can be benefited from this procedure, especially if you wish to separate it; use the olive-pointed scissors or the wide laurel-pointed scissors because all vessels are sound since there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when performing phlebotomy, you should avoid cutting the muscle's head with the scissors and seek the soft spot. It is not harmful if it is not struck on the first attempt; phlebotomy can be repeated until success is achieved, although in some people, swelling may occur if it is not struck on the first attempt.
But that swelling causes no harm, and as for the manner of phlebotomy, its complications, and what should be done to repair it, know first that phlebotomy is either used solely for preserving health.
And it is either used for diseases, and what is used for preserving and maintaining health and preventing the occurrence of diseases is that phlebotomy should be performed in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, basilica, or median cubital vein, and it should be done at the beginning of spring if signs of plethora appear.
And phlebotomy should be performed on a Sunday or Wednesday after noon has passed three hours, but for the phlebotomy used in diseases, there is no limited time; it can be done anytime day or night as needed.
And at every hour and time, except for children until they reach fourteen years old and elderly people who have exceeded sixty years of age. If anyone intends to undergo phlebotomy.
It is necessary to cleanse the stomach before phlebotomy with a gentle purgative if there is much retained food, so that no offensive matter from the stomach is drawn into the vessels during phlebotomy. Do not perform phlebotomy on the drunkard or intoxicated until that state has passed.
And phlebotomy should also be avoided after an attack of fever, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, fatigue, exercise, sleeplessness, and fasting. Then examine the thickness of the humors before that to see if the blood is thick.
If possible, he should consume foods, drinks, and drugs. Then he enters the bathhouse if nothing prevents him or engages in some exercise to make his blood flow freely and prepare for surgery as previously mentioned. He should aim to cleanse his mind that day from all negative emotional distractions.
He should also rid himself of physical afflictions like excessive fatigue and intercourse, as well as mental disturbances such as grief, anger, fear, etc. He should prepare the necessary items according to common practice, such as perfumes, incense, lamps, etc., to the best of his ability.
The surgeon sits on a cushion higher than the one for the patient. He then takes out his arm, washes it twice or thrice, ties the bandage with the tourniquet and twists it twice or thrice, ensuring that the tension is moderate.
For if the tension is not moderate, either excessive tightening will prevent blood flow, or too much slackness will also hinder it. After tying, the surgeon should rub his hands together to warm them.
Until the veins swell and become visible. Then, the surgeon wipes the knife with a little old oil, especially, before placing his left hand's index finger gently on the vein beneath the point he intends to cut, so that the vein does not slip away from the blow. Some veins may slip away like a bowstring during cutting, while others may be filled with wind, causing the knife to sink into them and deceive the surgeon without opening the vein or opening it only narrowly.
Therefore, the surgeon should be steadfast and cautious in all these matters. If he cuts and opens the vein with one stroke, well and good. Otherwise, he should repeat the stroke beneath or above that point quickly if there is no swelling.
If the patient bleeds or swells, leave him for a day or two without tightening the bandage, as it may cause hot inflammation. Do not let him enter the bathhouse. If he wishes to continue with the operation, and if the knife went deep but the opening was small and the blood flow was thin...
I feared that the intended amount of blood might not come out, so I prepared the lancet in the same hole and moved it straight forward, adding a little more to the opening. Do this swiftly before the area swells, for many people experience swelling even with a small incision.
If it has swollen, do not resort to it again for it will be of no use. Apply some castor oil on it instead; it facilitates blood flow and is better than any other oil or fat in this context.
Use castor oil in all bloodletting procedures where blood flow is obstructed. The medication Farouk al-Tariq and Shakuniza can also be applied to the site, as they help liquefy thick blood.
If a large swelling occurs at the bloodletting site, especially if the person has not been bled before or if the vein was opened barely, quickly apply a warm compress of saltwater-soaked sponge and hold it in place for some time to reduce the swelling.
It is preferable to do this after the blood has completely flowed out of the vein or another one. Any remaining discoloration or greenness at the site after a few days is not harmful. If desired, you may apply a solution of myrrh and frankincense.
Or you may use the juice of fennel or similar substances. Swelling and discoloration often occur during bloodletting from the basilic vein; if it feels soft when pressed, it indicates harmful swelling, so avoid applying any of the mentioned remedies.
If blood from an artery is released, tie off the site with a forceps to arrest bleeding and then treat it like any other wound until it heals. Each person should have their blood let according to their constitution and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red. If it is thick, allow it to become thin. If it is sharp, let its edge subside. For robust and strong individuals who require a significant amount of bloodletting at once...
He should expand the veins and use a broad lancet. If it is weak, then its opposite should be used. It is preferable to draw it out repeatedly with a narrow opening. The best tool for bloodletting is a polished, sharp-edged one without a guard.
This method of bloodletting is safe from excessive bleeding and nerve cutting. It is easier than crosswise or lengthwise bloodletting, especially if the patient has hairy skin; in such cases, it is preferable to feed him bread soaked in pomegranate water before bloodletting.
If the lancet is polished and blood has been drawn out three or four times, then if the patient's temperament is cold, he should be given bread soaked in syrup of pomegranate juice, honey syrup with saffron, or fragrant rose syrup before bloodletting.
However, if hair growth occurs during bloodletting due to excessive bleeding, then water of meat broth and diluted fragrant rose syrup should be given. Perfume with incense should be applied on his chest, and all the aforementioned methods in the chapter on hirsutism should be used.
For purging, the blood should be let out gradually, little by little according to one's strength on consecutive days.
For those who want to purge and let blood again, with a strong body, this should be done after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting. For those who wish to draw blood away from a particular side, they should wait two or three days before letting blood again.
However, if there is a lot of blood in his body that has become heated and inflamed causing fever, then the blood should not be drawn out all at once. Instead, it should be taken out gradually until hair growth appears after ensuring that all conditions for bloodletting are met. And place your hand on his pulse during bloodletting to prevent death in place of hair growth, as this often happens due to ignorance or negligence of the phlebotomist. This should not be done if you intend to free the arm and let blood again.
The mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of blood is difficult. Do not press upon it forcefully or twist it violently, for this would be harmful indeed. Either allow it to open again on its own, or gently remove with a scalpel what has congealed at the mouth of the vein.
Or hold upon it a little salt dissolved in water, or some of the drug Farouk or Shakhshinaj, and gently press until blood comes out. If the vein is swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity requires opening the vein again, either do so above that spot or in another arm or vein.
As for cupping the arm vein, use instead a mixture of Akhzal and Basilaq if they are not available or hard to find, as it is composed of them both. To perform cupping, place your hand in hot water until the skin reddens and the vein becomes visible. Then tie a bandage around it with moderate tightness.
Then cupping should be done at a slight angle, neither horizontally nor vertically. The opening should be wide and the cupping should be performed slightly above the wrist joint. If blood does not flow out, place the hand back in the hot water bowl and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Continue until you achieve your purpose. In summer, you may not need to return the hand to the hot water bowl, while in winter, blood flow into the water is more likely. Cupping at this vein is preferable to cupping any other vein. For beneath it there is no pulsating vein or nerve. Cupping at the Aslim vein on the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments. To perform cupping, tie the wrist with a bandage or press it with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein engorges.
For beneath it there is no pulsating vein or nerve. Cupping at the Aslim vein on the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments. To perform cupping, tie the wrist with a bandage or press it with your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein engorges. It will become very apparent to the touch, then cup it at a slight angle. Even if you cut completely around it, it will cause no harm. Be careful not to probe too deeply with the scalpel, as underneath there is a nerve and tendons are exposed. Then return the hand to the hot water.
Let it bleed until blood flows freely. Then, immerse it in warm water to prevent the blood from clotting at the mouth of the vein and blocking its flow. Once enough blood has been removed, apply grease and salt to the vein to prevent it from sticking quickly.
And similarly, you should do with every narrow vein. As for the benefit of cupping from the left hand's side, it is useful for treating spleen ailments, and you should perform cupping in this manner as you did previously on the second side. And as for cupping on the right side, its benefit is for lower body diseases such as uterine ailments.
And for retention of menses, kidney diseases, chronic sores on the thighs and legs, and similar ailments. The method of cupping is to place his leg in warm water, apply pressure with the rubbing tool until the veins appear, then tie it above the ankle joint where the vein emerges near the heel.
And many veins branch out on the face, so cup at its largest vein or at the heel where they converge, for that is better and safer. If you cup on the face, be careful not to touch the nerves underneath.
And make your cupping for him like this: pretend as if you are about to cut him, using a dull blade. If blood does not come out, place his leg back in warm water and let the blood flow until it is emptied. If the cupper mistakes the vein on the first attempt.
So move the cup upwards slightly for the second attempt. The safe place has no harm if you are careful about the nerve, as mentioned before. And do the same with cupping on the other leg, equally. As for the vein of the thigh, its location is as previously mentioned, at the back of the outer side.
And the benefit of cupping is for hip pain if it is caused by hot blood. And its method: have the patient enter the bath quickly, then tie his thigh from below the buttock to above the heel with a thin, long cloth strip, and you will see veins appearing only like this.
When the veins appear, cup at any visible vein. Either on a false cut or with a real cut using a scalpel or incising knife. The safe place is usually quite faint, so if you cannot find it.
And there appeared to the touch not at all, so cut some of its branches, those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little finger and the index finger, being careful of the nerves, and let go only as much blood as you wish.
Then release the tension and apply a piece of gauze to the site of the incision and secure it. The wound will heal quickly.
Chapter Sixty-Nine
On Cupping (Hijamah) and its Method of Use
Cupping vessels can be made from horn, wood, copper or glass. There are two types of cupping: one with cauterization and bloodletting, and another without cauterization.
And this cupping [without cauterization] is of two kinds.
And cupping can also be done on the sides of the neck, under the lower jaw, on the shoulders, on the upper back near the buttocks, on the insides of the arms, on the thighs, and on the calves.
Cupping draws blood from the superficial vessels beneath the skin, therefore it does not cause weakness like bloodletting. It should not be used with fire or without fire in any disease that is accompanied by fullness until the entire body has been purged.
If cupping is necessary due to illness or custom, it should be performed at the beginning, middle, or end of the month, at any time.
Some people have an excess of blood in their bodies, causing heaviness and headaches when it needs to be removed by cupping. Others experience fullness, redness, and heat in the face, head, and neck. Still others experience dryness, darkness, and inflammation in the eyes or itching at the site of cupping.
And some people may laugh excessively, while others may taste blood in their mouths or have blood on their teeth and gums. Some may experience excessive sleep, while others may see visions of blood, redness, wounds, and injuries [in their dreams].
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we should then prescribe cupping at that time after two or three hours have passed during the day. The benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that it helps relieve heaviness in the head and prevents water from building up in the eyes.
However, it is preferable that this should come after the evacuation of a large quantity of phlegm from the body. This cupping may also be used instead of venesection of the temples, but it should not be employed on those with cold temperaments or who are suffering from an illness, for it would cause them great harm.
It is therefore not advisable to use this (cupping) on the elderly, those with cold-headedness, or those who have become accustomed to it to the point that they forget.
Therefore, it is recommended to command the cupping practitioner to lower his hand slightly with the cup to avoid causing fainting. As for cupping on the nape of the neck, it serves as an alternative to phlebotomy on the temples and behind the ear.
Therefore, it benefits those with asthma, shortness of breath, headache caused by difficulty breathing, and coughing. However, one should raise the cupping vessel on the nape slightly, for if it is placed too low and causes suction, it may weaken the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping on the temples, it benefits from sharp pains in the head, inflammation of the eyes, migraine, congestion, and pain in the roots of teeth. It serves as an alternative to phlebotomy behind the ear, and one should command the cupping practitioner not to insert the needle too deeply lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
And cupping below the collarbone benefits from ulcers in the mouth, tooth decay, and similar diseases of the mouth. It can also serve as a substitute for phlebotomy on the veins of the lips. As for cupping on the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations caused by fullness or heat.
As for cupping on the lower abdomen near the hip bones, it benefits from what phlebotomy on the three veins of the temple, eyebrow, and forehead benefits, for it attracts blood from all the superficial vessels in that area and attracts those vessels from deeper ones until it reaches the three deep veins.
It is necessary for the barber to avoid examining the site as it is bare of flesh and beneath it lie nerves and vessels. The single cautery applied to the sciatic nerve benefits hemorrhoids and anal ulcers.
It is necessary to order the barber that the cautery be large and made of copper because the site requires strong heat and perhaps a glass cautery may break, causing many cauterizations. The cauteries for the legs should be less filled in order to avoid excessive swelling.
For it attracts blood from the entire body, benefits chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, regulates menstruation, treats boils and skin diseases, and acts as a substitute for cupping and leeching, except that it greatly weakens the body and often causes dizziness. The cauteries for leeches are similar to those used for bloodletting from the legs.
To apply the cautery, first place it empty and draw out a moderate amount of blood. Do not prolong the application of the cautery but place and remove it quickly to allow the humors to enter the site evenly and repeatedly until you see that the site has turned red and opened up.
When the redness of blood appears, then cauterize and gradually decrease suction. Afterwards, examine the patient's condition; if they have loose flesh with open pores, it is sufficient to cauterize once only, lest the site become necrotic.
Order the barber to widen and deepen the cautery slightly, regulate suction gently with slight movement. If there is thick blood present, cauterize twice: first to open a path for thin blood and watery fluid, second to ensure complete removal of thick blood.
If the blood is extremely thick, cauterize a third time to complete the procedure. In general, one cautery is sufficient for removing a small amount of blood, but more cauteries are needed for a large quantity of blood, especially if the blood is thick.
It is necessary to make a deep incision and moderate in the case of shallow skin depth. No ointment should be used when applying bandages, nor water or anything else that might harm the patient. Those with thick, hard, tough skin and small pores should be particularly careful.
It is necessary to anoint the places of bandages with open, ground, melted ointments. Especially in summer time, such as henna ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond ointment or cucumber seed oil should be used.
And in winter, ointments such as narcissus or crocus or papaya or saffron and the like should be used. If the weather is cold and dry, olive oil or sesame oil or butter or beeswax and the like should be used.
If the patient has large pores and soft flesh, ointment should be avoided. These patients should wash their bandages after cupping with rose water or cold water or raven's grape juice or cucumber juice or pomegranate juice and the like. But those whose blood is moist should avoid these waters.
They should wash their bandages with vinegar or water of oak and sumac and the like. But those whose skin is thick should wash their bandages with old wine or olive oil or beeswax paste or papaya paste and the like. Cauterization should be avoided in the bathhouse and after bathing.
Rather, cauterization should be performed two hours after leaving the bathhouse. No one should sleep immediately after cauterization. The patient and the cauterizer must not eat or drink before or after cauterization.
First, check if the patient or cauterizer is jaundiced. If there is sharpness and inflammation in their blood, they should take cooling agents such as pomegranate and hibiscus with vinegar, chicory and purslane, and make their diet consist of fruits, lamb meat, and chickpeas and the like.
He whose temperament is cold, it is advisable that he drink honey water and gruel of cooked barley or Bazoori Sunkin. He should consume medium-aged perfumed wine which is between old and new. Let his food be limited and make his diet partridges and quails, sparrows, and young pigeons. On the day of cupping and bloodletting, let drink exceed food.
It may be advisable for some people to drink the Farouq Tiryak or Musk Dawa or Shiltha medication before cupping and before bloodletting, or afterwards, to strengthen the main organs and relax the blood. It should not be given to the intoxicated.
The cuppings that are done without restriction are those placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, heart, kidneys and hip socket, as these organs cannot tolerate restriction. Rather, they are intended to draw blood from one organ to another.
When we place the cupping on the breasts in cases of engorgement or use it to remove cold wind from an organ, we also place it on the abdomen and heart. This clears the organ, warms it, and alleviates pain by releasing that cold wind.
Cupping may be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stones present. With sufficient drawing, it might open the blockage or dislodge the stone from its place. Similarly, it can be used on the liver and spleen for cold wind that disrupts them. These cuppings are typically performed using only suction.
Cupping can also be performed with fire or using water. For ailments like shoussha, fill the cupping vessel with warm water alone or water boiled with certain herbs suitable for that purpose.
Then place it on the desired location, secure it, and remove it repeatedly according to need. This is the method of cupping used with fire:
The mouth of the cupping vessel should be two fingerbreadths open and half a cubit deep. On one side, there should be a small hole large enough for an awl to pass through, made of Chinese or yellow copper with thick rims.
A flat, smooth cup is prepared so that it may not harm the member when placed upon it. In its center there is a hollow made of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed. This cup can be made large or small according to the diseases.
If you wish to use it, then the cups of boys and children are not like those of men. And as for how this cup is placed with fire on the member, it is that a tightly wound cotton wick or small candle made of wax is ignited and placed in the center of the cup's hollow.
So that the fire may rise upwards within the cup, and not burn the patient's body. Then it is placed on the member, with a finger on the hole mentioned until we secure the cup; then we remove our finger.
And the vapor comes out through that hole, and the cup adheres to its place. Then the wick is extinguished according to how it was lit, and relit if necessary. But the cup used in treating dropsy with water has no solid tube or hole.
But it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only, and its appearance is:
And the larger this cup is to hold much water, the better.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On treating hemorrhoids.
The cautery is used in most cases on members where cups cannot be placed, such as those that are too small like the lip and gum, or those that are bare of flesh like the finger and nose.
And its use is that you take a cautery from among those that are in fresh, pure water of the fountains, and leave it for one day and night in fresh water until it becomes hungry and nothing remains in its belly. Then the body is first purged with phlebotomy or cupping. Then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then the cautery is placed upon it. When it fills up and falls off, if possible, the place should be cupped with a cup. Otherwise, wash the place first with vinegar then with plenty of water, wipe and squeeze. If blood continues to flow after the cautery falls off, that is good. Then dip a cotton cloth in cold water.
Place it above until the discharge stops. If blood is abundant, sprinkle powdered gum or bdellium, or similar astringents until the bleeding ceases, or apply crushed chickpeas to the site.
Leave it until the chickpeas adhere to the site, then remove them if needed without touching the wound. If the dressing cannot be reapplied, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the site until some blood flows out, then apply it. If it feels blood, it will adhere to the area. To remove it, sprinkle incense, salt, or ash upon it.
The Third Chapter.
Regarding Fractures.
This chapter also contains what is necessary for the practice of medicine, namely, the reduction and treatment of dislocations and fractures of bones.
Know, O my sons, that many physicians who are ignorant and unlettered, as well as the common people, claim this book to be of no value, having neither read nor studied the books of the ancients.
For this reason, this art has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered any proficiency in it. However, I have benefited from what I have learned through my long study of the books of the ancients and my diligent effort to understand them, and by following this path throughout my life.
I have compiled for you in this chapter all that I know about it. And after years of practice, I have brought it close to you, freed it from extraneous matter, abbreviated it as much as possible, and made it clear and concise, illustrating it with many diagrams of the instruments used therein.
This is merely an addition to the clarity I have attempted in the preceding chapters, and all power belongs to Allah, the Most High, the Greatest.
Chapter One.
At the beginning of this chapter, it is necessary to mention general principles and categories regarding fractures of bones before discussing each member separately. Therefore, I will first present some general statements and sections. You must first understand and grasp its reality, both you and those who come after you who are eager to learn this noble art.
So I say that whenever one suffers a fracture, dislocated bone, sprain or fall, it is not advisable to rush immediately to reduce it or splint it or both. Unless there is a hindrance such as weakness of power, or the person who suffered it is a child or very elderly, or the time is extremely hot or cold.
He should then restrict his diet to cold grains, poultry and fish, avoiding wine, tough meats, overeating, and any food that enriches the blood vessels excessively until the swelling subsides and there is no risk of further discharge to the affected area.
Then the patient should return to his usual diet, but if the fractured bone becomes fixed in its place, he should avoid foods that are too thick and sticky, such as dates, rice, lentils, porridge, and beef brains. And eggs, fried fish, thick broths and similar foods, for with this regimen healing of the fracture will be quicker and better. Know that in strong men and elders, broken bones cannot always heal perfectly.
For the bone edges of strong men and elders are rough and brittle, although some bones may heal like those of young children. However, nature produces a substance resembling glue on all sides of the broken bone, which binds and holds it together.
And it binds them together until they become strong and firm like their original state, causing no hindrance to his actions.
And therefore, the patient's diet should include foods that are thick and sticky as mentioned earlier. Know that fractures differ in type according to the different parts of the body, for a fracture of the leg bone is not like a fracture of the skull bone, nor is a rib fracture like a spine fracture.
And similarly, all other parts of the body are different from one another. We shall come to a detailed description of each type of fracture in its respective chapter, as fractures also differ among themselves.
It may be that the fracture is a simple one without splinters, or it may extend along the bone with splinters and fragments both sharp and blunt. The fracture may also occur with skin wounds and tears, or it may be a slight displacement.
For each type of fracture, there is a specific method for its reduction which will be explained in detail later. One way to identify a bone fracture is by its deformity, swelling, prominence upon touch, and crackling sensation when pressed with the hand.
However, if there is no visible deformity, no crackling sensation upon touch, no prominent bone feeling upon pressing, and the patient does not complain of much pain, then it may not be a fracture but rather a dislocation, a hairline fracture, or a slight displacement. In such cases, one should refrain from moving or manipulating the limb.
But apply to the area one of the medications that will be mentioned later, then gently traction is applied. Know that if a bone fractures and breaks into two pieces without any splinters, each part becomes completely separated from the other.
It is not permissible for you to immediately attempt reduction and alignment of the fracture before the swelling has subsided. If there is already heat and inflammation, leave it alone until the swelling has decreased, then reduce it with gentleness and skill.
Know that the reduction and alignment of a fracture without splinters is easier than one with splinters. If there are splinters present, then traction must be applied in both directions, whether it's an arm or leg fracture.
Either use your hands if the limb is small, or use two ropes, or a rope and hand together. Ensure that the limb is placed on a level surface in its natural position so that when the fracture is extended, the displaced fragments may be gently guided back into place with skill and care.
Strive your utmost not to cause the patient any pain or swelling through your actions. Your goal is to approximate and hold one of the bone fragments in its proper position with the other, gently touching and feeling them with your hand. If you find anything out of place, repair it and reduce it as much as possible.
Beware of excessive cutting and strong squeezing, as many ignorant ones do. Often they speak of doing so, causing burns or contusions to the limb, which I have seen happen repeatedly.
After trimming, suturing and tying the limb, ensure its rest and tranquility. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness or sleep, nor during coughing or sneezing, nor at any other time. Ensure that the bandage is applied in a way that prevents pain.
If the patient feels pain or discomfort with a certain bandage, transfer it to another that does not cause pain, ensuring it is straight and even.
To avoid deformity if it heals, know that the method of tying broken limbs is as I have described. Be aware that broken members differ in size and shape; those that are small like the arm, finger, hand, etc.,
For small members, bandages should be soft and flexible; for large ones like the thigh, back or chest, they should be wide and firm to evenly compress the member on all sides without causing any displacement.
After completing trimming, begin applying the appropriate plaster. Gently place a bandage on the fracture site, then begin wrapping it around the fracture itself for three or four layers depending on what the limb deserves. Tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then bring it up to the upper side of the fracture.
Tighten slightly less than at the fracture site, then gradually unwind and loosen until you achieve a suitable tension. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fracture.
Wrap around again, then bring the bandage down to the lower side of the fracture. Repeat the process of tightening and loosening as previously described. Then place a layer of soft padding or cloth between the layers to correct any deformity.
If there is no deformity, do not alter anything, then wrap it with another bandage and straighten it using these tight layers of cloth if there is neither swelling nor heat in the member.
If there is swelling or heat, apply something to relieve the swelling and reduce the inflammation, then leave it for some days before tightening with splints. The splints should be made of half-rounded reeds prepared wisely, or they can be made of grape vine wood.
These splints are made from fir wood, date palm leaves, papyrus, or similar materials that are available. Each splint should be of this size and shape.
However, the splint placed directly on the fracture should be thicker and wider than the others. The length of the splint should be determined according to the size of the broken member. The shape of the splint is as follows:
Then bind the splints with another bandage, just like the first binding, and secure them from above with tight strings as mentioned before, ensuring that the binding is tighter at the site of the fracture. The further away from the fracture, the less should be the tension.
It is preferable that the strings are of moderate thickness and stiffness, particularly made from moistened cotton thread. It is an error to use thick strings like those made from twisted cotton strips, as seen among ignorant people.
Because excessive tension occurs with thick strings, and thin strings are also unsuitable as they do not allow for the desired tension. There should be no less than the width of a finger between each splint.
If the patient is discomforted by the ends of the splints after binding in the correct places, place padded linen or wool beneath them to prevent any harm. However, if the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every broken member should be immobilized with splints from the very first day. If the member is large, splints should not be applied until five or seven days have passed, depending on your assessment of the risk of heat and inflammation.
This is the description of the plaster used by the ancients for fractures, dislocations and contusions. It is a general, brief recipe that sets bones and suits most temperaments, especially children and women as it is neither too hot nor cold.
And it is that you take the flour dust which adheres to the walls of the millstone during its motion, and knead it as it is without adding egg white. Make your dough neither thick nor thin. Then apply it.
Another plaster for setting fractures, dislocations and contusions: Take ten dirhams each of mastic, ladanum, tragacanth, bdellium, frankincense, and styrax. Five dirhams each of myrrh and incense. Twenty drams of aloe and twenty drams of Armenian or Roman earth clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with aloe water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. This plaster has a good composition, it sets broken bones quickly and suits most people due to its balanced nature.
Another plaster beneficial for fractures and contusions: Take ten dirhams each of frankincense, mastic, white lead. Five dirhams each of myrrh and incense. Six drams of tragacanth. Twenty drams of Armenian earth clay. Grind finely, sift, knead with water or egg white and apply.
A plaster for dislocations and displaced bones, to relieve pain and headaches caused by them. Take some clean wool, soak it in vinegar and cooked oil, then apply it to the affected area. This plaster does not have setting power.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swellings and driving away pains particularly. Another plaster for setting broken bones: Take papyrus leaves and leaves of bramble-poppy, grind them together and apply wet. Another brief recipe used when a bone is displaced.
And if you wish to remove the rest of the tumor, take equal parts of cinnabar, camphor and woad, grind them together and knead with varnish if the member is not hot. If it is hot, use carob water or plain water and apply it.
A stronger plaster for analysis than the first one is used when a hard tumor forms due to bone injury. It is made from equal parts of the roots of camomile, cottonseed, saffron, and henbane, and half parts of frankincense, myrrh, and gum arabic.
He strikes them all together and kneads with water of vinegar or sweet water or oil, according to the heat of the member and its quietness. But Hippocrates did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member upon setting.
Except for the cerotto made of wax and oil alone, which should be moderate between hardness and softness. And Galen saw that things with voids containing some heat, such as myrrh, ginger, and balm, should be placed on the broken member upon setting.
A plaster that benefits for weakness and pain is taken of sesame, lentil, human hair powdered or bird feather, camomile, and salt, equal parts are crushed and sieved, kneaded, and bound with it. And as for the amount that should remain on the bandage.
Then when it is dissolved, it should be looked at. If no pain or itching occurs and the broken bone does not move from its place, do not remove it for many days. But if severe itching or disturbing pain or swelling appears.
So hurry up and remove it at that time, do not delay it at all. Then take a soft cloth or sea sponge moistened with warm water, dip it in and wash the place until the itching stops and the pain subsides. Then let the member rest for a while.
Then apply the white wool dipped in vinegar and oil or rose oil ointment on it for a night to secure the hot tumor, reduce swelling of the member, and alleviate its pain. Then return it to gentle stretching and light bandaging, do not stretch it as before.
And tend to it until it heals. If you see that the tumor, redness, pain, swelling, and all symptoms have disappeared completely and there is no need for bandaging and stretching, then return it to its original state as before. But if none of what we mentioned occurs in the member.
Only cut it open after three days or four or five, or even up to twenty days, depending on what you see regarding the state of the limb. And only do this when fracture has become necessary and flesh has begun to congeal.
Then also increase tension at this point more than you did initially. Also, continue to provide thick food for the patient as mentioned before. But if you find that the site of fracture has dried out and wasted more than it should, know that nourishment is not reaching it.
Rinse it with warm water every time you open it, doing so once every three days. Reduce tension slightly, for by this action nutrition reaches the limb and heals quickly. But what ignorant practitioners do in breaking the limb again is wrong.
This is because they did not first set the bone correctly but rather reduced it incorrectly, which was a great error on their part. If it had been correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, yet I have seen no trace of it from anyone. And it is not permissible to do this.
Chapter Two.
On accidental fractures of the head.
The types of accidental head fractures are many and varied in their shapes, with diverse causes. Among them is the fracture caused by a sword strike, which can be such that it breaks all the bone until it reaches the peritoneum beneath it. As when splitting wood.
And this type of fracture is called 'qdumi'. Or it can be that the sword cut only a part of the bone, breaking its surface without penetrating all the way through. This type is called a 'general qala' fracture. The injury in both types may be large or small.
Among fractures is the one that is crushed or contused, caused by a blow from a stone or falling onto a stone. This fracture may be deep, approaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
And the injury in both types may also be large or small. And there is the fracture that is hidden within the bone, like the splitting of a hair, which is a minor concussion.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called 'hairline,' and it is the kind that results from a fall or blow by stone or similar, where a part of the bone enters into itself and becomes displaced like when copper is struck by a blow causing a piece to enter within.
More commonly, this occurs in the moist bone head of children. Shattered fragments that are both sharp and blunt may occur with all these types of fractures, and their treatment will be discussed in its appropriate place. All these types of fractures will be identified through inspection.
To examine it with probes and remove putrefied flesh from it, but as for the hairline type, it is recognized by exposing the bone and wiping and painting on it with ink, the fracture will appear black. As for treating fractures, first one should look at the patient's symptoms.
If you find among his symptoms clear indications of fear, such as vomiting blood, extension, loss of consciousness, hoarseness, fainting, high fever, reddening and inflammation of the eyes, or similar symptoms, then do not approach nor treat him. For death is certain in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you find symptoms that do not deter you and you hope for his safety, then proceed with treatment. For when the injured person comes to you at first after being injured, especially during winter days.
In any case, do not strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, especially in winter days. And even in summer days, do not hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day lest what is under the bone should putrefy and display the symptoms we have described.
If a bone fracture has reached the meninges of the brain with comminution and inflammation, then it is not permissible to cut off the shattered diseased part as I have described for you. Rather, shave the head of the injured person.
Examine the bone from whichever side you can manage and according to the shape of the wound and what affects the patient. If blood appears or a hot swelling when exposing the bone, then deal with it appropriately.
This means that you should fill the place with cotton wool soaked in rose water and oil, then cauterize the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is secured. Then proceed to reset and extract the bone in one of two ways.
The lone face [operation], it is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow chisel; this is its shape:
Then use another cut after this one, somewhat wider; this is its shape:
Then also use another cut, still wider than the second; and it is necessary for you to have various cuts of different widths and lengths, some being broader than others, and some shorter than others, and all with very sharp edges; let them be made of Indian steel or good iron.
And use gentleness in striking the cut so as not to shake the head and harm it; for if the bone is strong and hard, do not drill around it before using the bores which they call 'non-immersed' bores, but rather use those called 'non-immersed'.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the cranial bone towards what is behind it, for the drill has a round edge beneath its sharp point like a ring or small circle which prevents it from penetrating too deeply and crossing the thickness of the bone.
And you should take many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have a drill fit for every skull with its sharp point equal in length and width to the thickness of that skull; here are pictures of three types. Large, medium, and small drills:
As for the method of boring around the broken bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another spot, leaving a space between each bore equal to the thickness of a finger or thereabouts.
Then cut with the chisels what is between each pair of bores, doing so with utmost gentleness as mentioned earlier, until you can remove the bone either by hand or with some other tool prepared for that purpose.
Like the gentle forceps and tweezers; and it is necessary to be very careful that neither the drill nor the chisel touches anything of the dura mater. When you have freed the bone from its attachment and removed it, do not strip [the dura].
Smooth the remaining roughness of the bone with another tool, similar to the saw but finer and more delicate. If any small bones or fragments remain, remove them gently using your available tools.
Then treat the wound afterwards with sutures and ointments mentioned previously. The other aspect of this procedure is straightforward, far from difficult as Galen mentions and praises highly, which he himself states. First, you should expose the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and apparent, so that beneath it you can place the tip of this crescent-shaped tool, which looks like this:
The blunt part should not cut anything, while the sharp edge faces away lengthwise. Place the blunt part against the peritoneum and the sharp edge against the bone, then strike the tool on one side only.
With a small hammer until all the bone is cut gently, while turning and being secure from the membrane, without causing any frightening incident at all. Even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and inexperienced person, he will still succeed.
If any part of the membrane adheres to the bone in some places, scrape it off gently with the blunt tip of the tool and remove it without harm or fear.
However, if the bone fracture has not reached the membrane and only the surface of the bone is broken with rough fragments remaining, then you should remove those roughnesses and fragments gently using various sized probes adapted for this purpose, choosing the most appropriate one according to the requirements of the situation.
The shape of the fractured bone and its edges should be considered when using probes. Begin with a broader one, then proceed to narrower ones until you reach the narrowest.
As for minor cracks and slight fractures, each one should be treated according to what is most appropriate for its correction. This is something that is not hidden from anyone with even a basic understanding of this field, as we have written and explained regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a piece of cotton cloth corresponding to the size of the wound, dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound; then take another folded or triangular piece of cloth. And dip it in wine and rose oil, placing it on the first cloth and doing this with the utmost care so as not to weigh down the peritoneum; then use it from above a wide bandage, pulling it only enough to hold the wound closed.
And leave it like this for a day or two until the inflammation has subsided, then remove it and afterwards use powders made from such things as are efficacious in case of dryness like red iris root, nard powder, frankincense and zedoary etc., making poultices with them.
And sprinkle them dry on the wound; in general, use a medicine that clears without burning. Always ensure during treatment of the wound that it remains clean, without oil foulness or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate.
For if pus accumulates on the cerebral membrane, it corrupts and inflames it, causing great harm to the patient; likewise, it may appear in the abdominal cavity of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If you observe blackness on its surface, know that if the patient exhibits the symptoms mentioned, he is surely doomed; although this blackness may have resulted from a medicine applied to it, whose potency could cause such discoloration.
It is necessary to take a portion of honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well together, apply the mixture to a cloth and place it on the peritoneum; then treat it with whatever remedies are appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Three.
How to set a broken nose.
Know that the upper part of the nose, which is composed of two bones, alone can be broken; whereas the lower part, being made of cartilage, does not break. Instead it may exhibit symptoms such as swelling, deviation or inflammation.
If one of its sides breaks, you should insert the little finger into the nostril hole and push this fracture back in place from inside using your index and thumb fingers from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently and ensure that you do not cause pain to the patient with your actions.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then a thick rounded probe should be used to set it in place. If the fracture is on both sides, do likewise. The splinting should be done on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, and if not then after the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided, insert a cotton wick into the nostril hole if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if it is on both sides.
And let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril hole. Some of the earlier splinting practitioners have mentioned that the wicks should be smeared with butter and changed daily, but I do not recommend this. Instead, the wicks should be dipped in beaten egg white mixed with flour.
Then leave the wicks in place until the bone has healed and the cartilage has hardened. You can also insert goose feather tubes into the nostrils after wrapping them with soft gauze to create a more secure splint, ensuring that the patient's breathing is not obstructed.
This is not necessary; if you wish, you can make the tubes or use wicks alone. If there is swelling during your procedure and inflammation occurs, bandage the nose with cotton wool soaked in camphor or linseed oil and rose water, or with some diacilicon ointment.
If there is no inflammation, then bandage the nose from outside with a paste made of semolina flour and almonds mixed with beaten egg white. Then place a soft compress on top without binding it tightly, especially if the nasal bones have only suffered a minor fracture.
Or if the bones are crushed, then make an incision over it and remove the fragments using a suitable instrument. Then suture the wound and treat it with appropriate healing ointments. If there is an internal injury in the nose, treat it with wicks and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
To reset a dislocated lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is fractured but not accompanied by an injury to the eye, and if it was fractured externally without being broken in two or dislocated towards the inside, then its diagnosis is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, insert the left hand's index finger into the patient's mouth and similarly if it is on the left side use the right hand's index finger. Then gently lift the fractured condyle from inside outwards to reduce it.
Place your other hand outside the bone to guide its reduction. If the jawbone is broken in two, use a bandage on both sides to align and reduce it. If the fracture occurred between the teeth, allow them to grow back together.
Or separate them and secure what you have reduced with a gold, silver or brass wire. Then place a plaster on the fractured jawbone followed by a folded cloth and finally apply a firm bandage or a piece of leather strap equal in length to the jawbone. Then tie it firmly from above according to your preference and ensure that the dressing is secure so that it does not displace. Order the patient to rest quietly and make his diet soft foods. If you notice any change in shape on any side, proceed with its removal on the third day.
Then tie it firmly from above according to your preference and ensure that the dressing is secure so that it does not displace. Order the patient to rest quietly and make his diet soft foods. If you notice any change in shape on any side, proceed with its removal on the third day. Then fix what you have immobilized using fine sand mixed with egg white or flour paste after removing the plaster. Place a soft compress on the dressing, leaving it until it adheres and there is no change in the condition of the bone.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and becomes solid. This usually takes three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during this period, apply the remedies mentioned earlier to alleviate it until the swelling subsides.
If the fracture is accompanied by an injury to the eye, and if any bone fragments have become detached from it, be gentle in removing those fragments using appropriate forceps. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, dilate it with scissors according to your need.
Then, if those fragments are removed and nothing remains of them, suture the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the dressings suitable for that purpose. The wound will heal together until it heals.
Chapter Five
If the collarbone is broken, reduce (set) it.
Most commonly the collarbone breaks at its anterior end near the shoulder joint. Its fracture can occur in one of three ways: either it cracks into two pieces without any fragments, which is the easiest type to reduce (set).
Or, it may occur with fragments, which is more difficult to reduce. If the fracture occurs without injury, arrange for two assistants: one to hold down the arm on the side of the broken collarbone and another to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then, use your fingers to align the fracture until its shape is as it should be without any displacement or deformity. If you need more traction, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
The bone should be at the desired length, and you should extend and elevate the collarbone while pressing on the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone out due to it being deep within the wound, then have the patient lie on their back.
Place a moderate pillow under the patient's shoulder and have the assistant press down on their shoulder until the deep collarbone rises up. Then, align and reduce the fracture using your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the collarbone has broken off and is moving around, carefully incise over it and remove the fragment gently. If the fragment is stuck in the bone, use one of the cutters prepared for that purpose to break it. After this, place under the collarbone the instrument that preserves the peritoneum, which is made of wood or iron and looks like this:
It resembles a spoon without a handle, with a width according to the size of the bone you are dealing with, and a length suitable for working with. It should have two ends: one wider and one narrower as shown.
If the wound you made to remove a bone fragment is wide and you fear inflammation, then sew it up with stitches. But if the wound is small or you are uncertain about inflammation, then cleanse the wound and dress it according to its extent.
If inflammation appears, then apply oil of roses and vinegar or honey to the wound. As for setting a broken bone without cutting, use a bandage made from flour paste mixed with egg whites. Then place a soft splint on it, and put a ball under his armpit if necessary. Then take a long bandage, about an inch wide, and wrap it around the splint and dressing. Finally, apply a plaster made from thin board. It should be three fingers wide and similarly long, then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the plaster into the middle of the bandage where it is positioned over the fracture, and tie the bandage around the fracture as usual. And here it is tied around his neck, under his sound armpit, and under his affected armpit. Tie it repeatedly on each side, as you have seen how strong the fixation of the fracture is, which should be clear to you.
And your aim should be that the plaster does not come off from the broken bone. So you should visit the patient every day. Whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the plaster has shifted, tighten it again. Also, make sure the patient sleeps on his back with a small pillow under his affected armpit at night.
To raise his shoulder blade off his side and thus elevate the broken collarbone above the elbow, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not remove the bandage unless there is an incident of itching or inflammation for twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if necessary. And continue to apply pressure until the fracture consolidates and the collarbone heals completely, which usually takes about twenty-eight days. Sometimes it may take less than that in some people.
Chapter Six
In the treatment of collarbone fractures, most fractures occur at its broadest part. However, it may also break at its extremities; therefore, it is determined by touch whether the fracture is in the middle or at an extremity. Treatment depends on how the bone is fragmented.
Reduce it and bring its ends back to their natural shape using any means possible, then apply powdered frankincense mixed with egg white and tragacanth gum over the affected area. Place a folded linen cloth on top, followed by a wide bandage made of wood.
Make it as thin as the collarbone or slightly wider. If there is any bulging underneath the bandage at any point on the collarbone, smooth it out using tragacanth gum until the bandage lies evenly. Then secure it with a long headband from above.
Tie it firmly and ensure the bandage does not slip from its place. Check the bandage daily; if it becomes loose, tighten it and adjust the bandage accordingly. The patient should lie on their sound side with the fractured collarbone elevated for twenty days.
After twenty to twenty-five days, remove the bandage. This bone is not prone to break or dislocate again. If there is any splinter protruding and piercing under the skin, make an incision over it and remove it. And do as mentioned for collarbone fractures regarding treating heat inflammation if any occurs.
Chapter Seven
On the treatment of rib fractures
The chest may fracture in its middle, though this rarely occurs. However, its extremities are more prone to break and dislocate. Symptoms include severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing when a fracture occurs in the middle of the chest, causing it to tilt downward.
Sometimes, blood may be coughed up, and the depression in the fractured bone can be felt. To treat this, have the patient lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulder blades. Then press down on both elbows and bring the ribs together using both hands, gently manipulating them back into place as much as possible.
He agreed until the bone regains its intended shape, then apply a splint and bandage over it, place on top of it a support made from a thin slice of willow or broomstick, wrapped in cloth. Then, gently secure it around the broken bone so that it does not slip off, pass the bandage around to the back in circles several times and tighten it moderately, then regularly check the bandage and whenever it loosens, retighten it.
And if necessity demands its removal due to pain or swelling at the site, promptly remove it and treat what has appeared there appropriately, then replace the splint if necessary. Face him and insist on pressure until he recovers.
Chapter Eight
If ribs are broken, know that they fracture only in the hard parts that follow the spine and its ends towards the front; this is evident due to the softness of these places. Recognition of this is not difficult by examining with the fingers.
And fix them by aligning the fracture with your fingers on the affected side until it assumes its intended shape, then bandage and tighten the broken bone with a support if necessary. If the rib fracture is inward, it will cause severe pain.
And like the crackling sound heard in those with pleurisy due to the bone piercing the pleura, it also causes difficulty in breathing and coughing up blood excessively, making treatment difficult. Many remedies have been devised for this by the ancients.
Among them, some said it is preferable to feed the patient foods that produce wind and gas to cause the abdomen to expand and push the fracture outward. We reject this as it may cause hot swelling if it has not already occurred, and if it has, it will increase and aggravate it.
And some suggested applying a cupping glass to the site and then sucking strongly, which is closest to the appropriate method except that it fears drawing out blood to the site due to its weakness. Others said the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The wound becomes inflamed between the ribs until it fills up, and the bandage is even. Then wrap it around evenly. After that, treat the patient with a diet and medication for healing wounds. We feared for the patient, so we did not cut on the spot and expose the broken rib. Instead, we brought forth the instrument that preserves the diaphragm, as previously described. We then gently cut the bone with it and extracted it.
Then, if the wound is large, we close its lips with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals. If inflammation occurs during this process, promptly apply olive oil compresses on the area and also administer internally something to alleviate the inflammation.
He should lie on the side that promotes sleep until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
Treatment of hernias of the back and neck.
As for the cervical vertebrae, fractures are rare and dislocations are more common. The same applies to the lumbar spine. If such an injury occurs, you may wish to know whether it will heal or not. If you observe that his hands are limp, numb, and paralyzed, with no movement or sensation in them despite being pricked by a needle without feeling pain, then know that recovery is unlikely and he will likely die.
However, if he can move them and feels the prick of a needle, know that the spinal cord is intact and the patient will recover with treatment. If a lumbar hernia is similar to this, you may wish to know whether it will heal or not.
Observe his legs. If they are also limp and similar to the condition of his hands, then if he lies on his back wind and feces will pass without control, and if he lies on his stomach urine will pass without control. If he lies on his back and cannot pass urine despite wanting to, then know that he is lost and do not bother with treatment. If this does not happen, the case is less severe. Treat by applying something internally to alleviate the inflammation.
To apply hot compresses of rose oil alone or with burnt garlic cloves, place them on the affected area three times a day until the inflammation subsides. Then, apply a moistened gauze dressing and bandage it tightly in place. Instruct him to remain at rest.
If the patient feels no pain when lying on one side, he should sleep on that side until healed. However, if a bone splinter or fragment has remained after fracture healing, do not cut open the skin to remove it. If the wound is large, bring its edges together with stitches and treat it with healing ointments until healed. If the last cervical vertebra (coccyx) fractures, do not insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus.
Align the broken bone with the other hand as best you can and apply a splint or bandage if necessary. Then secure it tightly in place.
If you feel a bone fragment inside, make an incision over it, remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until healed.
Chapter Ten
Treatment of Hip Fracture
Rarely do the thigh bones break; however, if one does break, it usually fractures at its ends and lengthens, tilting inward, causing pain, numbness, and weakness in the leg on the affected side.
To treat, run your hand along the bone until you reach the fracture. If the fracture is only at its ends, align it as best you can to resemble its natural shape.
If the fracture is in the shaft or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their back to align and reduce the fracture. Once reduced, apply a splint and secure it with a wooden or leather brace, then tighten it firmly.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or loosening of the splint. Fill in the spaces between the toes to ensure even tension and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or unaffected side if swelling occurs.
Leave off pressing it and heating it until the hot swelling has subsided, then apply to it what quiets it as mentioned earlier. Then return to your pressing and heating it as is proper; but if fragments or pieces of bone appear in it, it should not be removed.
And do not touch it, but smooth it from the outside as mentioned earlier, and leave its pressing until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
On Setting Fractures of the Arm.
The arm is what is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it breaks, press it back into place on either side, taking an oiled, smooth, stout splint of this form:
And tie its ends with bandages, then suspend it from an elevated place and have the patient sit on a chair. Then lay the broken arm on the splint so that the armpit rests firmly in the middle of its curve, and then suspend something heavy from above.
Or a servant extends it downward, then the physician straightens the fracture with both hands until the fragments return to their proper position. The other method is for the patient to lie on his back and have his hand suspended from his neck by a bandage.
Then order two servants to each hold one end of the fracture with their hands, one above it and one below it, and extend each towards his side. And if you wish for stronger extension, tie a bandage under the fracture and another above it, then have each servant extend his end towards his side.
If the fracture is near the elbow, then one bandage should be placed under the armpit and another under the fracture towards the elbow. And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the bandage should be placed on that spot and on the elbow itself.
Then gently align the fracture without force until the fragments are properly aligned and well united. Then bind it tightly if there is no hot swelling; but if a hot swelling appears, leave it unbound for seven days and apply a poultice of moistened lentils soaked in vinegar and ointment of roses.
Even if the swelling has settled, then pull it taut. The method of pulling is to apply a bandage on the fracture, then place a fresh cloth wrap over the bandage, and finally bring the arm up to rest on its own elbow with the hand open and placed on the shoulder.
Carry the cloth and tension on the elbow and arm so that the arm serves as a splint if there is no impediment preventing you from doing so and nothing has changed regarding the broken bone. But if you fear any change, then use splints.
And to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than other splints, with one finger's width between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture plus three fingers' width from both sides.
Then tighten on the splints with the tension mentioned at the beginning of this book.
And your tightening should be strongest at the site of the fracture.
And whenever you are after the fracture, the tension should decrease. So if you find that placing the splints and tightening as mentioned when you first set the bone is useful now, then do so.
However, if you fear heat in the swelling, then leave off the tension and splints until the seventh day as mentioned before. Then examine the bandage every three days to ensure that there is no itching or inflammation at the site, nor does excess tightening prevent nutrition from reaching the member. All of this will be as described.
If you are confident that nothing of this will happen, then do not remove the bandage until many days later. The patient should lie on their back with their hand on their stomach and a bolster filled with wool placed under their elbow in moderation.
And examine at all times, day or night, to ensure that the shape of the broken member does not change nor does the bandage become loose. If this happens, then repair it with your effort. Also, maintain the patient's diet on the level mentioned previously, starting with a soft diet until the bone begins to harden.
It is not permissible to make the patient's diet harsh, as it is customary for the arm and leg bones to harden in forty days. At that time, it becomes necessary to remove the bandage, use the bathhouse, and treat with appropriate ointments, especially if the fracture was severe and displaced.
Do not remove the stitches for fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve
In treating fractures of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius, one small which lies next to the thumb and the other large which is situated underneath the small one from below. Sometimes only the large bone may be fractured or only the small one may be fractured, while at other times both may be fractured together.
When the upper radius bone is fractured, its treatment is easy and recovery is quicker. But when the lower radius bone is fractured, its treatment is poor and recovery is difficult. The worst case is when both bones are fractured together. If the upper radius bone has been fractured
The doctor treating the fracture should make the reduction gently and carefully until it is aligned. If the larger bone has been fractured, then greater pressure should be applied during reduction. If both bones have been fractured together, then much stronger pressure must be used during reduction.
During reduction, the hand should be placed on a pillow with its palm facing down. The thumb should be held higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should rest on the ground beneath them, while the elbow is bent and folded under itself.
And the pillow should be at the same height as him to avoid causing discomfort for the patient.
Then a servant should extend the arm from below either by hand or using a bandage, and another servant should do likewise from above. The doctor should then reduce the bone until it returns to its best possible position. If there are any bone fragments, they should be removed.
Return each fragment to its proper place as much as possible. If there is a sharp fragment that has pierced the skin and cannot be reduced, cut into it and remove it according to the method described earlier. A separate section has been devoted to fractures accompanied by wounds.
If swelling and heat appear during the initial reduction, take a cloth soaked in rose oil and beeswax mixture of medium thickness, not too thick or too thin. Apply it to the affected area with gentle pressure until the swelling subsides.
Remove the plaster, apply a prepared dressing made of flour and egg white. Then bring the splints, with the one placed on the fracture being longer and stronger. Know that in most cases there are six splints for the arm.
If the fracture is in one bone or both bones together, make your bandage stronger and tighter at the site of the fracture. Whenever you wrap the bandage upwards or downwards, loosen it a little from what was mentioned earlier.
And let the bandage wrapped around the fracture be a soft, moist one and not too hard. And let the thread used to tie it be medium thickness linen as we described earlier, and examine the limb and bandage after some days.
If something happens that needs fixing, such as an itch appearing on the limb, then soak the limb in warm water until the itching stops. Leave the limb unrestrained overnight to rest, then resume bandaging.
If the bandage has loosened and the bone has shifted, then fix it all as much as you can. Also, if nutrition is not reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, loosen it a little and leave it for some days until nutrition reaches the limb, then retighten it.
If no improvement appears as mentioned, do not remove the bandage until after about twenty days. Then suspend the patient's arm to their neck and keep the arm level with the shoulder. The patient should avoid sudden movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this fracture of the arm usually heals in thirty days, or sometimes in forty-two days, and perhaps even in twenty-eight days, depending on the nature of the person's constitution and their strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
In setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
Most often, the palm of the hand and the fingers are not subjected to fractures but rather to contusions. So whenever a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand...
It is necessary for the patient to sit cross-legged with a chair in front of him at eye level.
Then he places his hand on it extended, then the bone-setting servant stretches it and the physician aligns it until it has healed properly.
