At that point, it is necessary to apply a splint and bandages if there is no heat inflammation. Then a plaster cast should be applied above it according to its size.
And he has wrapped it in a soft cloth, if the fracture is towards the palm of the hand then make a ball shape from the cloth and tell the patient to grip it with his fractured hand. Then tie it with a long cloth strap.
And let the plaster be made of soft leather that can bend with the skin as the hand is moved, and tie it firmly in place. If the fracture is on the outer side, then make one plaster above and another below the hand so that the hand remains open and elevated.
Then wrap the bandage around like the hand rotates and tie it between the fingers. If the fracture is on one of the little fingers, if it's the thumb then align it properly and wrap it with the hand. And if you wish to make a small plaster cast for it to keep the fracture in place.
And do not move it, if the fracture is on any of the other fingers such as the middle, index, ring or pinky then align it and bind it with the next healthy finger or bind all together. It is better, or place a small plaster cast upon it.
As we have said about the thumb, and examine him during healing and afterwards for any heat inflammation. Respond to it as necessary whenever something like that occurs, as mentioned repeatedly in its description.
Chapter Fourteen
On the Setting of Fractures of the Thighbone.
The thighbone often breaks and it is apparent because it rotates towards the front and back, its treatment involves tying a bandage above the fracture and another below it while the patient lies on their face.
Then extend each bandage towards its side evenly, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the hip joint then tie a soft bandage made of wool or similar material.
When the thighbone approaches the pelvis near the hip, let one ligament be above the fracture and the other below it. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then place the ligature near the knee with the traction downwards; thereafter, the physician should realign the bone using both hands until it resembles its natural form.
The bone should be set well and when it is, the bandages and traction may then be applied. But if inflammation occurs in the limb, allow some time for it to subside before resuming treatment.
As for the traction, it should not be wrapped too tightly around the fracture like a turban twice or thrice and left with some slack. Then wrap the leg so that the heel is near the buttock, insert a long thread between the thigh and the lower part of the leg near the knee, leaving both ends from above on either side.
Then wind the remaining part of the turban around both the leg and thigh, place padding on the thigh at the exact location of the fracture to make a single bolster for the shinbone, and fill the gap between the thigh and leg with linen wadding to even out the traction.
Begin strong traction from the center towards the fracture, winding three or four times around it. As you move away from the fracture with the ligament, decrease your tension and make it gentler and looser.
Then proceed to the two ends of the thread inserted between the thigh and leg, tie them around the nearby padding above, then bring each end downwards until they reach behind the knee. Tie them together there as well, pulling both ends gently.
So that the ligature does not slip from its place, leave the traction undisturbed as long as there is no pain, swelling, inflammation or similar complaints in the limb. But if any such thing occurs, promptly remove the traction and correct it according to what has been mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter embedded in the bone, it should not be realigned if possible; otherwise, cut upon it and remove it. Treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals. Sometimes the thighbone may fracture without involving the shinbone, requiring no additional padding on the leg.
As mentioned in the arm and forearm, except that its fracture is not accompanied by lameness unless it breaks alone without being attached to the leg, in which case its owner must always limp.
Know that the thighbone strengthens in fifty days or a little more or less, all according to the variation of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In fractures of the knee joint.
The knee joint rarely fractures, but it often dislocates. If it does fracture, it is either a fissure or a fragmentation of its parts, which may occur with or without laceration.
All of this is determined by examination, and its fracture is reduced by pressing the fragmented parts together with the fingers until they meet and align as much as possible with gentleness and care. Then a splint is applied and, if necessary, a circular bandage is used for immobilization.
Then tie the bandage above the affected area, and follow all the procedures mentioned earlier for other fractures, such as applying hot compresses, until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the reduction of leg fractures.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula, both of which are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its reduction is like that of the arm and the procedure is the same. If both bones are broken, the leg will rotate in all directions. If only the thin bone is broken, the leg will rotate forward. If only the thick bone is broken near its lower end, this should be obvious to you.
Therefore, use traction and reduction techniques to apply splints accordingly. However, if the leg fracture is a severe one with many fragments, then gentle traction should be used, taking great care in its reduction.
In addition to the work done on the arm, when you have finished reducing and immobilizing the leg fracture, take two pieces of pinewood used for flattening the spaces between floorboards or made from date palm leaves, and place them along both sides of the fractured bone.
Select ones that are slightly thick and not from the linen, let their length be equal to the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one twice in a bandage along its length, placing one on the inner side of the leg and the other on the outer side.
Let it be from the knee to below the foot, then tie the bandages at three points on both sides and in the middle. With this splint, the leg will be prevented from moving sideways and will remain steadfast. A wooden splint of the same length as the leg may also be used.
Insert it to prevent movement, especially in cases of fracture accompanied by a specific wound. Then examine the leg daily and pay close attention to any swelling or inflammation, etc. If anything like that occurs, address it accordingly until it heals. The leg bone takes about thirty days or so to unite.
Chapter Seventeen
On the fractures of bones in the leg and toes.
As for the heel, it never breaks, but the bones of the leg and toes may fracture. However, fractures in the toes are rare, with dislocation being more common.
If the bones of the leg are fractured and you see that some have shifted towards each other, have the patient stand with their foot flat on the ground, then place your foot on the elevated bones and press down gently to realign them.
Until they return to their original positions. Then bring the splint and bandages from above, placing a small board with flat ends underneath the heel of the foot. Tie the foot securely after wrapping it in cloth and any other necessary materials.
After three or four days have passed, release the bandage. You will find that the bones are straight and aligned, whether they were fractured or dislocated. Proceed accordingly based on what you see. As for fractures in some of the toes, reduce and align them according to the method described for reducing fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe along its length, making it slightly wider. Then place the board described earlier under the foot and tie it securely. If two or more bones are fractured...
Make a bandage for each finger, graduated according to its size, and place it in a soft cloth. Stretch the sole plate below the foot, ensuring that each head of the plate's heads is pressed evenly against the sole of the foot for proper adjustment.
And you must not forget to examine all that we have mentioned to you concerning the signs of fractures, and treat each sign accordingly.
Chapter Eighteen.
On fracture of the woman's hipbone and pubic bone.
When the woman's perineum fractures, seat her upright and gently lean her backwards while holding onto her from behind. Then allow the midwife to insert cotton into her vagina until it is full and resembles a ball inside her.
Then shake the woman gently and elevate her hips slightly until that cotton comes out to rest at the vaginal opening like a ball. Then place a support on her back, so that whenever she wants to urinate, the cotton can be removed gently for her to do so.
And return it as it was originally inserted. Then return to her first support, doing this for seven days or so until it heals. If you wish, you may also insert a sheep's bladder into her vagina by tying a reed tube onto its mouth and inserting the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, causing the fracture to heal. Then fill it with cotton and keep her on bed rest as previously described until she recovers. As for fractures of the pubic bone in men or women, use what has been mentioned for their treatment.
And treat it as described for fractures of the hipbone. These strange fractures are not common, especially if one has some experience and understands this book well; the fracture itself usually indicates its treatment.
Understand the correct method of treating and reducing it, especially for fractures of the neck of the thighbone. If a man's thighbone fractures at its neck, insert an ostrich bone into his penis, then wrap it with bandage and leave it for three days or so until healed.
Chapter Nineteen
In case of fracture with wound.
It is necessary that if a person has a fracture with wound, especially if it is a large bone such as the thighbone or the armbone or similar, to hasten and perform bloodletting from its onset provided the conditions for bloodletting are present as previously mentioned.
However, if the wound is bleeding profusely, it is necessary to hasten and cauterize it using powdered niter. If nothing else is available, then proceed with reduction of the fracture on that same day without delay.
If no inflammation occurs, then allow the fracture to remain undisturbed for nine days until any inflammation subsides. Do not approach it on the third and fourth day in case of ill effects.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, then carefully push it back and align it with your hands using gentle pressure. If you are unable to do so manually, use this instrument made of iron which should be approximately seven or eight fingers in length. Adjust its size according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of these instruments prepared beforehand according to the size required for each type of fracture. They should be slightly thick and not bend under pressure during use.
The tip of the instrument should be sharp with a slight bend at its end, tapering from thickness at the top to extreme thinness towards the bottom. Its shape is as follows:
It is called in Greek 'perma', meaning a small lever. The sharp, curved tip should be placed on the protruding end of the bone and gently pushed until the bone returns to its original position and the fragments are approximated.
If the tip of the fractured bone is thin and not gripped well by the instrument, then cut off the end of that bone so that the instrument can secure it. If you still cannot reduce the fracture as described, then use one of the cutting instruments mentioned earlier to cut it accordingly.
Alternatively, if possible, use one of the saws to divide it. Then remove any remaining rough edges and splinters from the bone. If after reduction, there is severe pain that persists, know that the bone has not returned to its normal position.
If you are able to restore it to its natural place, do so; this will greatly benefit the patient. Once the fracture has healed, dip a cloth in strong black vinegar and apply it, especially if it is summer. Do not use any ointment or greasy substance on the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and corruption, then use dressings when the bone has been set. Leave the wound exposed by making a hole in the bandages with scissors equal to the size of the wound.
And beware, with great caution, not to pull on the wound along with the fracture. Many ignorant physicians have done this and caused either death or the loss of a limb for their patients. Let your pulling be gentle and unlike the pulling used in other fractures.
If the wound is foul or large and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, do not apply dressings. Instead, make bandages from stiff cloth and bind it tightly.
If, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the liquid. Then apply ointments and dressings such as we usually use for wounds, like the four-ingredient ointment, etc., and loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals. It is also advisable to elevate the limb so that pus drains downward easily. If many days pass without the wound adhering or the discharge ceasing, know that there are small bone fragments remaining.
It is not permissible to search the wound with forceps. Any bone fragments that are clean should be removed and taken out. If they are dirty and cause pain and inflammation, then cut them away and remove them as much as possible.
If the wound exhibits inflammation, gangrene, or any other type of putrefaction and corruption, then treat each manifestation with its corresponding treatment as described in this book.
When a large bone is broken and presses on the member, such as the thighbone or armbone, do not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often, attempting this results in death. Rather, leave it alone until it rots, for it may fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
If at that point the wound appears treatable, then treat it; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty
On treating complications that arise after some fractures.
Such complications often occur during the healing of fractures, especially those near joints; they distort the shape of the limb and may even impair its natural function.
If the complication is fresh, use astringent drugs such as myrrh, bdellium, frankincense, and aloe vera, mixed with vinegar, egg white, or wine.
And apply it to the complication with pressure, binding it tightly and leaving it on for several days without removing it, then removing it and repeating the process until the complication disappears, God willing. Alternatively, use a tightly fitted brass plate.
Lead has the property of removing all that is excessive in the members. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and it is necessary to remove it, make an incision above it and cut away or scrape off the excess bone until it disappears, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One
On treating fractures when the limb remains thin after healing.
If a bone fracture has healed but the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this may be due to many reasons. One of the causes may be excessive tying of the ligaments with improper binding, too tight binding preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, excessive massage, performing movements at inappropriate times, or weakness and insufficient blood in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient's body and strengthen it until blood becomes abundant in it, use baths, induce happiness and pleasure, then apply warm pitch plaster on the limb to draw nourishment to it, or perform moderate massage with lukewarm water. Until food passes through it and returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Second
In the treatment of broken bones that are displaced and do not align properly.
Whenever a limb appears crooked after healing from a fracture or the bone is deformed, causing the member to appear ugly and misshapen. However, if the limb has not lost its natural function, it is not permissible to accept the claim of those who say that the limb should be broken from its root, despite many ignorant physicians and bonesetters practicing this in our land.
This action is greatly reprehensible and leads to great harm; however, if the deformity and complexity are recent, then it is permissible to use water that has been boiled with softening herbs such as papyrus leaves and roots, acacia fruit, and similar substances.
And the dressing should be done with softening cataplasms such as well-made diacylons, or else papyrus sap mixed with chicken fat and sherg oil, and dressed with it; or fig paste beaten with pigeon dung, among other medicines called "disjoining ointments".
And if the complexity cannot be resolved through continuous, gentle manipulation and movement of the limb in all directions at all times, then it is necessary to resort to treatment with iron.
It is necessary to carefully cut through the skin above and release the connection of the bone, then cut away any excess deformity or bone with fine saws, taking care and caution in this matter. Afterwards, treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Third
When a jaw joint dislocates from its proper place and causes immobility, deformity of the member, and severe pain to the patient, it is imperative to promptly reduce it back into position.
One should never delay in doing so, as any delay may cause swelling around the area and make reduction of the jaw more difficult. Therefore, one must not wait nor move nor stretch the affected area while it is still swollen, as this often causes tremors and severe pain for the patient.
However, if this condition presents itself, one should first proceed to perform bloodletting on the patient, then leave it alone until the swelling subsides a little. Then, soften the member with warm water and oil before gently replacing it and treating each organ as mentioned in its respective place.
I have arranged the sections of the jawbone according to what has been mentioned regarding fractures, from top to bottom of the body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In treating the lower jaw.
Rarely do the jawbones dislocate except in rare cases, and dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they slip out of their places easily and remain somewhat relaxed, or they become completely dislocated until they relax. Up to the point where the patient's saliva flows uncontrollably, and he cannot hold it back nor can he close his mouth or control his tongue for speech. But if the dislocation is slight, then in most cases he can return it to its place with ease.
However, if the dislocation is complete, one should not rush to put it back in place too quickly nor delay doing so. Instead, a servant should hold the patient's head steady while the doctor places their index finger into the origin of the jawbone inside the mouth on the affected side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, he should insert both his index fingers inside the mouth and use all his other fingers from outside to align it. Then, he should instruct the patient to relax their jaw and move it towards each side while the doctor guides the jaw back into place.
If returning the jawbone to its place is difficult, especially if both are dislocated, use a bandage soaked in warm water and oil to facilitate their return without delaying it. Once they have been returned and aligned, and the patient's mouth has closed but has not fully relaxed, then apply compresses made of linen with cotton wool dipped in wax and oil.
Then, tie it gently with a relaxed bandage. Ensure the patient sleeps on their back with two pillows supporting their head to prevent them from moving it left or right and avoid chewing solid food; instead, they should consume soft foods.
Even if the pain subsides and the jaw is dislocated, he should eat whatever he desires. He should not be hasty in his eating nor strain himself when opening his mouth for eating, drinking or yawning until the jaw is healed.
However, if the jaw does not return to its proper place after dislocation and remains misaligned, it often leads to feverish illnesses, constant headaches, abdominal pain or repeated vomiting. If you observe such symptoms, know that it is damaged.
He may die within ten days from this condition.
Chapter Twenty-Five
On the dislocation of the jaw and point of the shoulder-blade.
As for the collarbone, it does not dislocate towards the inner side due to its connection with the chest. If it becomes dislocated outwards, this can be determined by touch. Its reduction is achieved when the patient lies on their back and extends their arms.
Then press the site with your hand firmly; it will return to its place. Then apply bandages and splints, securing them tightly. As for the end near the shoulder-blade, it rarely dislocates. If it does, do not attempt reduction or alignment.
Then apply bandages and splints, securing them tightly. Order the patient to remain at rest until healed. With this same treatment, the point of the shoulder-blade can also be reduced if it has dislocated.
Chapter Twenty-Six
On the reduction of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that the shoulder may dislocate in three directions: downwards towards the armpit, towards the chest, or rarely, upwards above the shoulder.
It does not dislocate backwards behind the shoulder-blade or forwards towards the nerve. It most commonly dislocates downwards towards the armpit, especially in individuals with less fat, as it exits and enters quickly.
Those with abundant flesh find it difficult to exit and enter. Some people may receive a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become hot, leading them to believe it is dislocated. Do not examine this until you are sure of its condition.
Then in its treatment, you proceed as follows: If the shoulder blade is displaced downward towards the armpit, and if it is dislocated between the healthy shoulder blade and the shoulder blade, you will find a noticeable difference between them. You will also find the head of the shoulder blade damaged, and upon touching under the armpit, you will feel as if there is an egg.
If it [the shoulder blade] has dislocated towards the chest or upwards, you will find this evident upon examination; the patient is unable to raise their hand to their ear nor move it in all its movements.
If the shoulder blade is soft, or if the patient is a child, it can be easily reduced. You should raise the patient's hand upwards with the help of an assistant, then place your thumbs under their armpit and push the joint upwards forcefully into its proper position while the assistant raises the hand overhead.
Then if it does not return as mentioned above, and the shoulder blade has been dislocated for many days, it is not advisable for the patient to bathe in hot water. Instead, they should use a plaster that relaxes and softens the muscles, such as a mixture of linseed, hulba (fenugreek), and alfalfa boiled in water.
Then the patient should lie on their back, and a moderate ball of wool should be placed under their armpit. The physician should then sit on top of the ball, hold the patient's hand with one hand, and extend it downward with the other.
If the ball is pushed with his foot, it will return to its place. If you wish to reduce it in this manner, arrange for a man taller than the patient and have him stand on one side of the patient's bed. He should then insert his elbows under the patient's armpits and raise their arms upwards until the patient is suspended in mid-air. Then another assistant should pull the patient's hand downward towards their stomach. If the patient is light, it is not necessary to hang anything else on them for fear of weighing them down; the shoulder blade will return immediately upon doing this. It can also be reduced in another manner.
Another method is to place a long, round wooden pole on the ground, similar in shape to a mortar but not too thick or too thin. Then place a soft cloth on top of it and have the patient stand along its length with their armpit resting on the pole.
Then he reaches his hand downwards from the other side and stretches his body likewise with force; the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce, use all that we have mentioned and apply this treatment: take a stick about two cubits long. And its width should be about four fingers and thick like two fingers, having a rounded end to facilitate insertion into the depth of the armpit as follows.
Then tie a soft cloth on the rounded head of the stick to prevent harming the patient with the stick, then insert it under the armpit and extend the hand and arm downwards along the stick, tying the stick to the forearm and wrist.
Then place the arm on a support, suspending it by its width and extending the hand downwards while leaving the rest of the body hanging on the other side; the joint enters into its socket. Once it has entered completely, place a moderate-sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then apply the prepared poultice made from flour dust, incense, and egg white to the entire shoulder, wrapping it around from above. Then firmly remove the ball of wool from under the armpit, wrap the bandage over the poultice from above, and tie the hand to the neck.
He should keep his hand still for seven days, not moving it. It is advisable to keep the patient's diet light so that the member gains strength; it heals faster this way. After seven or five days, the bandage can be removed and movement can be exercised. If there is no recurrence and the joint does not relax, then recovery has taken place.
But if the joint dislocates frequently due to dampness or some other cause, then it should be treated with reduction using three splints as mentioned in the chapter on reduction. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days but the joint remains unstable,
Then know that the tendon of the shoulder joint has either been cut, stretched, or relaxed. Know then that this joint will never remain in place permanently.
If the jawbone extends towards the chest and breast, and even to the back, it is reduced by pushing and pressing with the hands until it returns to its place. The usual bandaging and treatment are employed until it heals. However, if healing leaves a scar on the member and movement remains slow.
Let the patient use hot compresses repeatedly until the scar softens and returns to its original state.
The Twenty-seventh Chapter.
In treating the elbow joint.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also reduces with difficulty, it can dislocate in all directions but especially anteriorly or posteriorly. Its dislocation is obvious to you as it lies beneath sight and touch, regardless of its shape.
When you compare the dislocated elbow with the normal one, it becomes clear that the joint is misaligned and the arm cannot be bent nor touched at the shoulder. Therefore, you should promptly reduce the dislocation before heat begins to accumulate in it.
For if heat accumulates in it, it becomes difficult to reduce and may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is posterior as this is the worst type of dislocation with the greatest pain. Often death accompanies it.
If it is reducible, order an assistant to extend both his hands and arms, palms facing up. The doctor should place one hand above the elbow and the other below it, then push the joint back into its place using the thumb or heel of the hand.
If the dislocation is anterior, it can be reduced by bending the hand until the heel of the palm strikes the shoulder that is adjacent to it. If reduction does not occur, then use strong and forceful pressure by ordering an assistant to extend the arm.
The patient should also hold the assistant's hands to prevent him from slipping away during traction. Then, wrap the arm with a folded cloth or wide bandage and rotate it in all directions after reduction. The doctor should grease his hands for easier manipulation of the joint.
Then push the joint forcefully until it reduces. After reduction, apply a splint that has been padded and dried with egg whites. Secure it gently but firmly, suspend the arm in a sling from the patient's neck, and leave it immobilized for several days.
Then if it becomes infected, if the joint stays in its place, cut away the bandage from around it and leave it. But if you see that the joint has not yet healed well, prepare a new bandage and ligature and leave them on for however many days it takes until it heals. If there is swelling after the joint returns to its place and is cut open.
If it is difficult to move, use massage in the bathhouse and gentle rubbing and kneading until it becomes soft. Or place a bandage of fat from a thick sheep on the joint, then leave it overnight and remove it the next day before entering the bathhouse. If he sweats, gently rub the joint.
Then put the bandage on again once, twice, or three times with entering the bathhouse until it becomes soft. If you wish to place a damp, softened piece of cow's udder on it with fat and tie it on, do so repeatedly for it will become soft and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eighth.
In the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint often dislocates and its return is easier than that of other joints, except that it should be returned quickly at the time when it dislocates before the place becomes stiff or inflamed.
To return it, place the elbow of the sick person on a board and have an assistant extend his hand. The doctor should place his palm on the back of the joint and push until it returns, but he should first look to see if the joint has completely returned to its place in the inner part of the hand.
The sick person should place the outer side of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the joint remains prominent on the outer side of the hand, then place the inner side of the hand on the board so that the doctor's hand lies directly on the back of the joint. If it does not return at this time, apply a bandage with an anti-inflammatory poultice.
Leave him alone without repeating this procedure unless the joint remains dislocated and cannot be reduced after some days have passed. This will not harm the patient except that his hand may become weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then know that if the nerve is severed or crushed, there is no remedy except to bind it with a splint; perhaps it will benefit and perhaps not. After wrapping the wrist, apply the bandage we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then remove the bandage and exercise the hand; if its movement is hindered or there is stiffness, soften it with hot water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine
In treating fingers.
If a finger becomes dislocated in any direction, when it is displaced towards the front or back of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place, then bind the base of the finger and suspend it in the direction from which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend the finger until it stands straight that day. If it is bound at night according to the same description, leave it unbound during the day, exercise it with movement and bind it again at night. Continue doing this until it becomes strong.
Do the same if a finger dislocates towards the palm of the hand, binding it in the same direction and following the initial procedure until it heals. Do likewise for dislocation to other directions.
Chapter Thirty
In treating dislocation of the spinal vertebrae
If there is a complete dislocation of one vertebra from the spine or neck, or if several vertebrae shift from their places, there is no treatment for this condition as death swiftly follows.
The sign of this is that the patient's feces come out without control and they cannot hold them back. Often, some of their limbs become paralyzed, either both legs or arms, or just one of them. However, if only one vertebra shifts from its place, it can often return to its position on its own.
When a vertebra shifts in four directions, that which shifts backwards is called a "hump." If the hump occurs from an injury to the spine in childhood, there is no treatment for it and it never heals. However, if the hump occurs from a fall or blow, etc.,
I have omitted many of the lengthy discussions and treatments mentioned by earlier authors, most of which are not beneficial. I have abbreviated those that may prove useful and provided clear explanations and illustrations different from what others have shown.
I say that the hump occurring at the front of the chest and also on either side is incurable, whereas those occurring in the back can be treated as I shall describe.
Place a bed level against the wall with a damp cloth underneath to prevent it from hurting his chest, then insert a post upright into a hole in the ground at the head of the bed and another one at its foot.
Have one servant hold the post at the head of the bed, not firmly fixed in the hole, and another hold the one at its foot. Then wrap a soft, tight bandage around the patient's chest and under his armpits, bringing the end towards the post at his head and tying it there.
Then tie another bandage above his hips, knees, and ankles, gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the post at his feet. Each servant should pull on their respective posts without removing either post from its position.
Unless the posts are not firmly fixed, as mentioned earlier. The physician presses his hands on the stone with force until it returns to its place or places a board on top of it and leans on it with his legs until it does so. If this treatment does not succeed, take a board about three cubits long.
And carve out a place in the wall mentioned earlier, near the patient, to insert one end of the board. Then place the middle of the board on the hump and have the physician stand on the other end with his legs and press firmly until the stone grinds against itself.
Until it returns to its place, and if you wish, you can use a screw that is turned by hand. Drive two stakes into the ground at the head of the bed about an arm's length long and do the same at its foot.
After piercing each plank with a bore of one-fourth of its length, and making in each a hole through which the screw may pass, fasten all the planks firmly to the ground so that they may not move at all. Then introduce into them the screwing stick, which is the instrument by means of which the ligature passes through both holes.
And at one end pierce a hole in which place an awl of the length of a span, upon which to turn. In the other two planks make like holes for the screw-stick; then draw tight the bandages which you have applied to the wound of the patient in the screw at its head and those placed on his legs in the screws at his feet.
Then, at each screw, let a servant stand and turn the handwheel with which the screw is wound; and the physician shall straighten the hump as we have shown it. This is an illustration of the screw, the bench, and the patient:
After the swelling has subsided and the part is straightened, then you should bind on the dried dressing with white of egg, followed by a bandage, and then place over it a splint made from boards three fingers wide or so, and its length equal to that of the hump.
After placing him upon a proper bed, then bind him with the bandage which is to be used. Let the patient take soft diet until healed; if any remains of the lump remain in the part after healing, then you should apply the ointments that relax and soften the parts together with the use of the splint we have described for a long time.
And in this case you may use a sheet of lead, but if the swelling appears at the end of the backbone it should be thought of as a fracture and not treated with this remedy lest death ensue.
Chapter Thirty-one
On the treatment of dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and knee joint are subject only to dislocation, and not to slight dislocations or subluxations. The hip joint is dislocated in four directions:
The hip joint dislocates inwardly, outwardly, anteriorly and posteriorly. The most common is inward dislocation, the least common is anterior or posterior dislocation. A sign of inward dislocation is that when you compare the healthy leg with the affected one, the healthy one appears longer.
The knee is protuberant beyond the healthy state, and the patient cannot flex his leg at the knee. The area behind the knee is warm and humid, clearly indicating that the femoral head has reached there.
The symptom of dislocation of the lower jaw outwards is opposite to these symptoms, and the symptom of dislocation forwards is that he extends his leg fully except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee. Even if he tries to walk, he cannot do so towards the front.
He retains urine and his buttocks are tender. When walking, he puts most of his weight on his heels. The symptom of dislocation backwards is that he cannot extend the knee or bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is also shorter than the other one.
The hip is relaxed, and the femoral head is at the location of the groin. As for the reduction of this dislocation, it is not possible if the dislocation is old and has not been reduced by its owner who did not attempt to do so, as there will be no treatment in that case.
It is not appropriate to reduce it, but if it is recent and of any type from the four dislocations, then proceed to move the joint by twisting, extending it inwards and outwards, and moving it right and left, perhaps this will be successful.
It returned without the need for anything else. But if it did not return in this way, then proceed to use a strong servant aid to extend his leg from below, either by hand or with a splint tied above the knee, and another servant should extend it from above.
So that he enters his hands under the armpits, then pulls with a soft bandage on the hipbone and another servant holds the end of the bandage. The extension should be either from in front from the hip towards the shoulder blade or from behind towards the back.
They should all extend together with one pull until the patient's body is lifted off the ground and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to all four types [of dislocation]. If the dislocation returns as mentioned, otherwise each type requires a specific treatment that I will mention.
His special treatment, if the jaw is dislocated inwardly, is that the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage should be applied at the root of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the pubis.
He then stretches the bandage upwards from the side of the pubis to above the chest towards the collarbone, and another strong servant aids by embracing the thick part of the thigh bone of the affected leg and pulls it outwards with great force, so that it returns to its place.
If this method proves difficult or impossible, then both of the patient's legs should be bound together tightly with a soft bandage at the ankles and knees.
Then, after every two of his own fingers (of the doctor), and with the affected leg extended more than the other by two fingerbreadths, the patient is suspended from a wooden beam in the house, leaving about two cubits between him and the ground.
Then, order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thighbone and another boy to pull the patient. Push the latter boy with force towards the former one holding the thighbone, so that the joint returns quickly to its place.
The patient should lie down on the couch as previously described for the case of hip dislocation. He should have a bandage tied around his affected leg and chest, then two beams are placed one at his feet and another at his head. An additional beam is placed in the middle of the couch, securely fastened.
Cover the beam with a wet cloth to prevent injury to the patient. Place the beam between his thighs to prevent him from slipping downwards during traction. Each servant pulls on his side while the doctor pushes the hip back into place with his hands.
If not, place the board on him and press as mentioned in the case of hip dislocation. However, the patient should lie on his sound side. If the dislocation is anterior, extend the affected leg while lying in this position on the couch.
The physician places his right hand on the patient's thigh, then presses it with his left hand. However, he should extend the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backwards, he should not extend the limb downwards.
He is high off the ground, rather he should be placed on a solid object. It is also necessary that his hips are turned outwards according to what we mentioned about the position on the bench while facing down with the bindings tightened.
As mentioned earlier, the compression should also be used with the slab on the place where the joint has dislocated. When the hip joint returns to its proper position, which will be evident by the extension of the patient's leg.
When they are level and the patient can easily bend and straighten his leg, know that the joint has returned to its proper position. Then, apply a bandage to the thighs and tighten it with a cloth strip so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient should remain still for three or four days, then remove the bandage and splint, and move his leg gently. If the dislocation has healed, he can walk. But if there is any relaxation,...
Then return and bind it again as before, leaving him for another three days. After that, remove the bandage and allow him to walk slowly until he regains his strength, God willing.
Chapter Two and Thirty.
In treating a dislocated knee.
The knee can dislocate in three directions: inwards, outwards, and downwards (backwards). It does not dislocate forwards. The sign of a dislocation is when the patient cannot bring his leg towards his thigh.
If the leg does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce it, have the patient sit down with his legs extended, if he can. Then sit behind him, hold his waist and tilt him back slightly. After that, you sit on his thighs.
Press your back against his face, place his leg between yours, then grip his thigh with both hands and interlock your fingers behind his thigh. Then squeeze his thighs tightly with both hands, while another servant extends the patient's leg until the knee returns to its original position.
The sign of its reduction is when the leg adheres to the thigh smoothly without resistance. Then, dress it and bind the thigh and leg together with a bandage for three or four days. Afterwards, release them and only allow walking little by little until they regain strength.
If reduction proves difficult despite the method described earlier, then apply strong traction using the bandages mentioned previously for hip dislocation treatment until reduction is achieved.
Chapter Thirty-three
In treating the broken heel.
The heel may dislocate easily or completely fracture, with the fracture either facing inward or outward. The sign of a fracture is that the heel appears swollen and prominent on the side where it has dislocated.
If it is only dislocated, it is easy to reduce by gently extending the limbs and aligning them until they return to their normal position. However, if it has completely fractured, do not let the patient sit. Instead, have a strong servant hold him from behind at his waist.
Then, grasp the patient's foot with your right hand from above and left hand from below at the ankle joint. Pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice, then extend it with your left hand while pushing the sole of the foot with your right hand gently towards the leg.
Repeat this process twice as described, then on the third attempt, push the sole of the foot towards the leg while pulling at the ankle joint. If it returns to its normal position in one or two attempts, you will see that the foot is straight. Otherwise, repeat the procedure until it returns.
If reduction is still not possible despite the described method, then lay the patient on his back on the ground. Drive a strong wooden peg into the ground between his thighs and secure it firmly with ropes to prevent movement. Cover the peg with cloth to avoid injury to the patient. Then have a servant hold the thigh steady.
Then another servant extends the man's leg either by hand or with a bandage tied around his neck, then each servant extends differently from his companion, and the rope is upright between the patient's thighs which he holds to prevent the body from slipping down during extension, then the doctor aligns the jaw with his hands.
Another servant holds the sound leg down, for the jaw returns quickly. When the jaw has returned and its correctness is apparent to you, then take up the splint and the bandage and tighten them with close bindings, and tie the foot down with a bandage. Take care to avoid the nerve that lies above the heel behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight upon it causing him pain, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage has loosened, then tighten it again on the third or fourth day, and the patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
But if he tries to walk before this period, there is fear that the jaw will dislocate and be ruined, and it will not accept treatment afterwards. If swelling appears, heat should not be applied in any other place of treatment or bandaging except where prescribed earlier.
Chapter Forty-three.
On treating dislocation of the toes.
It is necessary to align what has been dislocated without force by a slight extension, which is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the back of the foot, then the patient should place his foot on a level spot on the ground.
Or he should stand on a board as if walking, then you place your foot on the prominence of those joints and press it with your foot with force until it returns to its place and appears straight without any protuberance at the spot.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes in the whole foot and has two ends, then bind it tightly with a close bandage for three days. Then release it and let him rest from walking many days until it becomes strong and the return is secure, God willing.
Chapter Fifty-three
In types of fracture that occurs with wound or with breakage or with both together
When such things happen, if treatment and splinting are delayed, death often ensues.
Therefore, only someone skilled in the profession with extensive experience, compassionate, careful, and not hasty or reckless should undertake such treatment. Initially, medications to alleviate inflammation should be used.
O Allah, grant healing only if You will it, and do not allow harm with ease of illness. If there is some hope in the case, then restore health immediately before inflammation occurs.
If the bone returns to its original position, then apply remedies that alleviate inflammation and treat the wound with appropriate dried ointments. If the fracture is accompanied by displaced bone fragments, remove them. Follow what has been mentioned earlier in simple cases as detailed therein.
Strive diligently and keep yourself from straying onto paths of error, as I have warned you before. That is best for your reputation and safest for your patient, if Allah wills.
